This report is based on a project proposal prepared by the Project Prepara-
tion Team of the Empresa Brasileira de Pesquisa Agropecuaria (EMBRAPA) and
the findings of two Bank missions: pre-appraisal, July 1975, and appraisal,
July-August 1975. During pre-appraisal the research programs were reviewed
by six International Research Center Consultants: E. Alvarez-Luna,
A.C. McClung, J.C. Moomaw, J.G. Ryan, H.H. Stonaker, E.J. Wellhausen and
W.H. Wessling (technical program review team), plus D.B. Shoesmith (Consultant
Architect) and Messrs. R. Armour, E. Senner and N. Sharma (Bank). The apprai-
sal mission was comprised of R.P. Armour, G. Losson, F.M. Schorosch and
E.E. Senner (Bank) and A.H. Moseman, E.J. Wellhausen, J.M. Shelton and
D.B. Shoesmith (Consultants).

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
1.        The proposed five-year project is designed to strengthen the agri-
cultural research capability of the Brazilian Agricultural Research Corpo-
ration (EMBRAPA). It would focus on three important tropical agro-ecological
areas -- North, Northeast, and Central-West -- and give emphasis to accelerat-
ing production of nine commodities, or commodity groups, selected as being
of major importance to the Brazilian economy. The project would thus support
nine National Commodity Research Programs and three Area Farming Systems
Research Programs. The core research programs of the former would be imple-
mented through eight National Commodity Centers (NCC) and the latter through
three area Agricultural Research Centers (ARC), while adaptive research with
new technology, provided by the core research programs, would be carried out
at 14 state-level experiment stations (UEPAEs) 1/. These stations (UEPAEs)
would also play an important role in ensuring, through on-farm demonstration
plots, that the new technology evolved is appropriate and available to farmers
at all levels of production.
ii.       The nine crop/livestock commodities to be given priority would be:
rice, maize/sorghum 2/, cassava, cotton, beans, rubber, dairy cattle, beef
cattle and sheep/goats 2/. The project would provide for a broad program
of agro-biological research in each of the three designated agro-ecological
areas, giving priority to the smallholder dominated semi-arid Northeast; the
project would also provide support to the humid tropics of the North
(Amazonia), and the Campos Cerrados region, which is important because of
its rather unique soil/water/plant relationships. The basic goal of farming
systems research would be to study the scope and limitations of the natural
resources available, and to develop, from this knowledge, farming and indi-
vidual commodity production systems technology capable of supporting varied
and long-range utilization of these resources. Although the broad goals of
the proposed research programs are long term, the project is designed to
facilitate, as quickly as possible, transfer of existing knowledge, which is
currently available, both nationally and internationally. In this way, the
strengthening of the national agricultural research capacity is expected to
have a more immediate impact on production goals for the commodities in-
volved. The project would also finance fellowships, either in Brazil or
abroad, to meet EMBRAPA's entire training program need in the project area
for s!taff development; expansion of staff; and consultant research specialist
1/   Derived from the EMBRAPA designation of "Unidade de Execucao de
Pesquisa de Ambito Estadual."
2/   Considered as a single commodity group.  Taken separately they total
11 crop/livestock commodities.



- ii -
services. In addition, the project would fund civil works, purchase of
laboratory and farm equipment and incremental establishment costs, comprising
salaries, materials and supplies for operation of the 25 experiment stations.
Total project costs are estimated at US$189.4 million (Cr$ 1,682.4 million),
with an approximate foreign exchange component of US$40.0 million, or 21%.
The Bank loan would be for US$40 million and the Government would supply the
balance of required funding. EMBRAPA would be responsible for executing the
project and would establish a Technical Support Unit to handle civil works
and procurement.
iii.      Due to the relatively small size and dispersed nature of the civil
works components, it is not expected that foreign, or even major national
firms, would compete for contracts; therefore, Government bidding procedures
would be used. However, foreign bidders would not be excluded in the invita-
tion to bid. Work by force account would be permitted in remote areas. Pro-
curement of laboratory equipment, vehicles and other specialized items would
follow standard Government procedures, especially taking into account the
local availability of spare parts and maintenance capacity, in addition to
bid prices.  EMBRAPA would contract with one or more scholarship and advisory
services in the countries selected for graduate training programs to implement
that portion of the fellowship program which is foreign based. EMBRAPA would
also contract the services of a specialized agency, or agencies, to assist
in the recruitment and administration of the complex technical assistance
component.
iv.       Possible areas of more than usual risk lie in (a) the complexity of
the project extending over a very large geographic area, approximately 80%
of Brazilian territory, and the administrative difficulties inherent in the
effective administration of such a program; (b) the lack of adequately trained
personnel during the early years of project execution, when the training pro-
gram would exert a depleting, rather than supplementary, influence on the pool
of professional staff available; and (c) the difficulty of organizing the
speedy and effective application of research results at the farm level on a
large scale. Nevertheless, agricultural research is a vital element in the
Government's efforts to achieve an announced goal of a 6.2% annual increase
in agricultural production during the 1975-79 period and to maintain adequate
growth thereafter. Since this goal can be achieved only through substantial
production increases per unit area and the rational utilization of new lands
being brought under cultivation, a strong program of agro-biological research
is fully justified. Actual quantification of project benefits is complex and
of questionable value in view .of the uncertainties inherent in research, but
even negative results add to the knowledge of a specific commodity or area.
However, experience has demonstrated that agricultural research, properly
executed and utilized, can provide satisfactory rates of return and that an
appropriate level of national capability is necessary for a country to
benefit from imported technology. In addition, the project would strengthen
the national economy as a whole by providing the means for import substitu-
tion and expanding export possibilities for a limited but important range of
essential food and fiber commodities. By placing major emphasis on one of the
three agro-ecological areas of influence -- the Northeast -- and upon basic



- iii -
commodities most commonly produced by small farmers, it is expected that the
project would ultimately have a major impact on about 1.6 million families
on farms of less than 10 ha in addition to some 400,000 families on farms in
the 11 to 50 ha range. Most of these families have incomes below the relative
poverty level.
v.        Since 1967 the Bank has made seven loans, totalling US$185.4 mil-
lion, for agricultural projects in Brazil. These projects included two for
livestock development, one for grain storage, one for agro-industries, and
three for agricultural development in the Northeast - Alto Turi Land Settle-
ment, Lower Sao Francisco Polders and Rio Grande do Norte Rural Development.
The proposed project would effectively complement many of these earlier
projects as well as provide crucial support to the various rural development
projects now under preparation (and which the Government also hopes to sub-
mit to the Bank for financing) by providing the new technology necessary to
stimulate increased agricultural production.
vi.       Subject to appropriate assurances, the project is suitable for
a Bank loan of US$40.0 million for a term of 20 years, including five years'
grace. The borrower would be the Government of the Federative Republic of
Brazil.






BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      In 1972, the Brazilian Government requested Bank technical assist-
ance for the then newly formed Brazilian Agricultural Research Corporation
(EMBRAPA) and a project was tentatively identified in mid-1973. Several
Bank preparation missions visited the project area during 1974, a pre-
appraisal mission visited Brazil in May 1975, and the project was appraised
during July-August 1975. During the preparation phase the scope of the
project was broadened to cover not only technical assistance but also the
training of scientific staff, the development of research infrastructure,
and all the requirements (except land) for research programs on nine basic
crops. The geographical scope of the project, was, however, reduced to
focus primarily on the Northeast region of the country, but with some sup-
port for research activities in the Amazonia (North) and Campos Cerrados
(Central-West) regions on the assumption that the latter regions might
receive further attention during a later phase. Additionally, in view of
the Government's efforts to promote rural development and small-farmer
assistance programs in Northeast Brazil, the project was tailored to support
such specific programs.
1.02      The preparation missions assisted EMBRAPA in formulating an overall
national research policy that would depart from a disciplinary-oriented,
regional research station approach, and follow one that was commodity-
oriented and multi-disciplinary, but concentrating on no more than three
basic agricultural commodities at each station. With this in mind, EMBRAPA
developed a project request based on the commodity research program approach
and limited its research activity to 16 basic crops, nationally, with only
nine of these selected for development in the project area. The relatively
few crops to be investigated were chosen for their importance, either as a
basic food crop or as a valuable export or import substitution crop. The
original proposals were very ambitious, but further discussion resulted in
the total number of research stations to be supported under the project being
reduced from 41 to 25. Also, since existing research efforts in the North-
east have so far largely failed to improve agricultural production in the
area, particularly that of the small farmer, the need for including farming
system techniques in the research program was recognized by EMBRAPA. This
approach was further refined through assistance from the International Re-
search Centers such as the International Center for Tropical Agricultural
(CIAT), the International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-arid Tropics
(ICRISAT), the International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA), the
International Rice Research Institute (IRRI) and the International Maize and
Wheat Improvement Center (CIMMYT).
1.03      The Bank has made seven loans, totalling US$185.4 million, in the
agriculture sector in Brazil: two, amounting to US$66 million were for live-
stock development; one for US$54 million for agro-industries; one for US$23.7



million for grain storage; one for US$6.7 million for settlement; one for
US$23 million for the Lower Sao Francisco Polders Project; and one for
US$12 million for the Rio Grande do Norte Rural Development Project. Several
additional agricultural projects are under active preparation, including a
second agro-industry project; rural development projects in the States of
Minas Gerais, Paraiba and Ceara; and a land settlement project in the west-
ern state of Mato Grosso. Also, a nutrition project proposed by the Govern-
ment for Bank financing would have important rural components, and a recently
approved secondary and feeder road project would be of direct benefits to
farmers in improving access to markets and opening new agricultural areas.
Parallel to recent Bank lending activities in agriculture, a special report
entitled "Rural Development Issues and Objectives in Northeast Brazil," No.
665a-BR, dated June 23, 1975, was prepared.
II. BACKGROUND
A. The Agricultural Sector
2.01      Brazil's economy has traditionally maintained a strong agricultur-
al base and the relative importance of agriculture has shown only a slow
decline over the past decade, from 22.5% of GNP contributions in 1963 to
17.4% in 1973.  It still remains a very important producer of food and
fiber crops, however, and accounts for 62% of all exported goods. In recent
years, output has grown at an average rate of 6.7%, as compared to average
annual increments of over 10% for the economy as a whole. There is still
enormous potential for further expansion, however, both from increases in the
area brought under cultivation, and also from increases per unit area.
2.02      Agriculturally, Brazil can be divided into five major agro-ecologi-
cal regions (see map). Data 1/ for the past decade (1960-1971) show the
following shifts in relative importance of these five areas:
Percentage of
Total Agricultural      Total
Production         Population          Area in

Region                                     (thousands)       (thousands)
1.  Southeast           44.1      37.5        41,050             918.8
2.  South                30.5     36.0        16,900             562.1
3.  Northeast            18.0     18.5        28,950           1,542.2
4.  Central-West          6.0      6.5         5,300           1,879.4
5.  North                 1.4      1.5         3,700           3,554.0
100.0    100.0        95,900           8,456.5
1/   Source:  EMBRAPA:  Program to Strengthen Agricultural Research,
Vol. II, Page 19, 1975.



- 3 -
These figures clearly indicate the current importance of the Southeast and
South, but they also hint at the potential of the remainder of the country,
in effect, the project area, and the very large numbers of people that would
benefit were agricultural production to be substantially increased.
2.03      Until recently the strategy for development has been based on con-
solidating production in the South and Southeast, but more recently con-
centrated efforts have been made by the Government to significantly increase
production in the remainder of the country. It has also been customary to
expand production by increasing the area under cultivation, rather than by
increasing yields or improving levels of efficiency generally, an expansionist
policy that has had much to recommend it in many ways but one that cannot be
sustained indefinitely. It is now more fully appreciated that in order to
meet the challenge implicit in the National Development Plan (PND II - 1975-79)
and to keep pace with projected growth rates in population and development of
the industrial sector, agriculture must look increasingly towards the use of
additional capital and technical inputs, as opposed to land, as a means of
sustaining yearly increments in production. Improved and more effective agri-
cultural research has, therefore, an absolutely essential role in providing
the required technology to execute such a program, particularly in the North,
Northeast, and Central-West.
B. Importance of the Selected Commodites in the
Agricultural Economy
2.04      Coffee, soybeans and sugar are Brazil's most important export crops,
generating 40% of the total export earnings, but foodcrops are far more im-
portant in terms of employment generated and areas under cultivation. Also,
about 60% of agricultural output is consumed in the domestic market. Beans,
cassava and rice are the staple diet of the poor in Brazil, and an increase
in production of at least 3% per annum is needed to meet the requirements of a
population growing at a rate of 2.9% per year. Actual production increases
in the period from 1967-72 were, however, only 1.4% for cassava, 1.6% for
beans and 2.5% for rice. Since there were no increases in yields per hectare,
even these small production gains relied heavily on new land being brought
under cultivation. Maize is also an important staple food in some areas,
but, again, increased production has barely kept pace with demand and has
also been achieved largely as a result of an expansion of the cultivated
area.
2.05      Beef is an important commodity in both the domestic and export
markets, and annual increases of at least 3.5% will be required to meet
demand. Meat production from sheep and goats is also of importance, mainly
in the domestic economy, and demand is not likely to grow more than 1 to 2%
annually. However, the export demand for sheep and goat skins, which repre-
sents 30% of the animal's value at slaughter, is expected to have a strong
influence on production in areas where harsh conditions severely limit other



- 4 -
types of agricultural enterprise.  Dairy cattle have little relative import-
ance in the project area at present, accounting for only 21% of national
milk production. However, population shifts are expected to increase demand
in the project area by 5.5% over the next seven-year period.
2.06      Cotton, of major importance in the South and Southeast, is also
an important crop in the Northeast. The project area at present produces
59% of Brazilian cotton, and cotton demand is expected to increase by 6%
annually during the next decade. The fact that cotton production in the
Northeast, particularly its harvesting, is not mechanized in the project
area means that there is a significant demand for local labor.  Natural
rubber, which is produced only in the project area, is harvested mainly
(90%) from native trees. Cultivated rubber production will require con-
siderable incentives and new technology to retain even its current 10% hold.
Per capita consumption of rubber products increased by 130% during the past
decade and is expected to maintain numbers of the rural population, parti-
cularly in the North (Amazonia). The relative importance of all commodities
included in the project, by geographic area, is shown in Annex 1, Table 4.
C. Present Status of Agricultural Research
2.07      The structure of agricultural research is currently in transition,
from a verv loosely admninistered group of about 230 research units operated
by many different federal, state and private agencies to a more centralized
arrangement where one federal authority is in charge of all agricultural
research programs. The former approach suffered severely from the lack
of any planning, coordination and guidance at the federal level, and, even
at regional and state levels, the benefits of research were largely unused
due to poor farmer support services. Complementary inputs, such as exten-
sion, credit and marketing, were often either entirely lacking or not avail-
able to a significant proportion of producers. Also, research efforts often
failed to take into account the need for additional inputs such as credit.
It was to remedy this situation that a strong unified research corporation,
Empresa Brasileira de Pesquisa Agropecuaria - EMBRAPA, was created by law
(No. 5,851) in December 1972. This newly established corporation, linked to
the Ministry of Agriculture, was charged with the full national responsibility
for the coordination and promotion of agricultural research throughout Brazil.
2.08      EMBRAPA intends to exercise its new mandate through two main
avenues of activity: (a) direct action through executive units of EMBRAPA
at the national and state levels; and (b) coordination of the planning,
execution and evaluation of research programs which are being carried out
by state research stations as part of federally integrated development pro-
grams. EMBRAPA intends to further concentrate its initial efforts, within
the broad framework of its mandate, on only 16 agricultural commodity groups,
nine of which are included in the proposed project. The major problems facing



-5-
EMBRAPA were first to provide centralized management and guidance to a pre-
viously severely fractionated organization, while, at the same time, to avoid
disruption of worthy ongoing research projects, and to maintain the enthusiasm
of the very limited cadre of experienced research personnel. Most of these
early organizational problems have now been bvercome, but the need to build up
adequate numbers of qualified professional staff, currently only some 10% of
overall requirements, is still a formidable task, and one which demands a
strong infusion of technical assistance programs at all operational levels if
EMBRAPA's goals are to be realized on a national scale.
2.09      The Brazilian Agricultural Research Corporation (EMBRAPA), began
operations on April 26, 1973. It has taken over the functions of the previous
National Department of Agricultural Research (DNPEA) and is responsible for
the coordination and promotion of agricultural research throughout Brazil. It
will establish coordinated, multi-disciplinary, national research programs in-
volving the EMBRAPA research centers, cooperating universities, state and pri-
vate organizations. The nationwide cooperative system is designed to concen-
trate the efforts of the cooperating institutions on problems of national,
regional and state concern. Emphasis will be placed on development of new
technologies to make the nation's agriculture more efficient and productive.
2.10      EMBRAPA's total assets amounted to about Cr$ 1.4 billion (US$185
million) as of December 31, 1974. It is almost totally dependent upon Govern-
ment budget allocations, or Government secured borrowing, to finance its opera-
-a    i .s  i       a n_'- j'>-              arc subjact t  Go ovvernmeant approval
as part of the Ministry of Agriculture budgets. In 1974, EMBRAPA's total ex-
penditures were Cr$ 181.9 million in 1975 prices, or about US$21 million.
Roughly 72% of EMBRAPA's funding in 1974 came directly from the Federal Treasury;
approximately another 18% represented transfers from special contracts between
EMBRAPA and other Brazilian Government entities; about 4% came from a USAID
loan; and 6% came from miscellaneous sources. EMBRAPA's approved 1975 budget
amounted to Cr$ 495.5 million (or roughly US$58 million), about triple the
1974 budget, and represented a very substantial increase in EMBRAPA invest-
ments. The programmed expenditures of EMBRAPA in 1975 represented about
12.5% of the total programmed expenditures of the Ministry of Agriculture.
D. Present Status of Agricultural Education
2.11      In 1975, there were 40 institutions of higher learning offering
57 programs in the agricultural sciences. Most of the schools and programs
have been started in the last 10 to 15 years; few are older than 25 years.
Most animal husbandry, forestry and fisheries study programs are of recent
origin since, traditionally, only agronomy and veterinary sciences were
taught at the older institutions. Total enrollment in the agricultural
sciences in 1973 was 15,090 students, or only 16 per 100,000 inhabitants



- 6 -
and an average of 3.06 students per 1,000 farm enterprises. The majority of
courses offered are at the undergraduate level only, and currently there are
500 places per year for graduate level studies in 29 study programs. Opportu-
nities for graduate education are still concentrated in the South and South-
east, but recently there has been greater interest in the Northeast and North.
Almost 80% of all graduates are employed by federal and state agencies; the
private sector has been slow to engage graduate agriculturalists. Training
capability to meet national requirements is believed to be adequate at the
undergraduate level, but there is still a strong reliance on training abroad
for advanced degrees for agricultural research staff working in specialized
fields of studies (see Annex 2 for details).
E. Present Status of Agricultural Extension
2.12      Every state in Brazil has its own extension service (ACARs).  Qua-
lity and effectiveness vary from state to state, but most state services
have been severely hampered by lack of continuity in policy-making, staff,
and financing and poor support services. Each service has traditionally
operated fairly independently, financing itself and determining its own
policies as circumstances demanded, but all were loosely affiliated to a
national Association of Credit and Rural Technical Assistance Services
(ABCAR), which came under the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Agriculture.
The link with credit has been significant and, in most instances, indivi-
dual services have deployed most of their field staff on supervising credit
on behalf of the banking system, to a considerable extent because such work
has been a source of much needed financing. Genuine extension work has
suffered as a result, as did attention to the small-scale farmer.
2.13      The Government has recognized the need to improve matters, par-
ticularly in regard to better and more assured financing, and in mid-1974
created the Brazilian Corporation for Technical Assistance and Rural Exten-
sion (EMBRATER) to replace ABCAR. EMBRATER, an autononmous entity, but also
under the control of the Minister of Agriculture, is presently in Lhe pro-
cess of organizing itself. Its primary functions will be to collaborate,
with other agencies of the Ministry of Agriculture, in formulating and im-
plementing policies pertaining to technical assistance and rural exten-
sion and to promote, stimulate and coordinate technical assistance pro-
grams. The staff of the former ABCAR system - 4,724 technicians and 3,594
administrative personnel - are being absorbed by EMBRATER and its state
subsidiaries. EMBRATER plans to double the number of technical staff with-
in the next four years. At the federal level, EMBRATER will be responsible
for all federal budgetary allocations for technical assistance and exten-
sion. Projects in the individual states will be integrated into state
extension programs and these, in turn, will serve as a basis for the formula-
tion of a national extension program. Implementation of extension projects
will be in the hands of the former ACARs, now to be converted into "Empresas"
or state companies (EMATERs). These State Technical Assistance and Rural



-7-
Extension Companies will be sufficiently autonomous to allow for flexibility
and efficient management of day-to-day operations. However, the Federal
Corporation (EDIBRATER) will (a) participate in the share capital and in the
selection of board members, (b) act as the channel for all federal funding
for extension through contractual arrangements for specific programs related
to development projects in the individual states, (c) participate in the
planning of such projects, and (d) assign headquarters (EMBRATER) staff to
the state affiliates for consultation and training purposes.
2.14      The major innovation in the new extension service is the strengthen-
ing of the federal technical and financial support for the activities of the
state affiliates, and the reorientation of the state system towards a broader
range of farmers. The Brasilia headquarters group will provide assistance
at the administrative, planning and operational levels to the state agencies,
which will, henceforth, receive federal funds on the basis of projects pre-
pared in accordance with selection criteria now being set up by EMBRATER.
These criteria will reflect priorities in extension work for geographic areas,
selected commodities, and farmer target groups. It is also anticipated that
EMBRATER and EMBRAPA each will work extremely closely in disseminating re-
search results to farmers. The reorganization is a significant shift in
policy and illustrates the Government's determination to improve support
services to farmers, particularly the smaller farmers. It is interesting to
note, for instance, that one of EIBRATER's first actions on being created was
to organize a seminar to inquire as to how small farmers needs for extension
and technical support services could best be met. The reorganization of the
extension service in effect attempts to focus extension work on those activi-
ties that will contribute to the achievement of national agricultural develop-
ment objectives. Success will depend to a considerable extent on EIIBRATER's
ability to encourage state affiliates (EMATERs) to adopt the new extension
philosophy through specific and clearly defined projects and to develop the
intra- and inter-institutional arrangements so vital to an effective extension
service. It is hoped that Bank support for rural development projects will
help to assure the realization of these objectives (see Annex 2 for details).
F. Production and Distribution of Improved Seed
2.15      Currently, improved seed of several crops is produced in Brazil,
both by private firms and by public or semi-public agencies. The Brazilian
Government has been acutely aware of the need to produce and distribute
improved seeds that would ensure maximum returns from the application of new
technology to agricultural production. During the years 1963-69, a National
Seed Improvement Plan (PLANASEM1) was put into operation under the auspices
of the Ministry of Agriculture. The plan was designed to help develop seed
production capacity in two distinct regions. For one of the regions, which
combined the South and Southeast, a seed production program with investments
of some US$25 million during 1973-75 was partly financed by the Inter-American
Development Bank (IDB). Seed improvement programs for the second project
region of the North-Northeast and Central-West are less advanced. However,



- 8 -
Ministry of Agriculture funding has now been allocated to seed improvement
programs for these areas, such programs to be expanded over the five-year
period, 1975-79, of the Second National Development Plan (PND II). The
basic goal of all these programs is to make each region largely self-
sufficient in the production and distribution of improved seed.
2.16      EMBRAPA, through its Department of Technology and Science (DTS),
is well aware of the importance of improved seed as an essential link in
the transfer of improved technology to the farmer. EMBRAPA intends to play
an active role in this process by helping set up regional and semi-autonomous
companies which will take the small quantities of foundation seed stock pro-
duced at the experiment stations and multiply them up to the level of basic
seed stocks, which will then be sold to interested commercial enterprises
which, in turn, will produce the certified seed for farmers. EMBRAPA expects
to act only as a catalyst in this process and will release the companies to
full private enterprise control as soon as possible.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Brief Description
3.01      The purpose of the project would be to assist EMBRAPA to strengthen
its capability for research programs on nine 1/ commodity groups. The project
would be largely, but not entirely, concentrated in three agro-ecological
areas, namely: (a) the densely populated, but agriculturally poor, Northeast,
where periodic droughts create acute social and economic problems; (b) the
North (Amazonia), where there is limited knowledge of the agricultural poten-
tial; and (c) the Central-West, or Campos Cerrados, an area fringing on
Amazonia, which is also being rapidly opened to human settlement.
3.02      Two types of research programs would be supported under the project--
one type would deal with commodities and one type with farming systems. Com-
modity research programs would be strongly oriented toward increased production
while the principal goal of farming systems research would be to increase agri-
cultural production through more efficient overall use of available resources.
The project would include a limited amount of land purchase and the construc-
tion and development of research laboratories and field facilities at three
Agricultural Research Centers (ARCs), eight National Commodity Centers (NCCs)
and 14 State Experiment Stations (UEPAEs). These facilities represent 54% of
1/   These are grouped as follows:  beans, cassava, cotton, maize/sorghum, rice,
rubber, beef cattle, dairy cattle, sheep/goats. Taken separately these
total 11 crops or livestock commodities.



- 9 -
the 45 stations now operating under EMBRAPA's jurisdiction. Also to be fi-
nanced under the project would be laboratory and farm equipment, technical
research services, and incremental establishment costs over a five-year
period. EMBRAPA would be the executing agency and would establish a Technical
Support Unit (TSU) to plan and supervise the civil works program and to
monitor progress of the project.
B. Detailed Features
The National Commodity Research Programs (Annexes 3 to 11)
3.03      The project would finance nine commodity-oriented research programs 1/
in a national network. NCCs for seven of the commodities chosen, i.e., cotton,
rubber, rice, beans, cassava, beef cattle, and sheep/goats, would be within the
project area. The remaining two Centers, for dairy cattle and maize/sorghum,
would be outside the project area, but would play an important role through
sub-station (satellite) activity in the three agro-ecological areas of the
project's zone of influence. The commodity programs would be strongly oriented
towards increased production, consistent with national policy and development
programs. Short-term goals would be the elimination of constraints to pro-
duction at the farm level by adaptation and adoption of existing technology.
Long-range goals would be the development of new technology through research
on crop/livestock improvement, soils, disease and pest control, cultivation
techniques, farming systems, harvesting, food processing, marketing and pro-
duction economics. The headquarters stations for core research, and the
cooperating UEPAEs for each commodity, have been carefully sited to reflect
areas of major production and climatic and soil variations. Cooperating
experiment stations and institutions, outside the project area, have also
been identified as part of the national research program in each case. The
technical staff of these programs would maintain close linkage with the
farmers through the state and regional offices of EMBRATER.
Research Programs for Farming Systems (Annexes 12 to 14)
3.04      Programs have been designed for farming systems research in the
three agro-ecological areas covered by the project (see Map No. 11884).
Each program would have one headquarters center, which would coordinate
regional research through a network of NCCs and UEPAEs in its area of in-
fluence. The principal goal of these programs would be to increase agri-
cultural production through more efficient overall use of available resources
and would be based initially on the large amount of basic information already
gathered. As a short-term goal, farming systems research would evaluate
1/   Although there are nine commodity research programs, there are only
eight National Commodity Centers (NCCs) because both rice and bean
research will be carried out at the NCC station at Goiania.



- 10 -
this knowledge and apply it to current systems of production. The long-
range objectives would be in-depth studies of the natural resource base
to determine the most effective ways to increase agricultural production
through carefully planned and rational use of each resource, with particular
emphasis on plant/soil/water relationships. The program would also take into
consideration Federal and State development plans and programs related to
the agriculture of the region. Dissemination of research results, and new
technology developed under these programs, would be through the cooperating
NCC and UEPAE stations. The latter would adapt the new techniques to comply
with local production requirements and demonstrate these techniques to the
farmers.
Capital Costs
3.05      The project would finance a civil works program to expand and im-
prove existing experiment station buildings at one ARC site, four NCC sites
and eight UEPAE sites and the partial construction of one ARC site, one NCC
site and six UEPAE sites (Annex 15). Where minor upgrading and repairs to
existing buildings are required, these would be carried out by EMBRAPA simul-
taneously with project development at these sites. Assurances to this effect
were obtained from EMBRAPA at negotiations. Other items also to be financed
would be laboratory and farm equipment for 12 research programs at 25 experi-
ment stations; the development of research farms (land preparation and level-
ing and installation of irrigation systems as necessary) at 21 of the sites;
vehicles for all 25 stations; and land purchase, as may be required, at eight
of the experiment stations.
Technical Services
3.06      Technical services financed under the project would include the
provision of substantial technical assistance to EIBRAPA in the management
and organization of its research programs and a national and international
fellowships program that would assist EMBRAPA to improve its staff. A
Research Program Advisor would be recruited internationally to work closely
with the Director in charge of EMBRAPA's Department of Technology and Science
for a period of five years. Senior scientists would also be recruited in-
ternationally to provide program leadership, including (a) commodity con-
sultants for a term of three years for the three ARCs for a total of
16.5 man-years of long-term consulting services; (b) research program con-
sultants for the commodity and farming systems research programs, for a
total of 43 man-years of long-term consultancy services; and (c) selected
specialist consultants for 41 man-years of short-term consultancy work for
periods of less than one year. These would include farming system and farm
mechanization specialists, plant physiologists and other selected experts
for specific high priority research programs. Under the fellowship program,
123 EMBRAPA staff would be trained in specific commodity-oriented programs
to the Ph.D. level and 663 to the M.Sc. level. Short course training would
also be provided for an additional 180 staff members in subjects specifically
related to the designated commodity research programs in the project area.
Through such short courses and exchange visits to several of the International



- 11 -
Research Centers, there could be a valuable interchange of research results
and techniques in the following areas: research on beef cattle, beans,
cassava and farming systems at CIAT, on farming systems at ICRISAT and IITA,
on maize and sorghum at CIMMYT, and on rice at IRRI.
Establishment Items
3.07      The project would also finance (a) salaries for incremental techni-
cal staff, administrative staff and laborers in accordance with the estimated
staff development pattern over the five-year period; and (b) operating mate-
rials such as fuel, laboratory supplies, fertilizers, insecticides, pesticides
and general expenses such as utilities, communications and travel.

may be summarized as follows:

C. Cost Estimates
3.09      Total project cost, including contingencies, is estimated at
US$189.4 million, of which 21% or about US$40 million, would be the foreign
exchange component. Cost estimates for capital inputs are based on informa-
tion obtained in Brazil and comparative data from international research cen-
ters. The costs for buildings, laboratory equipment and furniture were
estimated on the basis of recent cost levels in Brazil, as provided by
architects of the Ministry of Agriculture and the private sector. Estimates
lor farm development, field equipment, vehicles, library documentation and
information services reflect similar data from existing research centers
operating in Brazil. Although architectural design and detailed engineering
work is not yet completed for some of the stations, the project includes all
of the major construction works which will be necessary to complement other
project components for which implementation could begin without delay. The
civil works cost estimates have been based on recent experience in Brazil for
comparable construction, taking into account expected space requirements of
the project research stations and the wide regional variations in construc-
tion costs. Costs of technical services are based on international experience.
Some flexibility has been allowed in the case of consultant research special-
ists to be engaged in the research programs and for fellowships, the number
assigned to each of the 12 research programs being indicative only. Annual
costs per consultant were estimated to be US$75,000, of which 40% went for
salary; 30 for insurance, retirement, family educational allowance and other
benefits; and 30% for duty station allowances, including rent, travel expenses
and repatriation. These estimates were based on (a) comparative data from
three previous Bank loans for similar research projects, (b) current informa-
tion from eight International Research Centers, and (c) the limited availab-
ility of internationally recruited scientists with the necessary qualifications
and competence to meet the specifications provided in the appropriate terms of
reference in each case. Salary calculations are based on an estimated staff
development pattern for the five-year period, using EMBRAPA's salary scales.
From data obtained from various EMBRAPA research stations, and from the
experience of international agricultural research centers, it is estimated
that other operating expenses would be 30% of salaries during the first two
years and 40% in years 3 to 5 (see Annex 16). In all cases, the existing
salary and operating costs have been appraised and subtracted from the total
establishment cost, so that only the incremental cost is shown as part of
project cost.
3.10      In view of uncertainties bearing on building requirements, a
physical contingency of 20% was taken to cover such works. A physical con-
tingency of 5% was applied for all other components. The total physical con-
tingency amounts to 6.5% of the base project cost and the price contingency
to 24.5% of the base cost. Price contingencies are based on projected future
international price increases and are calculated in US dollars. Project cost
estimates, details of which are given in Annex 16, are summarized below:

D. Financing
3.11      The Bank would finance US$40 million, or 21% of total project
cost, which is equivalent to the estimated foreign exchange cost. The Govern-
ment would finance the remaining 79%. Assurances were obtained at negotia-
tions that the Government would supply these funds in a timely manner. The
borrower would be the Federative Republic of Brazil, which would also carry
the exchange risk, and make available the proceeds of the loan to EMBRAPA on
the same terms and conditions as such proceeds are lent by the Bank to the
Borrower. The Bank loan would be for 20 years, including five years of grace,
and at the prevailing interest rate.
E. Procurement
3.12      Due to the dispersed nature of the project civil works and the
relatively small size of the construction contracts, it is doubtful whether
foreign contractors, or even major national contractors, would be interested
in building the proposed facilities. However, Government procurement pro-
cedures would be followed with contracts advertised in the local and national
press. Foreign bidders would not be excluded. Work by force account would
be permitted in remote areas where it is difficult to obtain contractors by
competitive bidding. Laboratory and documentation equipment, which is highly
specialized, and for which after-sales service is of critical importance and
must be provided locally, would be purchased through prudent international
shopping. When a reasonable number of suppliers could be eligible for com-
petitive bidding tenders would be advertised locally, but not excluding
foreign firms. It is expected that approximately US$5.7 million in special-
ized laboratory equipment and library and documentation equipment and materials
would be imported. Furniture, farm equipment and vehicles, which would be
provided for the 25 widely scattered project stations over several years,
would also be purchased according to normal Government procurement procedures,
which are satisfactory.
F. Disbursement
3.13      The Bank loan would be disbursed against (a) 100% of the direct
foreign exchange expenditures for fellowships (about US$12.0 million), con-
sultants (about US$9.9 million), laboratory equipment and furniture (about
US$3.1 million) and library and documentation facilities (about US$2.6 mil-
lion); 1/ and (b) 30% of all other project expenditures, excluding those for
land purchase and establishment cost (about US$12.4 million). Prior to sub-
mitting disbursement requests for civil works at research stations, EMBRAPA
1/   The figures given in this paragraph relate to costs of project com-
ponents, inclusive of physical and price contingencies. Therefore,
they are not directly comparable with the costs given in the cost
estimate table.



- 16 -
would complete the purchase and/or arrange for the legal rights for use of
the land at the respective stations (Annex 15). Assurances to this effect
were given by EMBRAPA at negotiations. An implementation schedule for civil
works is provided in Chart 1, Annex 15.
G. Accounts and Auditing
3.14      The accounting system of EMBRAPA is satisfactory.  Accounts are
audited by Price, Waterhouse, Peat and Company. During negotiation, assur-
ances were given by EMBRAPA that adequate project accounts would be main-
tained, in accordance with appropriate accounting practices, to show the
financial position of the project, and that, within four months of the end
of each fiscal year, the Bank would be provided with copies of the audit
report made by auditors acceptable to the Bank.
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Administration
Project Responsibility
4.01      The Brazilian Agricultural Research Corporation (EMBRAPA) would be
the executing agent for the project. One of its three Directors, the Director
of 'the Department of Technology and Science (DTS), would have prime responsibi-
lity for the execution of the research project. He would be assisted by a
team of 12 senior scientists. This team will consist of two units. The
first, a Technical Unit, will be composed of eight specialists in such discip-
lines as genetics, entomology, plant pathology, climatology, veterinary
science, agronomy, agricultural economics, and agricultural engineering. The
second four-man unit will act as a management team to provide the technical
director with administrative liaison between the experiment stations and
EMBRAPA headquarters.
Civil Works Technical Support Unit
4.02      A Technical Support Unit (TSU) has been established by EMBRAPA to
plan and supervise the project's civil works program. Staff will gradually be
built up to include about five architedts and engineers; there will be adequate
support staff (about seven) for administrative and clerical duties. The TSU
will act as owner's representative in dealing with the executive architects
who will be retained for preparing the development plan, architectural design
and contract documents and for providing site supervision. Other duties of
this unit would be to prepare detailed development plans for each station to
be financed under the project. EMBRAPA will then submit such plans to the
Bank for approval prior to calling for construction bids. Assurances to this



- 17 -
effect were given by EMBRAPA at negotiations. Details concerning the specific
duties of the TSU are given in Annex 15. Assurances were given at negotiations
that the TSU would be maintained and adequately staffed during the project
implementation period.
National Commodity Centers (NCC)
4.03      The nat,onal commodity research programs would be conducted through
a network of research centers, including a national headquarters center,
satellite or sub-centers, and cooperating institutions. The composition of
the network would vary for different commodities.  Core integrated, multi-
disciplinary research on the factors or problems that limit production of
the commodity would be undertaken primarily by the team of scientists
located at a headquarters research station or center. The Director of each
regional station would have overall administrative responsibility for the
operation of the station, while the Associate Director for Research would be
responsible for overall management and supervision of the research activities
and would also serve as the national program leader/coordinator. The latter
would have principal responsibility and authority for planning, execution,
coordination and evaluation of the national program and would be the principal
liaison officer with the technical directorate staff of EMBRAPA, with parti-
cipating research team members, and with collaborating institutions.
Agricultural Research Centers (ARC)
4.04      The research on improvement of farming systems for the three main
agro-ecological regions would be conducted primarily at ARCs for (a) the
semi-arid tropics, at Petrolina/Juazeiro, in the states of Pernambuco and
Bahia; (b) the humid tropics, at Belem in Para State; and (c) the Central-
West, or Campos Cerrados region, at Planaltina, in the Federal District.
Work would be done, however, in close cooperation with NCCs and with other
institutions with mutual interests. The Director of each regional station
would have overall administrative responsibility for the operation of the
station, while the Associate Director for Research would serve as the program
leader/coordinator for the respective area farming systems research program.
He would function in the same manner as the national commodity research pro-
gram leaders in taking responsibility for planning, execution, coordination
and evaluation and also for maintaining relations with EMBRAPA headquarters
and cooperating institutions.
State Research Units (UEPAEs)
4.05      UEPAEs would conduct research to improve farming systems in the
area or micro-regions in which they were located. The multi-disciplinary team
of researchers at the UEPAE station would collaborate with the NCCs and the
ARCs in adaptive research and testing where there is mutual interest in new
materials and practices. Research personnel would be located at these
stations, on assignment from national commodity research programs or from an
agricultural research center, to work on specific problems. The UEPAEs would
function as the bridge between the research workers developing new technology



- 18 -
and the extension workers responsible for its diffusion to farmers. The
UEPAE would include an extension specialist, a training officer, and, in
some instances, a seed production specialist. These officers would assist
in coordinating farm demonstrations, training programs and field days to
ensure effective transfer of research findings to farmers.
Program Planning, Execution, Coordination, Evaluation and Monitoring
4.06      Advisory Councils.  The National Committee for Agricultural Re-
search, Technical Assistance and Rural Extension (COMPATER) would provide
guidance to EMBRAPA on national priorities for agricultural development and
on general policy and procedural matters. Advisory councils would be estab-
lished for the national commodity or area farming systems research programs
to assist in identification of priority problems in the general planning of
research programs and in the dissemination of improved technology.
4.07      Execution and Coordination.  The execution of research would be
handled through maximum delegation of responsibility and authority to the
national program leader/coordinators and to the participating stations.
The national program leader/coordinators for the various research pro-
grams would be responsible for developing and integrating research efforts
of the multi-disciplinary teams. They also would serve as the principals
in facilitating cooperation between commodity programs, regional research
programs, state research units, and others collaborating in investigations of
mutual interest.
4.08      Evaluation and Monitoring of Research Programs.  A Program, Plan-
ning and Budgeting System (PPBS) would be established for each research
program to provide detailed information on the problem, objectives, research
plan, program leader, participating institutions and principal scientists,
terms of reference for the project, and funding. This system would serve
as the mechanism with which EMBRAPA would monitor the programs, as well as
a basis for general management of research. Monthly or quarterly alloca-
tions of funds, together with periodic reports of expenditures and annual
financial accounting, would provide effective financial monitoring, while
the annual workshops and reports on technical aspects of the project would
supply data for effective scientific evaluation and monitoring for the
research programs. EMBRAPA would provide the Bank with detailed quarterly
reports on the status of project execution (see Annex 17). An assurance
that this would be done was obtained at negotiations.
4.09      Evaluation of Project Organizational Structure and Management.
Since many of the organizational links between state, area and commodity
centers, national programs, and EMBRAPA headquarters are still being devel-
oped (see Annex 17), it would be necessary for EMBRAPA and the Bank to review
and revise arrangements, as necessary, at a later date. Assurances were
given by EMBRAPA that it would prepare a full review of its structure and
research program administration with the Bank by December 31, 1978. This
review would take place after completion of the first three years of project
execution and would concentrate on study areas such as (a) adequate exchange



- 19 -
of ideas between producers and research staff, (b) participation of research
staff in research program planning, (c) methods by which both administrative
and technical problems are resolved between administrative (headquarters) and
operational (experiment station) staff, (d) methods by which research pro-
grams respond to the needs of development plans at the federal, state and
local levels, and (e) level of staffing at the various research centers and
significant progress towards achieving project goals in this crucial area.
Necessary revisions, as mutually agreed upon, would be implemented at that
time.
Contract Research
4.10      EMBRAPA would provide support for research on special problems
of priority concern in agricultural development to be conducted under con-
tract or agreement by organizations with special staff capabilities or
facilities. Matters requiring such attention would be identified by
EMBRAPA and studies would be planned jointly by EMBRAPA and the institu-
tion or agencies which would actually do the work.
B. Staffing
4.11      The reorganization of the Brazilian research system provides
EMBRAPA with the opportunity to selectively employ research scientists
from formerly independent research institutes and to reassign them accord-
ing to their abilities and preferences. However, there are not nearly
enough scientists to fulfill the research programs that are envisaged.
EMBRAPA management has anticipated the extent of the recruiting problem
created by the expansion program and is currently undertaking an extensive
in-service training effort to upgrade the staff it already has. At the same
time, university graduates are being selected, employed, and sent on to post-
graduate studies.
4.12      A personnel manual, "Plano de C:rgos e Salarios," has been pre-
pared providing detailed information for employees on policies regulating
salaries; minimum training requirements for specific staff positions; and
comprehensive job descriptions and guidelines for staff selection, evalua-
tion and promotion. Staff positions are basically divided into three cate-
gories: researchers, research support staff, and administrative staff. At
present, overall EMBRAPA salaries compare favorably to salaries paid in
Government institutions and not unfavorably to salaries paid in private
industry. The comparative attractiveness of a given salary, however, varies
according to geographical region. Through annual performance reviews, special
individual achievements can be rewarded by salary increases and promotion,
or both. EMBRAPA's Department of Human Resources is constantly revising
salary policies in order not to lose qualified staff to other employers who
could offer better and more attractive conditions of employment.



- 20 -
4.13      The staff development pattern, based on present staff and anti-
cipated project requirements for each program, is as follows: 40% of total
staff in the first year and 60%, 70%, 80% and 100% during the second to
fifth years, respectively. The staff development pattern has been integrated
with the fellowship program (see Annex 17).
C. Consultant Services
4.14      There is currently in Brazil a shortage of highly trained personnel
for agricultural research leadership and program planning roles. Scarcity
of senior personnel with adequate field experience is particularly acute for
the farming systems program in the semi-arid tropics and for the commodity
programs involving cotton, cassava, rice, beans, rubber, and sheep and goats.
The ambitious in-service training program that is needed during the early
years of project execution would tend to aggravate the personnel problem
during that period. To remedy this, the project would supply 61 man-years
equivalent of consultancy services (one- to three-year contracts) in specific
disciplines and areas of specialization, including one adviser for five years
who would work closely with EMBRAPA's Director of Technology and Science.
In addition, 39.5 man-years of short-term consultancy services (less than one
year) would also be provided. It is expected that the long-term consultants
would make a valuable contribution in helping to develop leadership capabili-
ties and in training; the short-term input by specialists would have special
advantages in the area of research program conceptualization and evaluation,
while providing a link to similar programs at the International Research
Centers.
4.15      In view of the large number of specialists to be hired and the
difficulty of locating competent specialists in many of the specific areas
of expertise listed, EMBRAPA would contract an organization, or organiza-
tions, having specific experience in selecting and administering large
numbers of consultants. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that
EMBRAPA would engage such an agency or agencies, under terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank. Terms of reference for key specialists are included
in Annex 17.
D. Training and Fellowships Program
4.16      EMBRAPA has estimated its present and future needs for trained man-
power and a scale of priorities for training in different academic areas has
been established, indicating the number of professional staff to be included
in each. The most promising candidates for on-the-job training and for post-
graduate studies are identified, taking into account both EMBRAPA's priorities
and the educational and scientific achievement of the candidates. Legal
contracts between each beneficiary of the fellowships program and EMBRAPA are
signed to make sure that when the trainee completes his studies, he will work



- 21 -
for EMBRAPA a period at least equal to the fellowship study period. An eva-
luation has been made of Brazilian and foreign universities that offer M.Sc.
and Ph.D. degrees, relevant to the training needs of EMBRAPA. These universi-
ties are then contacted and invited to participate in the training program.
The availability of student places at these universities, per academic field,
has also been assessed. Thirteen universities have been classified as suitable
in Brazil, and EMBRAPA has direct contacts with two American universities -
Wisconsin and Purdue - going back to a USAID loan in 1971 (see Annex 17). The
Universities of Wisconsin and Purdue, in turn, can subcontract with other
universities. At present, Brazilians study at 28 United States universities
and at one in Canada. Under the proposed project agreement, however, study
in other countries would also be eligible for Bank financing and would be
encouraged.
4.17      The project would provide for 786 long-term fellowships (123 Ph.D.
degrees and 663 M.Sc. degrees) and 180 short-term fellowships. About one-
fifth of the Ph.D. program would be for studies at Brazilian universities
and the rest abroad, while about four-fifths of the M.Sc. program would be
for studies within Brazil and the rest abroad. The total of 786 long-term
fellowships would include 338 for the training of EMBRAPA staff to work
at research stations in the project area, although not necessarily on the
specific commodity programs included in the project. This has been done
to help ensure a balanced development of staff and research capacity within
EMBRAPA.  During negotiations an assurance was given that EMBRAPA would
employ the services of one or more specialized agencies to help administer
the fellowship programs, under terms and conditions satisfactory to the
Bank.
V. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
5.01      All commodities included in the project, with the exception of
rubber, are significant sources of income for a great many farmers both
large and small. Additionally, maize, beans, cassava, rice and sheep/goat
meat are important elements of the staple diet of the poorest segment of
the population. Cotton is a major cash crop in the Northeast and its
production absorbs a substantial amount of labor. An analysis of the demand
and supply situation of these commodities shows that, at present, production
does not keep up with the combined effects of population growth and rising
demand. Since farmers do not have the means to carry out their own research,
an expanded official effort in agricultural research on these commodities
is urgently needed, both to improve the socio-economic conditions of the
farmers, and to achieve the objectives of the Government's five-year devel-
opment plan..
5.02      The socio-economic situation of most of the farmers in the project
area is precarious. A Sudene-IBRD/DRC Survey (1973) of the Northeast shows
that the distribution of land is a good proxy for the distribution of capital,
wealth and income among landholders. In the three agro-ecological areas
of the project, about 65% of the farm holdings, or 1.6 million farms, are
of less than 10 ha. The farm population of more than 8 million people on



- 22 -
these farms is living in relative poverty, as defined by the Bank's sector
policy paper on rural development. The per capita income of virtually all
of these families is estimated to be below the relative poverty level, or
US$225 (about one-third total per capita income in Brazil in 1974). The
project is expected to lay the basis for substantial improvements of the
living conditions of this segment of the population. The project would
also benefit up to 400,000 families living on farms in the 11 to 50 ha range.
Most of these families also have incomes below the relative poverty level.
5.03      The potential for improving production is very great since crop
yields in the project area are low compared to those in other Latin American
countries and in other regions of Brazil. Livestock production is similarly
inefficient, and the current extensive use of land and labor is indicative
of the enormous potential for economic and social gains through more inten-
sive use of these resources. In the Campos Cerrados and Amazonia areas,
farming systems are in a rudimentary stage of development. Much practical
knowledge has been developed in various parts of the world with similar
ecological conditions and even in the more advanced regions of Brazil,
but it has not yet been adapted by scientists to the needs and conditions of
farmers in the Northeast, Amazonia, and Campos Cerrados. Agricultural
research is thus vital to open up the land economically and to ensure that
its full potential is realized, especially since these areas represent a
significant part of the world's remaining potential for increased food
production.
5.04      It is impossible to quantify the benefits of this particular
project in precise terms; however, it is evident that an expanded and
effective research program in the project area would serve as an important
catalyst for general development and would have substantial long-term
advantages. There is also abundant evidence from other countries, on the
basis of rates of return to individual crop research programs, and in terms
of aggregate output per hectare, that agricultural research is a sound
national investment, with estimated internal rates of return ranging as
high as 90% (see Annex 18).
5.05      This project would have a major impact on the organization and
philosophy of the Brazilian agricultural research system. The commodity-
oriented, multi-disciplinary team approach to the problems of the region,
concentrating on a few commodities, shows great promise. Many of the
constraints to economic agricultural production, particularly in the North-
east and Central-West, will not be removed until adequate research provides
the new technology that can be used to overcome the basic problems of
plant/soil/moisture relationships and makes available new crop varieties.
The expansion and improvement of farming-systems research, to adapt the
results of research to a broad range of farms in different areas, should
lead to a better overall utilization of available resources. The project
would also help EMBRAPA to consolidate a multiplicity of research stations
and research centers which previously pursued uncoordinated and inadequately
funded research programs and would be an effective device to help integrate
research and extension services to the benefit of a great many individual
farmers. Indirect beneficiaries would ultimately be a major portion of
the population of the project area.



- 23 -
VI. RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01      During negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government
that it would provide the necessary counterpart funds in a manner to assure
timely completion of the project (para 3.11); and that
6.02      EMBRAPA would:
(i)  carry out, simultaneously with the project development,
a required minor upgrading and repair to existing
buildings and facilities for which major civil works are
not planned (para 3.05);
(ii)  prior to submitting disbursement requests for civil works
at research stations, complete the purchase and/or arrange
for the legal rights for use of the land at the respective
stations (para 3.13);
(iii)   maintain adequate project accounts and would provide
the Bank with satisfactory audits within four months
of the end of each fiscal year (para 3.14);
(iv)   maintain, during the project implementation period,
an adequately staffed Technical Support Unit for civil
works and procurement purposes (para 4.02);
(v)   submit to the Bank, for approval, the detailed develop-
ment plan for each station to be financed under the
project before inviting construction bids (para 4.02);
(vi)   provide detailed quarterly reports to the Bank on the
status of project execution (para 4.08);
(vii)   prepare a full review of its structure and research
program administration for discussion with the Bank
by December 31, 1978, and would undertake any revisions
which may be mutually agreed upon after the completion
of the first three years of project execution and
program development (para 4.09); and
(viii)   contract with an agency or agencies, under terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank, to engage necessary
consultant services (para 4.15) and to administer the
fellowship programs (para 4.17).
6.03      With the indicated assurances, the proposed agricultural research
project constitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$40 million for a
term of 20 years, including five years of grace.
