BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
I. INTRODUCTION
1.        While agricultural output has been increasing at a satisfactory
pace, agriculture's relative share of GNP fell from 19.9% in 1960 to 16.1%
in 1972 (Table 1). Further estimates by the Getulio Vargas Foundation
indicate that this participation fell to 15% in 1973, a trend that can be
explained as being a result of the economic development process, which
brought about the rapid expansion of the industrial and services sectors.
Despite this relative decline of agriculture in GNP formation, however,
the importance of the sector in the national economy has grown considerably,
not only in terms of domestic supply, but also in its competitive position
relative to international trade. In recent years, Brazil's farm sector
grew at an average rate of 6.7% per year. In 1973, according to preliminary
estimates, it accounted for 15% -of total national product and 62% of Brazil's
exports. Farming uses 35% of the total area of the country and 44% of its
economically active population.
2.        Brazil, traditionally an agricultural country, still has massive
potential as a producer of food and raw materials. To date, agriculture
has expanded, primarily by increasing the amount of land in crops, with
only small gains coming from higher productivity. However, because of
regional limitations of climate and soil (para 14), it is becoming increas-
ingly necessary to intensify and diversify farming systems if agriculture
is to continue developing at even its present annual rate of 6.2%.
3.        The recent performance of the farm sector, where production has
grown faster than the population, has ensured equilibrium in domestic
demand, thereby avoiding inflationary pressures. Furthermore, export
surpluses of products such as cotton, soybeans, tobacco, corn, sugar,
juices and oils have served to offset, to some degree, the adverse balance
of payments situation created by increased prices for imported oil, which
were marketed on the international market. To sustain national development
plans and maintain growth rates akin to the industrial and services sectors,
agriculture will have to be restructured and modernized, to achieve high
rates of productivity per unit area. Agricultural research has the basic
mission of generating technology capable of contributing to the steady
upward trend of economic production and social improvements in the rural
sector.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
II. PRODUCTION FACTORS
Major Agro-ecological Areas
4.        Brazil is rather easily divided into five major agro-ecological
areas, which follow fairly closely the five main geopolitical regions -
North (Amazonia), Northeast (Semi-arid zone), Central-West (Campos Cerrados).
Southeast, and South (Table 2). Emphasis has been on expanding the areas
cultivated in the North and Central-West regions and upon increasing produc-
tivity in the remaining three, but with particular emphasis on the Northeast.
5.        The North occupies more than 3.5 million square km, or approximately
42.1% of national territory, including all of the Brazilian Amazon. This
region is made up of the states of Amazonas, Para and Acre, and the terri-
tories of Amapa, Rondonia and Roraima. Annex 14 provides details.
6.        The Northeast, with 18.2% of national territory, includes the
states of Maranhao, Piaui, Ceara, Rio Grande do Norte, Paraiba, Pernambuco,
Alagoas, Bahia and Sergipe, plus the territory of Fernando de Noronha.
Population is 28.1 million, or 30.3% of total population, and population
density is 18.5 per km . Population growth in the 1960-70 decade was esti-
mated at 2.3% per annum. Details are in Annex 12.
7.        With an area of 92.5 thousand km2, the Southeast represents 10.8%
of national territory. It comprises the states of Minas Gerais, Espirito
Santo, Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo. Population is 40.3 million, or 42.7%
2
of total population, and population density is 43.6 per km . Population
growth in the 1960-70 decade was estimated at 2.5% per annum.
8.        The South has an area of 577.7 thousand km2, or 6.8% of the national
territory. It comprises the states of Parana, Santa Catarina and Rio Grande
do Sul. Population is 16.7 million, or 17.6% of total population, and popu-
lation density is 28.9 km . Population growth in the 1960-70 decade was 3.4%,
due in part to large-scale immigration into the state of Parana.
9.        The Central-West has an area of 1.87 million km2. or 22.1% of
national territory. It comprises the states of Mato Grosso and Goias.
Population is 5.17 million, or 5.5% of total population, and population
density is 2.7 per km . Population growth in the 1960-70 decade was
estimated at 5.4% per annum. Annex 13 has details.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
10.       A study of the data 1/ for the past decade (1960-70) shows the
following shifts in relative importance of the five areas based on value
of agricultural products:
Relative Change in Agricultural Production
Region           1960           1970
North             1.4            1.5
Northeast        18.0           18.5
Southeast        44.1           37.5
South            30.5           36.0
Central-West      6.0            6.5
Brazil          100.0%         100.0%
The major change in emphasis, from the Southeast to the South, has been due
to a shift in coffee cultivation from the Southeast, because of disease,
and the increasing economic importance of wheat and soybeans in the South.
Nationally the 10 major agricultural crops are, in order of economic impor-
tance, rice, coffee, maize, cotton, sugarcane, beans, cassava, soybeans,
bananas and citrus. The states showing the most significant increases in
agriculture were Parana, Hato Grosso, Goias, Maranhao and Ceara. Further
information on distribution of agricultural product, labor force and pro-
duction indices is provided in Table 3.
Climate
11.       The continental size of Brazil and its location, mainly between
the Equator and the Tropic of Capricorn, determine the climatic variations
leading to a range of different cropping and livestock patterns. Although
tropical farming activities predominate, the regional and subregional dif-
ferences due to climatic features bring about clear distinctions between
the cropping patterns in each of the five main agro-ecological regions.
Minimum and maximum mean annual temperatures (60 and 280C, respectively)
are an indication that most of the national territory lies within the
temperate and hot climates. However, extreme temperatures have been
observed in the subtropical region of Rio Grande do Sul, with a minimum of
-14.10C in Sao Francisco de Paula, and the maximum, 45.80C in the 'Sertao'
or dry interior of Bahia in the Northeast. As for temperature ranges, the
1/   Source:  E1BRAPA:  Program to Strengthen Agricultural Research, Vol. II,
page 19, 1975.



ANNEX I
Page 4
least variation occurs in the low latitudes of the humid tropics (Belem,
Para, with 1.30C variation) and the other extreme in the more southernly
regions, such as Santa Victoria do Palmar, Rio Grande do Sul, with a
temperature spread of 11.60C.
12.       Annual rate of light intensity, an extremely important factor
for agriculture, shows considerable variation between the agro-ecological
regions, as a function of latitude. As an example, Western Santa Catarina
has recorded only 1,600 hours of sunshine per year and northeastern Bahia
a maximum of 3,200 hours.
13.       Huch of Brazil's national territory receives rather abundant
rainfall, with average amounts of precipitat-on exceeding 1,000 mm.
Exceptions to this rule are certain parts of the "sertao" and the semi-
arid regions of the Northeast where precipitation ranges from 500 to 700 mm,
causing periodic failure of crops based on traditional agriculture. In
the more southernly parts of Brazil, despite high rates of annual precipi-
tation, serious problems are caused by the poor distribution of rain: too
rmuch rain falls during the winter months followed by periods of drought
in summer.
Soils
14.       Classification of certain soils, as well as their agricultural
potential, began in the 1930s. This was a pioneering effort by several
soil scientists working in Sao Paulo. Since then, programs to prepare
soil maps for all of Brazil have been intensified and maps have been made
in varying detail for several regions. The first preliminary map of
Brazilian soil types was published in 1966. According to the studies
available, and considering the variables of climate, vegetation, geomorphic
and geologic origin, Brazilian soils are, generally speaking, low in ferti-
lity, rather deficient in exchange bases and show considerable variation
from region to region. From the standpoint of international classifica-
tion, Brazilian soils fall into two large categories: lowland latosols,
which occur primarily in the vast Amazon area, have drainage problems
and are generally low in fertility, and highland types, which occur
primarily on the Brazilian crystalline shield and where the diversity
of bedrock leads to different types of soils, including high fertility
series such as those in northern Parana and in the grasslands of Rio Grande
do Sul. Based on present knowledge, it can be stated that approximately
85% of Brazilian soil types show characteristics which are limiting, to
some degree, for agricultural production. Drainage problems are typical
of many of the soil types of Amazonia, low natural fertility, with poor
moisture retention in some soils of the semi-arid regions of the Northeast,
being illustrative of a few of the problems involved.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
15.       Continued expansion of Brazil's agricultural frontiers no longer
remains a simple matter in the long term, and finite limitations to such
expansion are already evident in some of the more populous areas of the
country. Continued incorporation of new lands also appears to be a ques-
tionable procedure in some areas such as the Amazon basin, for instance,
where detrimental effects to the rather delicate balance of the existing
environment frequently follows the clear.ing of virgin forest.  For this
reason, research efforts to investigate the means whereby increases in yield
per unit area can be achieved take on particular significance for national
development plans for the agricultural sector.
Population Aspects
16.       The population of Brazil, according to the 1970 census, was
94,508,554, spread in varying concentrations throughout the different
geographical regions of the country. This diversification, which is
seen primarily along Brazil's coast, is the result of the difficult
settlement conditions found in the interior of the North and Central-
West. The mountains and the climate combine to limit the penetration
of settlers to the interior plains. However, the settlement of the
lnte.lroof Brazil is now a recognized policy and is bascd on the need
for territorial integration, expansion of the agricultural frontier,
and the search for new mineral resources to further Brazil's industrial
expansion, as well as to feed its increasing population. Table 2
provides information from the 1970 census, showing the area, population
and demographic density of each of the five geographic regions.
17.       The first conclusion drawn from the figures of Table 2 is
that the South and Southeast, which together account for 17.6% of
the national territory, contain 60.3% of Brazil's total population
and also show the highest population density rates. The remaining
39.7% of the population is scattered over more than 7 million square
km, except for a few important urban centers in the North and Northeast.
The North, which has more than 42% of national territory and only 3.9%
of the population, has a population density of only 1.02 inhabitants
2
per km



ANNEX 1
Page 6
III. ROLE OF SELECTED COM4ODITIES IN AGRICULTURAL ECONOMY
18.       Of the nine commodity groups selected for attention under indi-
vidual research programs within the project, four (rice, maize, beans,
cassava) are basic food crops and one is an important export crop, i.e.,
cotton. In the area of livestock, beef and milk cattle, plus sheep and
goats, all seem destined to play an important role in supplying much
needed protein-s to a growing population.  The ninth, rubber, must meet
rapidly increasing domestic demand where, if possible, it could eventually
reduce the heavy import expenses currently being incurred for this commod-
ity (Table 4).
Relative Importance of Selected Crops by Region
19.       Based on value of product (1973) data 1/ we can have some
indication of the current relative importance of each crop in the three
geographical project areas as follows:
(a) Northeast: beans, cassava and cotton all play a leading role,
sheep and goats and rubber (plantation) are in second place,
maize/sorghum and beef cattle in third place, and milk and rice
production in fourth place.
(b) North: only with one crop, rubber, does the North play a
leading role, and this is mainly through the extractive rather
than the plantation rubber industry; however, it is responsible
for 92.1% of national rubber production; in all the remaining
commodities chosen, the North is in fifth place but has great
potential for expansion with rice, beans, cassava, beef cattle
and maize.
(c) Central-West: rice production is in second place nationally
and the region is in third place for milk production, much of
which is actually consumed in the Central-South, the relative
importance of the area for beans, cassava, maize, cotton, sheep
and goats is relatively minor (fourth place nationally), but
the potential for increased production of these commodities
is considerable. (See also Table 5 for details of production
by regions.)
Potential for Future Production of Selected Crops by Region
20.       Just as current production levels are dictated by many factors,
such as climate, soils, infrastructure, technological inputs, credit,
fertilizer, fiscal incentives and availability of labor, the marketing
1/   Source:  Anuario Estatistica Brasileira, IBGE, 1974.

of the commodities can, and does, play a major role in decisions which
influence their production. Broad annual fluctuations in the production
of the principal export and food crops are indicative of this (Table 6).
21.       Taking the above factors into account, however, and in the
light of existing conditions, there would appear to be reasonable
assurances that potential increases of 4 to 7% per annum could be achieved
within the parameters of calculated demand and production potential.
Further, with the exception of rubber, there would appear to be no known
technical constraints, as opposed to market forces, which would prevent
such goals being reached within the five-year period of project execution.
Even so, it is realized that actual goals, and, consequently achievements,
may be, of necessity, amended to reflect the desired levels of economic
and social development of the rural sector influenced by the project.

AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH SERVICES
A. Introduction
1.        Progress in agricultural services, particularly in research, has
varied greatly over the past century as to regional emphasis and effect
on agricultural production. It is gen7erllY agreed , however, that when
one area is compared to another, research and agricultural education have
been more effective in the South and Southeast than in the Northeast
and North.
B. Agricultural Education Services, Higher Education
2.        Building up the number of institutions and providing agricultural
courses at the university level has been a slow process. By 1973, 40 insti-
tutes offered 57 programs in the agricultural sciences, but most of these
programs had been started in the past 25 years. The majority cover agronomy
or veterinary medicine, although forestry, animal husbandry and fisheries
programs were introduced in the last decade.  Repnrts 1/ indicate that the
quality of instruction given by different institutes varies considerably,
with an overall professor-student ratio of one to seven. This may be
misleading, however, since many professors teach part-time only.
3.        In 1973, 15,090 students were enrolled in the agricultural sciences,
providing a ratio of only 16 to each 100,000 inhabitants, low for a country
so greatly dependent on the agricultural sector. On the other hand, producers
have been slow to employ agricultural science graduates. The Government
employed fully 79% of all professional agriculturalists working in 1973,
i.e., 7,973 out of a total of 10,288. In view of the preponderance of recent
graduates in the total number of professional staff available and the obvious
dominance of the state and federal government as the major employers, it
would seem that the current number of student openings at the undergraduate
level (4,490 in 1973) is more than enough to meet current demand for grad-
uates' services.
4.        Graduate study programs for agricultural sciences started in 1961
and by 1972 there were 29 programs being offered at the MI.Sc. but only
six at the Ph.D. level. MAost popular areas for post-graduate studies are
agricultural economics, plant science, soils and animal husbandry. There
were 454 openings for graduate students in 1972.
1/   Data supplied by EMBRAPA's project preparation unit.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
5.        The demand for post-graduate students is still not clearly
defined, particularly in the private sector. During the 15 years such
programs have been in operation, records show that 48.5% stayed on to
teach, an equal percentage went into Government positions, and only 3%
went to private industry. Until greater numbers of post-graduate students
are required in the private sector, it would appear that the number of
openings available for post-graduate study is more than adequate. Table 1
provides information on the relationship between population, enrollment
and number of agricultural estab'Lishments by region.
C. Agricultural Research Services
6.        The development of agricultural research services has gone
through three periods. The first period ran from the late 19th Century
up to the mid-1940s, when plantation agriculture was at its peak, but
there was no defined farm policy to provide clear research objectives at
the federal level. Efforts were largely confined to the major export
crops such as coffee, cacao and sugar, and emphasis was clearly on expand-
ing the agricultural frontiers rather than increasing production per unit
area. This period ended as industrialization began to play a more impor-
tant r C - in c                 - -C -vl -* CJ'.  -At M ^c--d pcrivd Wctll from the mid-1950s
to the mid-1960s when economic expansion of the industrial base was a
clear prerogative and allocation of resources, away from agriculture,
resulted in less support for this sector, particularly its research
institutions. With the gradual withdrawal of support from institutions,
at both the federal and state levels, several went out of existence entirely
and research activity was severely curtailed at others.
7.        The third period, which began in the early 1960s and continues
to the present, is characterized by a broader understanding of the role
of agriculture in the economic development process. It has been recog-
nized that expansion of the agricultural frontier alone cannot continue
to meet demands from both domestic and foreign markets and that higher
productivity per unit area must be part of agricultural policy in the
future. In 1962, the Ministry of Agriculture was reorganized and rural
universities became autonomous. As a result, agricultural education
and research were no longer subordinate to a single administration. The
old National Center for Research and Agricultural Education (CNEPA) was
disbanded and its research duties taken over by the Department of Agri-
cultural Research and Experimentation (DPEA). In 1970, three more
institutes were added and the Department moved from Rio to Brasilia.
The name was changed to Office of Research and Experimentation (EPE),
but in 1971 it became the National Department for Agricultural Research
(DNPEA). This Department again had six divisions and nine regional
research institutes.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
8.        In 1972, a Government group was formed to study agricultural
policy and propose institutional reforms, and, as a result of its
recommendations, the Empresa Brasileira de Pesquisa Agropecuaria
(EMBRAPA) was organized late that same year.
9.        The statutes of EMBRAPA are contained in Law No. 5,851 of
December 7, 1972, which outlines the agency's mai  fcto4-ns 1/ as
follows: (a) direct, control and execute agricultural research activ-
ities for the purpose of producing new technology for the development
of national agricultural production; (b) assist the Federal executive
branch, and its entities, in technical and administrative matters related
to the agricultural sector; (c) stimulate and promote the decentralization
of research activities to the benefit of state and local interests; (d)
provide technical coordination of research projects, the execution of
which involves the technical-administrative services of other Federal
agencies, (e) maintain close contact and coordination with the Brazilian
Technical Assistance and Rural Extension Corporation (EMBRATER) to
effectively execute diffusion of research results and technology, and
(f) plan, program and budget research activities to reflect the guide-
lines and policies established by the National Commission for Agricul-
tural Research, Technical Assistance and Rural Extension (COMPATER).
D. Agricultural Extension Services (EMBRATER)
Background
10.       Technical assistance and rural extension activities in Brazil
have long been fragmented, with responsibilities spread among several
institutions which, in most cases, provide commodity services in only
selected geographic areas. The most important of these institutions
until mid-1974 was the Brazilian Association of Credit and Rural Technical
Assistance (ABCAR), which was primarily responsible for disseminating
agricultural technology to farmers. ABCAR operated through 23 state
associations (ACARs), and all functioned as non-profit civil corpora-
tions, technically and administratively independent. Five training
centers (Ceara, Pernambuco, Minas Gerais, Espirito Santo and Santa
Catarina) also were part of the ABCAR network, providing basic and
advanced training for technical and auxiliary personnel in the rural
extension services. The ABCAR system, however, proved unsatisfactory
because of several major weaknesses.
1/   Only broad outlines of EMBRAPA's duties and responsibilities in the
areas of research and cooperation with agricultural extension ser-
vices are presented here.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
11.       First, the ability to transfer technology to farmers was impaired
by the weak financial and institutional structure of the extension system, 1/
plus the fact that Brazilian research operations provided virtually no
results that were relevant to the needs of large segments of the farming
sector, especially the small farmers in the Northeast. In any case,
extension activities were generally limited only to those farmers who were
actually receiving credit, the main objective being to assess the credit-
worthiness of the client and to ensure that he undertook the technical
recommendations necessary to make his enterprise a success. Also, coordina-
tion among institutions providing technical assistance in agriculture remained
at a low level, which resulted in an increased per unit cost of providing
such assistance, as well as a waste of scarce trained manpower available
for extension activity because of duplication of effort. Another problem
stemmed from the fact that, although ABCAR was supported by public funds,
it did not interact with other Government institutions. Most technical
assistance agencies lacked long-term planning and objectives, but in the
case of ABCAR, the situation was further complicated when different sponsor-
ing organizations pressured ABCAR into pursuing objectives that did not
contribute to accomplishment of the country's development goals. Further,
overall planning was hampered because of the instability and short-term
nature of the funds provided by the various na-e      -
12.       The Government is now taking positive steps to strengthen the
extension system, and, in an effort to overcome institutional and inter-
institutional impediments, it has assigned full responsibility for technical
assistance and rural extension to the Ministry of Agriculture (MA). Addi-
tionally, in mid-1974, the Brazilian Enterprise for Technical Assistance
and Rural Extension (EMBRATER) was created as an autonomous entity within
MA to supersede the ABCAR system. It is headquartered in Brasilia and
has 24 state extension institutions (formerly ACARs), which are now being
legally reestablished as State Extension Companies (EMATERs) under EMBRATER
guidelines. Staff at headquarters and state offices totals 8,318: 4,724
technicians with university degrees in agriculture, veterinary sciences or
home economics and 3,594 administrative personnel. Twenty-three technicians
and 23 administrative workers are stationed at headquarters (Table 2).
EMBRATER intends to expand to a total of 7,000 extensionists within two
years.
13.       The primary functions of EMBRATER are to collaborate with other
agencies of MA to formulate and implement policies pertaining to technical
assistance and rural extension, and to promote, stimulate, and coordinate
technical assistance programs. Centralization of all federal budgetary
allocations for technical assistance and extension gives EMBRATER control
1/   Past investigations have indicated that only 20% of available scientific
information was transferred to farmers. Moreover, a high proportion of
technical assistance flowed only to the larger and more creditworthy
farmers.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
over the state level agencies, which now will have to comply with EMBRATER
regulations if they wish to receive federal monetary support.
14.       In April 1975, "Guidelines for the Operational Structure of
EMBRATER" was published, having been put together by 15 working groups
integrated by interdisciplinarian teams of 155 technicians, representing
49 public and private institutions, plus 15 affiliates of the EMATER system.
The involvement of so many technicians, representing such a wide range of
institutions, reflects EMBRATER's decision to interact with all the insti-
tutions that contribute in some way to successful extension work. Simul-
taneously, this cooperative work is intended to lay the basis for future
collaboration with these entities.
Interinstitutional Relationships of EMBRATER
15.       EMBRATER regards itself as a catalyst for bringing about tech-
nical, economic and social change in the agricultural sector by mobiliz-
ing the respective institutions which must contribute to these efforts.
At the national level, EMBRATER will coordinate its work with agencies
handling research, rural credit, commercialization, rural cooperatives,
the supply industry, education, nutrition, health, and vocational
training.
Relationship between EMBRATER and the Future State Extension Companies
16.       The State Extension Companies will be sufficiently autonomous
in their day-to-day operations to allow flexibility. However, EMBRATER
will exert its influence at three levels:
(a) Political-administrative. Legal norms in the different State
Extension Companies will be equally applied. EMBRATER will
hold a capital share in each of the companies and will
participate in the selection and nomination of the management.
In order to get financing for their projects, contracts between
EMBRATER and the State Companies will have to be signed.
(b)  Planning.  Legal instruments will allow EMBRATER to have a
part in the planning of projects. General goals will be
outlined at the federal level and State Companies will
formulate their projects within this framework.
(c) Operational. EMBRATER headquarters staff will be assigned to
the state affiliates for consulting, orientation and training
purposes.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
Relationship between EMBRATER and EMBRAPA
17.       The National Commission of Agricultural Research and Technical
Assistance (COMPATER) has been set up as the coordinating link between
Empresa Brasiliera de Pesqisa Agropecuaria (EMBRAPA) and EMBRATER and will
be responsible at the federal level for synchronizing, reviewing and coor-
dinating programs of both agencies. Members of COMPATER will be one repre-
sentative of the Ministry of Agriculture, as Chairman; the presidents of
JDDAnA a EMIBRAT       one  reprresentat4ve ea^h fro'm the Central Bank, the
National Farmers' Confederation, and the National Agricultural Workers'
Confederation; and two representatives from groups outside the Government.
COMPATER will be jointly financed by EMBRAPA and EMBRATER. Within the two
institutions, the Department of Diffusion of Technology (DDT) of EMBRAPA
and the Department of Technical Support (DTS) of EMBRATER will act as
liaison units. At state level, the representative departments responsible
for diffusion of technology in the two institutions will work together.
The main point of contact between research and extension is intended to
be at the farm level, and extension bulletins (Pacotes Tecnologicos) are
to be prepared as a result of discussions between researchers, farmers
and extension staff.
18.       EMBRATER considers the new focus on projects as the main innova-
tion i-n the Brazilian extcnsion system.  Tn an official doc, ment P-itled,
"Manual for the Elaboration of Technical Assistance and Rural Extension
Projects," the following types of projects are covered:
(a) projects to increase productivity and improve living conditions;
(b) indirect production support projects; and
(c) projects to strengthen the extension system.
The way resources are allocated to crops, areas and types of beneficiaries
will indicate EMBRATER's order of priorities.
Production and Distribution of Improved Seed
19.       For the past decade, the Brazilian government has been acutely
aware of the need to produce and distribute improved seeds that would ensure
maximum returns from the application of new technology to agricultural pro-
duction. During the years 1963-69, a National Seed Improvement Plan
(PLANASEM) was put into operation under the auspices of the Miinistry of
Agriculture. On the basis of national priorities, the plan was designed
to provide services to two distinct regions. Because one of the regions,
which combined the south and southeast, had developed a technology that was
already fairly advanced at the farm level, it was given first priority.
Additional funds for implementation of this program in the seven states of



ANNEX 2
Page 7
the south and southeastern area were received under a loan (327/SF-BR) from
the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB). The execution period for this
project, with a total cost of US$25.0 million, was three years (1973-75).
Seed improvement programs for the second project region of the North-Northeast
and Central-West were given less priority because no improved technology had
been applied to agricultural production there in the decade of 1965-74. Start-
ing in 1973-74, funds (US$33.0 million) were made available, from the PROTERRA
program, through SUDENE for seed improvement in the Northeast, and later the
ministry of Agriculture allocated Cr$ 2.5 million (US$358,000) for studies
on rice, potatoes, beans, castor beans, cassava and maize. The Seed Improve-
ment Program for the Central-West began with a (1974) study of the needs in
this area. As a result, a program for seed improvement was devised which
concentrated on seven major crops--rice, cotton, peanuts, beans, maize, soy-
beans and wheat. That program was designed to cover a five-year period of
proejct execution, 1975-79, and it forms an integral part of the Second
National Development Plan (PND II). A similar plan has been developed for
the Northern Region, where activities will be concentrated along the Trans-
Amazonia highway axis. This plan, which is only now becoming operational,
is similar to that for the Central-West but will concentrate on four crops
only, i.e., rice, corn, beans and jute. The basic goal of all these programs
is to make each region somewhat self-sufficient in the production and distri-
bution of improved seed.
20.       EMBRAPA, through its Department of Technology and Science (DTS) is
well aware of the importance of improved seed as an essential link in the
transfer of improved technology to the farmer. EMBRAPA intends to play an
active role in this process by setting up regional and semi-autonomous com-
panies which will take the small quantities of foundation seed stock produced
at the experiment stations and multiply them up to the level of basic seed
stocks, which will then be sold to interested commercial enterprises who, in
turn, will produce the certified seed for farmers. EMBRAPA expects to act
only as a catalyst in this process and will release the companies to full
private enterprise control as soon as they have gained sufficient experience
in quality and cost control techniques, with a production level sufficient
to meet the demands of the region.

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR RICE
I. BACKGROUND
A.  Importance of Rice in Agriculture
Rice Production in Brazil
1.        Although Brazil is a big rice producer, its share is only 2.8% of
world production. Major exporters in international trade are: Thailand
with 29.5%, the United States with 26.8%, and Mainland China with 14%. Many
importing countries have become self-sufficient, thus causing international
prices to move toward relative stagnation and Brazil's exports to fall
some 46% in value during the period 1969-1973.
Brazilian Rice Exports - 1969-1973
Year            Quantity in Tons         Value - US$1,000
1969                 70,178                   7,821
1970                 94,745                   6,800
1971                148,829                  11,469
1972                  1,892                     152
1973                 33,422                   4,233
Source: CACEX Information Bulletins.
2.        Rice is one of the five most important products of the agricultural
sector in Brazil. It takes up 13.4% of the farming area, and it is produced
in all Brazilian states. The output is concentrated in certain areas and
seven states produced 88.5% of the rice harvested in 1972. These states are:
Rio Grande do Sul, where irrigated rice accounts for 20.5% of the country
output; Goias, where dryland rice cultivation accounts for 14.9%; Minas
Gerais, which turns out 13.4% of Brazil's output, and where the crop is
also developed under upland conditions; Sao Paulo, also with upland, producing
11.5% and M4aranhao with an output of 10.5% under upland cultivation. Other
rice producing states, of lesser importance, where upland rice is cultivated
are: Mato Grosso with 9% and Parana with 8.7% of national production. In
the Northeast the crop is also one of the most important, occupying 7% of
the region's cultivated area, and contributing approximately 15% to the
national output of rice. The region's principal producing states in 1972
were as follows: Maranhao, 71.8%; Piaui, 8.7%; and Ceara, 2.9%. The rice



ANNEX 3
Page 2
produced in the Northea$t is, for the most part, consumed in the region
itself. The relatively poor quality of the product is the main reason
for the low price it gets on the markets in the South and Southeast
of the country. However, rice culture in the Northeast still offers
major potential for improvements to both output and quality (especially
along the lower Sao Francisco Valley, in the states of Alagoas and Sergipe),
based on lowland rice culture in the flood plains or "varzeas" along the
lower reaches of the river.
Potentials and Constraints
3.        Given both the available land potential - particularly for rice -
and also the relatively low average production, at present (1.3 tons/ha)
the announced goal of 9.4 to 10.0 million tons by 1980 should be readily
attainable by raising the average national yield to 2.0 tons/ha, a modest
goal where the potential for lowland rice is five tons/ha and that of
upland rice, 1.75 tons/ha.
4.        Present constraints are related mainly to use of untested seed
of low yielding unimproved varieties, which also gives rise to low quality
grain. Imports of fertilizer are also low, and soil fertility - particularly
for upland rice in the Central-West, - one of the major producing areas - is
still a severely limiting factor. Lowland (irrigated) rice cultivation
in the Amazon basin is a relatively new technique, and some of the problems
caused by minor element deficiency, particularly sulphur, are only now
being identified and corrected. Even so, the major constraints to produc-
tion continue to be the lack of application of new techniques, rather than
lack of knowledge concerning new technology, which can readily be trans-
ferred from other major rice producing areas and quickly adapted to
Brazilian conditions. Further evidence of the failure to increase unit
area yields is the fact that during the period 1968-73, production increased
by 7.7% and area cultivated by 7.5%.
Demand Outlook
5.        According to the demand estimates based on food consumption research
and population increase, Brazil will require 9,450,000 tons for domestic
consumption by 1980. Add to this the average figure for exports (148.8
thousand tons), and there is an overall potential demand of approximately
9.6 million tons for 1980, even if Brazil only maintains its present position
in world trade. The role of rice production in Brazil's development stra-
tegy would appear to be, first, to maintain its status of self-sufficiency -
at present endangered during poor crop years - and, second, to regain its
export position of the early 1970s or annual exports of at least 150,000
tons.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
B. Current Status of Rice Research
Current Research
6.        Rice research is being carried out in all of the states of the
Northeast, under both upland and lowland conditions. The work done on
upland rice is located in the states of Maranhao and Piaui. Work is
also being done on low'land (paddy) rice in the basins of the public
dams (reservoirs), most of which are controlled by the National Department
for Drought Control Facilities (DNOCS), an agency of the Ministry of the
Interior, and along the banks of the Sao Francisco River, mainly in the
state of Sergipe. Various agencies are involved in rice research, such
as: the Superintendency for the Development of the Northeast (SUDENE),
the Sao Francisco River Valley Development Company (CODEVASF), the Agricul-
tural Secretariats of the states and by agronomy schools and colleges.
Lack of coordination, however, has resulted in dissipation of the limited
financial, material, and human resources available. In addition to these
problems, lack of continuity in research programs has also been a limiting
factor.
Organization and Personnel
7.        Sixteen institutions have been active in rice research.  The
number of technical personnel at these stations totalled 409 in 1974;
they do not work exclusively on rice research, but are also involved
in teaching activities (schools of agronomy), technical assistance for
irrigated areas (DNOCS), settlement and technical assistance (CODEVASF)
and state agricultural policy planning (agriculture secretariats). Of
the institutions active in the agricultural sector of the Northeast and
East, only EMBRAPA is primarily concerned with agricultural research and
experimentation through the Federal research institutes, Instituto de
Pesquisas Agropecuarias do Nordeste (IPEANE), Instituto de Pesquisas
Agropecuarias do Este (IPEAL), and a state Agricultural Research Institute
(IPA).
Achievements
8.        Most of the research work done concerns spacing, fertilizer
levels, selection of varieties and simple breeding trials. From this
work, significant yields of up to 1,500 kg/ha for dryland rice, with
varieties CICA-4 and IAC-47 have been obtained. With lowland (paddy)
rice culture, yields of 6,200 kh/ha to 7,000 kg/ha have been recorded
with CICA-4 and IR-655 varieties. Unfortunately, these results have,
so far, had only limited application at the farm level, and this has
proved to be the major constraint to improving upon the national average
yield. Research has proved, however, that significant gains can accrue
to unsophisticated applied research methods when technology transfer to
the farmer is not neglected.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
II.  THE NATIONAL RICE RESEARCH PROGRAM
A. Brief Description
Agro-ecological Coverage
9.        Since rice is an important crop in all 26 states, the agro-ecolog-
ical coverage to be given bY the national research program must cover all
five major regions i.e., the North, Northeast, Central-West, Southeast and
South. Currently, the last four regions mentioned are each responsible for
20 to 30% of production, and only 2.5% comes from the North. The potential
for greatly increased production from the latter region is, however, undis-
puted.
Program Goals
10.       The research program will have two main thrusts, and these will be
equally applied to both upland (dryland) and lowland (paddy) rice culture.
The programs will be designed to (a) increase yields per unit area, and (b)
to adapt varieties to conditions of new land areas and to develop cultivation
practices for these areas to permit rational expansion of the area in rice.
Ann"ial -roduction increases of 4.2% are thought to be necessary during the
1976-80 period to maintain Brazil's current position for both domestic and
export markets. Land under rice cultivation is expected to increase to 5.2
million acres by 1980, and this would indicate that varieties capable of
producing average yields of 1,800 to 2,500 kg/ha for upland rice under
commercial conditions must be developed under the program. A goal of
6 tons/ha/year for irrigated rice is also believed to be a reasonable
research goal.
Program Components
11.       In order to achieve the broad goals, as outlined above, the program
would have the following components:
(a) civil works at the National Commodity Center for Rice Research,
Goiania, and also at the state research centers (UEPAEs) of
Bacabal, Penedo, Rondonopolis, and Goiania;
(b) research farm development at the above stations;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at all stations
mentioned above;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship programs;



ANNEX 3
Page 5
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental establishment costs of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2, and 3.
B. Detailed Features
Varietal Improvement
12.       Efforts in breeding would be directed toward the developemnt of
high yielding, widely adapted, drought tolerant, fertilizer responsive,
more nutritive varieties resistant to prevailing diseases and insect pests.
In the development of these varieties, the plant pathologist, entomologists,
physiologists, chemists and agronomists would work together with the gene-
ticists and breeders in a team approach. Procedures would primarily involve
recurrent selection techniques with wide-based populations derived from
mixtures of elite germ plasm complexes. Selection would be done in the
areas for which improved varieties are desired and would take into account
those characteristics necessary for either upland or irrigated rice culture.
It is proposed that the breeding program have close coordination with similar
work being carried out at the International Center for Tropical Agriculture
(CIAT) and the International Rice Research Institute (IRRI).
Farming Systems and Cultivation Practices
13.       Work here would include studies related to land preparation, crop
associations, rotations, population density, irrigation, weed control and
harvesting methods.
Soils and Plant Nutrition
14.       This would include studies on major and minor element nutrition
timing, levels of fertilizer application, and economic factors governing
rates of fertilizer application.
Plant Health
15.       Studies on plant health would deal with the major pests and
diseases of rice and screening of new varieties for pest and disease resis-
tance. Also, tests of disease and pest control methods under normal produc-
tion conditions in the rice producing localities of the project area would
be conducted.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
Economics
16.       Economists would work closely with the research staff in assess-
ing economic impact of the cultural practices developed from the studies
as noted above. In addition, there would be independent studies concerning
the handling, storage and marketing aspects of the rice crop at all levels
of production.
C. Organization and Management
17.       The national rice research program team would be headquartered
at Goiania, Goias, together with the National Bean Research Program. The
station director would have overall responsibility for administration of
the headquarters station and would represent EMBRAPA for community rela-
tions at the center. He would be assisted by an advisory council 1/ and
three associate directors, one for administrative services, usually func-
tioning as the financial and procurement officer and two for the implemen-
tation of the research programs -- one for rice and one for beans.
l Q.      The associate director fbr research, as team leader/cocrdinator,
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs)
and other cooperating institutions concerned with research on rice and the
application of new technology in rice production would be achieved through
contacts between the team leader and representatives for the appropriate
research at these stations. An annual workshop would be held at which
current activities would be evaluated and plans for the research program
for the coming years would be made. Input from producers would be achieved
through representation at such workshops or field days in the distinct
sub-areas of major production as may be necessary. As a member of the
advisory council, the associate director for research would provide the
necessary liaison between the council and producers.
19.       Two hundred and forty hectares of land would be available at the
National Commodity Center for Rice Research at Goiania; in addition, ade-
quate laboratories and field workshops for the multi-disciplinary team of
researchers would be provided at this main station. These would include
plant physiology, plant pathology, entomology, soils and seed laboratories
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members,
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
example of membership).



ANNEX 3
Page 7
with the necessary equipment and furnishings. Facilities at the satellite
stations or UEPAEs would be as follows: Bacabal, 5 ha of land; Penedo, 5 ha;
Cuiaba, 3 ha, and Goiania, 3 ha. Field laboratories and crop storage facil-
ities, plus additional field equipment for field trial plot establishment both
at the stations and on farmers' fields would also be provided.
Research Station Network and Collaborating Institutions
20.       For the project area, the research station network comprises the
National Commodity Research Center at Goiania, plus the four state stations
(UEPAEs) previously mentioned. In addition, two of the three regional
Agricultural Research Centers -- at Belem, Para, for the North, and Planaltina,
D.F., for the Central-West -- would contribute data on farming systems research
for the principal crops including rice, being cultivated in these specific
agro-ecological areas. Linkages to rice research programs in the South and
Southeast will be through the regional and state-operated systems, yet to
be determined. Collaborating institutions in the Northeast would be repre-
sented by those currently engaged in rice research (para 7), namely, DNOCS,
CODEFASF, and IPA. In the South, there are two institutes in the state of
Sao Paulo and one in Rio Grande do Sul. A complete outline of the proposed
research network is given in Table 4.
D. Staffing
21.       The staff at full development would comprise 64 professionals
and 31 administrative support staff at the National Research Center for Rice,
Goiania, and an additional 30 professional staff and 21 administrative
support staff at the four satellite stations, UEPAEs, under the program.
Existing staff for rice research is estimated to be no more than 10% of
this requirement, and the staff would have to be developed mostly on an
in-service training basis. A detailed staff list is provided in Table 5.
E. Training and Fellowship Program
22.       To overcome the present lack of research workers for the program,
the project would provide for the academic upgrading of 27 professionals
to the M.Sc. degree level and five to the Ph.D. level. Details of this
training component of the project are provided in Table 2. A training
officer has been included in the staff list to assist in the selection of
candidates for the fellowship program.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
F. Consultants
23.       In order to strengthen the core of trained professional staff,
in the short run, the project would provide for 5.5 man-years of consultant
services to be assigned as mutually agreed upon between EMBRAPA and the Bank
(see Table 2 for details).
G. Linkages to International Centers
24.       As has been previously mentioned, the International Research
Centers of CIAT, Colombia and IRRI, the Philippines, can provide a wide
base of existing technology on rice research, including valuable germ plasm
and farming systems methodology, both for upland and irrigated rice. It
would be the responsibility of the program leader/coordinator to establish
contact with these institutions and to develop collaborative programs of
research within the scope of their established outreach programs.
III. COST ESTIMATES
25.       Estimated cost for the National Research Program for Rice for the
five-year period would be approximately US$9.0 million. The estimated costs
are summarized in Table 1.

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONiAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR BEANS
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Beans in Agriculture
1.        Brazil's bean production, at 2.2 million tons, is 20% of world
production and represents 6.35% of that portion of GNP derived from the
agricultural sector. Even so, Brazil fluctuates between the roles of net
importer to net exporter of edible beans. Species of two main genera
(Phaseolus and Vigna spp) 1/ are cultivated on 3.8 million ha; 44% of the
crop is produced in the Northeast; 28% in the South; 22% in the Southeast;
5.5% in the Central-West and only 0.5% in the North. Beans play an
important role in the Brazilian diet because of their high protein value
of approximately 20%.
-Productl4o" Pe'rfomac
2.        During the period 1960-71, there was a 3.8% increase in the
area under bean cultivation, but output did not respond accordingly and
by 1967 was stagnant for the remainder of the period under review. Many
factors contributed to this result, but the main reasons would appear to
be lack of certified seed, poor cultivation practices and lack of proper
incentives due to poor marketing facilities. The 1973 statistics show
production of 2,228,940 tons from 3,814,662 ha for an average production of
584 kg/ha. Average production for the states of Rondonia, Acre and
Amazonas, however, where Vigna _p*p are grown, showed annual yields of
approximately 1,000 kg/ha. High annual yields from Vigna spp reflect the
raising of two to three crops per year under conditions of the humid tropics.
Potentials and Constraints
3.        Per capita consumption is fairly stable at 26.0 kg/year but, as
the population drifts to the cities, this would tend to decrease somewhat
as there is evidence of negative demand elasticity as per capita income
increases in the urban sector. Based on population growth alone, however,
there would be an average internal demand increase of 3% per year, or an
additional 96,000 tons above the consumption figure of 2,500,000 tons in
1970. Taking into account shifts in demand preference, however, it is
1/   Throughout the report, only Phaseolus spp and Vigna spp are considered,
and the soybean (Glycine Max) is not included.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
estimated that annual increases of 2.7% in production would satisfy
Brazilian needs. In addition, the export demand remains strong and the
role of beans as a potential export crop, as they were during the period
1967-70, should not be forgotten. Domestic demand for 1973 was 3.2
million tons, while production was 2.2 million tons, giving a deficit
of one million tons. Demand for beans in Brazil in 1980 may well
reach 3,331,310 tons. The actual export demand for edible beans is
difficult to quantify, but potential exports of an additional 10%
(330,000 tons) seem a conservative estImat-  -ar -wuld also act as a
buffer stock against fluctuations in internal production and demand.
Role in Development Strategy
4.        First priority for increased production of beans is the goal of
self-sufficiency, or an increase of 50% to cover domestic needs, by 1980.
Of the two methods available for achieving increased production, i.e.,
increase cultivated area (by 1.9 million ha) or increase yield per unit
area, to 800 kg/ha - the latter alternative would be much more logical in
this case, since beans are cultivated in conjunction with corn or cotton
and both these crops show certain limitations to any comparable increase
in area under cultivation. The yield increase, of 37% over five years, is
high, but not unduly so when it is considered that several important bean
growing areas in the states of Para, Sergipe, Parana and Santa Catarina,
Rio Grande do Sul and Mato Grosso have already reached and surpassed this
goal. In view of the low level of current research on beans, particularly
Vigna spp, the increase of average yields to 800 kg/ha seems entirely
feasible. The gradual increase of 10% in the area cultivated over the
same period would not only lead to self-sufficiency, but allow for a small
export market.
B. Current Status of Bean Research
5.        In the north (Amazonia), research work on Phaseolus has been
carried out by research institutes in the states of Amapa, Acre and Roraima,
while work on cowpea, mainly fertilizer use, has been done in the State
of Para. Bean research in the Northeast has been quite widespread and
has been concentrated mainly on Phaseolus spp. Records 1/ show numerous
experiments at over 20 sites in all nine states of the region, concentrat-
ing on soil fertility, genetic improvement, plant protection and irrigation.
Average bean yields from the region, however, would seem to indicate that
new technology developed by the experiments either did not reach, or was
not financially viable for use by the producers to any significant degree.
1/   Source:  Reorganization of Research Resources in the Northeast
UFCE/CEPAS/SUDENE - 1974.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
In the Central-West, research has been much more limited and confined
to variety trials and cultivation practices in the states of Goias
and Mato Grosso.  In the Southeast, the states of Minas Gerais and Rio de
Janeiro have indicated work done on variety selection and breeding,
fertilizer use and production systems. Average yields for other states,
however, are still relatively low. In the South, the states of Rio
Grande do Sul and Parana have done considerable research on breeding,
disease resistance, weed control, virus studies, production systems and
trials with competing varieties and both show considerable increases in
production as a result. The main reason why findings of this research
have not received greater acceptance by growers, particularly small
farmers, is that beans are traditionally used as a catch crop combined
with maize, cotton or cassava culture. The higher yielding varieties
which have been developed must be grown intensively, with adequate
fertilizer and pest control methods, as a monoculture, as in the case
of soybeans. Also, because of low prices, farmers have been reluctant
to risk using other than traditional systems of production.
II. NATIONAL BEAN PROGRAM
A. General Description
6.        The national program for research on beans is designed to pro-
vide a new technology which would stimulate bean production in the main
growing areas of the Northeast, Southeast and South. Assistance would
also be given to the Central-West and North regions, which, although
of lesser importance, show considerable potential for the future as
population moves into these regions.
Objectives and Production Goals
7.        The main objective of the research program would be to provide
the technology essential to bring about the increases in production necessary
to maintain at least self-sufficiency in bean production, as indicated in
paragraph 4. Mlost of the beans are produced, for direct human consumption,
on small plots cultivated by small scale farmers and their families. In
Brazil, 80% are planted in association with other crops such as corn,
cotton, and cassava. Planting is done manually and virus diseases are very
common and cause the greatest losses. The crop is often subjected to varying
degrees of drought and flooding. Use of improved seed is rare, use of ferti-
lizer and other inputs is uncommon, and prices fluctuate considerably due
to lack of adequate marketing arrangements, pricing policies and storage
facilities.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
8.        The major production objectives of the program must be achieved
through wider use of technology by small farmers. The function of the
research program at the National Commodity Center (NCC) would be to
improve yield capacity and biological efficiency of varieties and to
develop appropriate agronomic practices for their maximum production in
different ecological situations. It would also involve the development
of technological packages and their application in close collaboration
with the state micro-regional research and production teams (UEPAEs).
This would be backstopped by production-oriented research carried out
at the commodity centers, to ensure that, by means of demonstration and
close cooperation at the farm level, the new techniques developed are put
into practice.
Program Components
9.        In order to achieve the broad goals, as outlined above, the
program would comprise the following components:
(a) civil works at the National Commodity Center for Bean Research,
Goiania, and also at the state research centers (UEPAEs) of
Teresina, Caico, Alagoinha, Irece, Barreiras, Manaus and
Altamira;
(b) research farm development at the above stations;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at all stations
mentioned above;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental establishment costs of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2, and 3.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
3. Detailed Features
10.       The first step in the research program would be a survey to
determine the biological and economic factors most limiting to production
in the ecological areas to be covered. This would more clearly define the
research priorities and other activities or modifications required to
attain the above objective. Although certain regional adjustments may
be needed after this survey, the research program, based on current
knowledge, would be concerned mainly with the activities and procedures
discussed below.
Varietal Improvement
11.       Considering the high losses now, due to insect pests, viruses,
and other diseases, the development of high yielding, highly stable disease-
and insect-resistant varieties would receive major emphasis. This research
activity would involve the identification of basic breeding materials with
high yield capacity and with as many desirable attributes as possible for
adaptation in the bean production macro-regions. Once the best materials
for each area were identified, populations would be formed with these
materials, heterogeneous for such desirable characters as yield, disease,
and insect resistance, through special multinle cross-breeding techniques.
Advanced segregated generations of these populations would be grown,
preferably in areas where there was an exceptionally high percentage of
natural cross pollination among plants provided by certain insects. Seed
of populations grown in these environments, consisting of a wide mixture
of genotypes, would then be planted in each of the areas for which improved
varieties are desired, and the most adapted plants with desired attributes
would be mass selected.  Seed of the selected plants would be mixed and
again planted in the environment where a high incidence of natural hybridiza-
tion occurs, and the resulting population then returned to the area in which
previous selections were made for further mass selection. Repetition of
this process would provide for a gradual concentration of desirable attri-
butes and could be continued for as many cycles as needed to establish the
variety. Within this procedure, the resulting variety would continue to
be a mixture of genotypes and might be further selected at the micro-
regional level by the UEPAE teams in collaboration with the farmers of
the regions. The formation and distribuLon of genetically pure varieties
should be avoided in the tropics because, with these kinds of varieties,
it is virtually impossible to maintain disease resistance for any length
of time.
Farming Systems and Agronomic Practices
12.       Production systems and agronomic practices would vary according
to location, and much of the research would have to be done at the micro-
regional level in collaboration with farmers. Research in these two



ANNEX 4
Page 6
aspects would involve studies with weed control, planting densities,
planting dates, and various cropping patterns, including interplantings
and multiple cropping. It would also involve the identification of the
kinds of varieties needed to permit the use of the most useful regional
cropping system evolved for farmer use at various levels of production,
i.e., small farmer to large grower.
Economic Research on Production Costs and Marketing
13.       Research in economics would be conducted to determine the cost
of production in various systems (input cost-product price relationships)
and to identify and solve marketing problems in the various regions of
production, including seasonal storage as may be necessary.
C. Organization and Management
14.       The national bean research program team would be headquartered
at Goiania, Goias. The station director would have overall responsibility
for administraton of the headquarters station and would represent EMBRAPA
for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an advisory
council 1/ and three associate directors, one for administrative services,
usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and two for
the technical implementation of the research programs --- one for beans
and one for rice.
15.       The associate director for research, as team leader/coordinator,
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program,
and would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs)
and other cooperating institutions concerned with research on beans and
the application of new technology in bean production would be achieved
through contacts between the team leader and representatives for the
appropriate research programs at these stations. An annual workshop
would be held at which current activities would be evaluated and plans for
the research program for the coming years would be made. Input from pro-
ducers would be achieved through representation at such workshops, or
field days, in the distinct sub-areas of major production as may be
necessary. As a member of the advisory council, the associate director
for research would provide the necessary liaison between the council and
producers (see Annex 17 for broad discussion of Organization and Management).
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members,
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
example of membership).



ANNEX 4
Page 7
Research Facilities
16.       Full laboratory and field research facilities would be located
at Goiania where 150 ha of land are available. Limited laboratory facil-
ities for farming systems and field demonstration plots would be made avail-
able at the following UEPAEs, with land areas available for bean research, as
noted, in the Northeast: Teresina (Phaseolus), 5 ha; Caico (Vigna), 5 ha;
Alagoinha (Phaseolus), 3 ha; Irece (Phaseolus), 5 ha; Barreiras (Phaseolus),
3 ha; and in the North: Manaus (Vigna), 5 ha, and Altamira (Vigna), 3 ha for
trials. Personnel may be seconded to these UEPAEs by the national program as
needed. The research network for the national program is shown in Table 4.
University Contract Research
17.       As with other national commodity programs, university contract
research would be requested through EMBRAPA headquarters, Brasilia (see
Annex 17). Rather than accept programs presented by the universities, the
plan should be to request university participation as and when needed and
confine the requests to carefully selected areas of research where answers
are needed for clearly defined problems of a basic nature and where specific
laboratory facilities and discipline-oriented inputs are more readily
available and can, at the same time, provide graduate study for university
educational programs. Care would always be required, however, to see
that duplication of effort is avoided and that specific problems related
to the current research programs are given national rather than regional
importance.
D. Staffing
Staff Requirements
18.       The staff of the national bean program as proposed, at full
development, would be as indicated in Table 5. There would be 64 profes-
sional staff assigned to the headquarters center at Goiania. This
includes some junior and supporting staff to be assigned to certain
outlying stations in the station network. In addition, there would be
39 professional staff and 27 administrative support staff at the UEPAEs
mentioned in paragraph 16.
Present Staff
19.       There are very few experienced bean workers in Brazil, and, as
in the case of the other commodities, the staff would have to be upgraded
largely on an in-service-training basis to fulfill the requirements of the
research program.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
E. Training and Fellowship Program
20.       The project would provide scholarship funds to train five staff
members to the Ph.D. degree level and 23 to the M.Sc. level and it would
also support in-service short courses for 14 participants selected from
the program for training in specific areas. In addition to degree train-
ing, major emphasis would be on in-service training of inexperienced young
men with B.S. degrees. As in other commodity programs, a training officer
has been included in the staffing pattern.  Insofar as possible, the
young staff members in the in-service training programs would work as
research assistants with more experienced staff members. Candidates for
short courses and M.Sc. training would be selected from the group that
has demonstrated interest and special competence in the national program
(see Table 2 for details).
F. Consultants
21.       The project would provide for three man-years of long-term and
one man-year of short-term consultancy services in the area of plant breed-
ing. In addition, 18 man-months would be provided for program evaluation
services (see Table 2 for details).
G. Linkages to International Centers
22.       Linkages with CIAT in Colombia and IITA in Nigeria would be
established through cooperative programs in the screening and evaluation
of materials. There are 10,371 collections of Phaseolus in the CIAT bean
bank and appoximately that many collections of Vigna in IITA. Cooperative
breeding programs would also be established where desirable and feasible.
Staff members would be sent to these institutions for in-service training
and short courses where indicated.
III. COST ESTIMATES
23.       The cost for the National Research Program for Beans for the
5-year period would be approximately US$9.2 million. The estimated costs
are summarized in Table 1.
