BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR MAIZE/SORGHUM 1/
I. INTRODUCTION
A.  Importance of Cron in Agriculture
1.        Maize is the principal grain crop in Brazil.  Production in
1974 was 16 million tons on 12 million ha. Eighty-three percent was
produced in the Southern and Southeastern region; about 12% was produced
in the Northeast, 0.5% in the North (Amazonia), and the remainder, about 5%,
in the Central-West (Campos Cerrados). Average yields per hectare are
low, about 1,350 kg. Unit area yields vary from region to region, from
a low of 800 kg/ha in the Northeast to about 1,500 kg/ha in the South
and Southeast. The low average productivity in general is primarily a
consequence of low soil fertility. Fertilizers and other soil improving
inputs are used in varying degrees on only about 16% of the total acreage.
Annual increases in production in the past have come from expansion of
area planted.
2.        Low soil fertility presents the major constraints to the accelera-
tion of unit area yields. Varieties in general are good, due to a successful,
dynamic breeding program, but only a small part of the yield capacity of
these varieties is now being realized because of generally low soil produc-
tivity. Unit area yields could easily be doubled through an economic solu-
tion to the soil fertility problem. Research leading to an improvement
in soil fertility and agronomic practices would have first priority in the
new national maize program.
3.        It is estimated that, by 1980, 21 million tons of maize will be
needed to meet local and export demands. To meet such a demand, an annual
average production increase of 5.2% would have to be sustained for the
decade. Presently, about 17% of Brazil's total production is exported to
Italy, Spain and Japan. In the Northeast alone, if local demands of maize
for food and feed are to be met, annual production will have to be increased
by 550,000 tons by 1980. This is about 35% more maize than is now being
produced in the area.
1/   Currently, millet is not included in EMBRAPA's approved program of
research. However, there is increasing interest in the crop and it
is expected that it will soon be included as a commodity in this
combined program.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        So far, sorghum has had only limited acceptance in Brazil.
Production is now concentrated in the South, where varieties introduced
from temperate areas are fairly well adapted. According to research
underway, sorghum has a good potential in the Northeast (agreste area).
Primary estimates indicate that sorghum production could reach 684,000
tons by 1980, and could be a substitute for maize as an animal feed.
Early results with sorghum trials in the Central-West (Campos Cerrados)
area indicate a high tolerance of some varieties to aluminum toxicity.
However, a gret deal of research work is needed with both varieties
and cultural practices before this region can be considered as one with
a high potential for sorghum production.
5.        Millet is generally unknown in Brazil.  It is widely grown as
a food crop in South Asia in relatively hot dry areas and could become
a useful crop in certain areas of the Northeast, especially where the
rainy season is very short. Research workers in India at the Interna-
tional Crops Research Institute for the Semi-arid Tropics (ICRISAT),
Hyderbad have developed some excellent varieties that should be tested
in Brazil. Some of these might be sufficiently well adapted for immediate
use in various cropping systems in hot, arid areas of the Northeast.
B. Current Status of Maize/Sorghum Research
Current Research
6.        Brazil's maize breeding program is one of the most dynamic in all
of Latin America. Highly productive varieties are now available for the
Southern and Southeast regions where over 80% of the 16 million tons of
maize are produced. It is estimated that over 50% of the total maize
planted in these two areas is planted with high yielding hybrids, developed
from a combination of local and exotic materials. Most of the remaining
area is planted with improved open pollinated varieties derived largely
through recurrent selection within exotic mixtures. Most of the seed is
produced by private seed companies or farmers' seed associations.
7.        In the North, Northeast and Central-West (Campos Cerrados) regions,
varietal improvement programs have only recently been initiated. An excel-
lent cooperative breeding program between the Institute of Genetics at
Piracicaba (Sao Paulo) and the Secretariat of Agriculture in Pernambuco
is underway in the Northeast. This program is primarily concerned with the
development of high yielding open-pollinated varieties for the different
climatic situations in the Northeast through recurrent selection within
wide-based populations consisting of various mixtures of elite tropical
materials. Considerable progress has been made and several high yielding
open pollinated varieties are now available for the Mata and Agreste areas
and for production under irrigation along the Sao Francisco river. Seed



ANNEX 5
Page 3
is being produced and distributed by SUDENE (Superintendency for the
Development of the Northeast). This cooperative breeding and seed
production program will continue to be promoted and further expanded
through the National Maize Program headquartered at Sete Lagoas.
8.        Research with maize in the Amazon area has largely been limited
to testing tropical varieties developed elsewhere. Many of the elite
tropical varieties, developed by CIMMYT (International Center for the
Improvement of Corn and Wheat) in cooperation with interested countries,
are adapted to the Amazonia region and could be made immediately avail-
able for commercial production. So far, little effort has been made
in the formation of varieties well adapted to acid soils of this region.
However, the prospects for developing such varieties are good.
9.        As previously mentioned, the major constraint to the acceleration
of maize production in Brazil today is not varieties, but mainly low soil
fertility combined with outmoded traditional agronomic practices. More
research is needed in the improvement of soil fertility and cultural
practices throughout Brazil, if highly productive technological packages
are to be made available to farmers and unit area yields increased. Very
little is now known about fertilizer requirements, population densities
and production systems for maize in the different maize producing area
of Brazil.
10.       Sorghum is a new grain crop for Brazil with excellent potential.
Research in the production of this crop is just getting underway and, with
the exception of the Northeast, is primarily concerned with screening exist-
ing varieties to identify materials useful in breeding programs or for
immediate commercial production. In the Northeast, the cooperative sorghum
breeding and production program between the state of Pernambuco and the
Ford Foundation has made excellent progress, and has clearly demonstrated
the potential value of this crop for areas with limited and poorly dis-
tributed rainfall. No research with millet is presently underway in
Brazil but, based on the performance of this crop in other arid areas
of the world, its value in arid areas of the Northeast ought to be
determined.
Present Research Station Network
11.       Maize breeding work was started by the Instituto Agronomico at
Campinas and the University of Vicosa in the early 1940s. The work at
Vicosa was largely taken over by Sementes Agroceres S.A. when it was
organized as a private seed company. Agroceres has done an outstanding
job in the development of hybrids for South and Southeast Brazil and
last year produced 2,500 tons of seed. However, simultaneously with
this important effort, the Instituto Agronomico at Campinas was developed
to serve as the main breeding center until about 1960. Since then, how-
ever, the Genetics Institute at Piracicaba has become the leading center



ANNEX 5
Page 4
for research in breeding and the development of corn breeders. The work
of the institutions at Campinas and Piracicaba has, from time to time,
been supplemented by individuals at certain other state and federal
institutions in Minas Gerais, Parana and Rio Grande do Sul.
Research Personnel
12.       There are only five highly experienced maize breeder-agronomists
in Brazil: namely, one in the Genetics Institute at Paracicaba, one in
the Instituto Agronomico at Campinas, one in the University of Campinas,
one in Agroceres, and one in Cargill Seed Company at Campinas. In
addition to these five, an outstanding M.Sc. graduate in maize breeding
was recently named as leader/coordinator of the national maize program.
In sorghum, there are two outstanding young men: one is an expatriate
with a Ph.D. degree provided by the Ford Foundation, and another, a
Brazilian with an M.Sc. degree, who has been named head of the national
sorghum program. There is no one presently in Brazil with experience
in millets. In addition to the experts mentioned above, there are about
150 junior and graduate level personnel engaged in specific projects
concerning maize/sorghum research working with the above leaders and
certain researchers at other institutions.
Research Achievements
13.       In spite of the outstanding accomplishments in breeding and wide
spread usage of improved varieties, unit area yields have not significantly
improved during the last 10 years. In the absence of well organized pro-
grams for the improvements of soil fertility and agronomic practices,
these varieties have made little impact. Increase in total production is
primarily due to an expansion of the area planted.
II. NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR MAIZE/SORGHUM
A. General Description
Northeast
14.       Major concentraton would be on multi-disciplinary research leading
to a greater efficiency of maize, sorghum and millet production in the North-
east, in collaboration with the Regional Research Center at Petrolina and with
indicated state research institutions, or UEPAEs, operated directly by EMBRAPA.
Research in maize will be aimed primarily at the development and utilization of
higher yielding, more nutritive, biologically efficient varieties, improved
cultural practices and more efficient production systems for the improvement
of income and general welfare of the small subsistence or semi-commercial



ANNEX 5
Page 5
farmers concentrated in the more humid, coastal areas. Research with
sorghums and millets will be focused mainly on the development and
utilization of high yielding, disease-resistant varieties and cropping
systems for a better exploitation of the limited and irregular rainfall
pattern of the Agreste and arid zones.
15.       The immediate goal for maize research during the next five years
will be to increase current production by 35%. For sorghum, the goal
will be 380,000 ha by 1980, with a total production of 684,000 tons, or
about 1,800 kg/ha. Very little sorghum is now being planted.
Amazonia
16.       The Amazon area is sparsely populated and very little commercial
maize is now being produced. Improved varieties developed in other areas
of the world in the humid tropics would also be adapted in the Amazon
region, or, if necessary, they could be readily adjusted to the area
through simple recurrent selection techniques. The major problem is to
determine the most efficient systems and agronomic practices most indi-
cated for the production of maize under prevailing conditions of soil
and climate for all kinds of farmers. Very little information or expe-
rience is presently available.
Campos Cerrados
17.       One of the first steps in the increase of maize production in
the Campos Cerrados, where very little maize is now grown, would be the
development of varieties and production systems adapted to the soils
and rainfall pattern of the region. The goal would be the development
of varieties capable of yielding two to three tons per hectare in crop-
ping systems with minimum inputs and adapted to mechanized production.
Program Components
18.       The purpose of the project is to strengthen research capability
as a means of increasing yields of maize/sorghum. To accomplish this, the
program consists of the following main components:
(a) civil works at the research stations Terezina, Caruaru, Penedo,
Irece, Altamira, Manaus, Goiania, Caico and Itapetinga to
provide the necessary laboratories and research facilities;
(b) research farm development at the above stations, plus head-
quarters station of Sete Lagoas;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at all stations
mentioned above;



AViNEX 5
Page 6
(d)  library, documentation and information services;
(e)  technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs;
(f)  land purchase; and
(g)  incremental establishment costs of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
Varietal Improvement
19.       Efforts in breeding would be directed toward the development of
high yielding, widely adapted, drought tolerant, fertilizer responsive, more
nutritive varieties resistant to prevailing diseases and insect pests. In
the development of these kinds of varieties, the plant pathologist, entomo-
lugis,s, physiGlogists, chemi_ts and a,,rcon^-sts .'uld work together -"it"
the geneticists and breeders in a team approach. Emphasis would be on the
improvement of open pollinated varieties rather than hybrids. Procedures
would primarily involve recurrent selection technique within wide-based
populations derived from mixtures of worldwide elite tropical germ plasm
complexes. Selection would be made in the areas for which improved
varieties are desired.
Improvement of Soil Fertility, Agronomic Practices, 11anagement and
Production Systems
20.       The most efficient cropping systems and agronomic practices for
maize, sorghum and millets would be determined for each of the different
ecological situations within the three regions. Sorghum and millets would
be promoted in the drier areas or for production in the drier part of the
year in multiple cropping systems in the more humid regions. Fertilizer
requirements and its most economical use in different rainfall regimes
and cropping systems would be more precisely determined in farmers
fields in collaboration with farmers. The soil fertility and cropping
systems specialists would closely interact with the breeders and orient
them to the kinds of varieties most indicated for the most efficient
cropping systems in the different environments.
21.       The national program would seek to strengthen the cooperative
breeding and seed production work underway in the Piracicaba-SUDENE project.
Since some good varieties have already been identified in this project,



ANNEX 5
Page 7
the national program would place major emphasis on the improvement of soil
fertility and agronomic practices and help determine the production systems
most indicated in the Northeast in close collaboration with the UEPAEs.
Furthermore, the national program would assist the UEPAEs in tying in their
research effort with the direct technical assistance support to farmers
and rural development services that are provided in the area in an attempt
to increase production and unit area yields at the farm level. Most of
the soil fertility research and much of the cropping systems work would
he done on farm fields in collaboration with farmers.
22.       Research work in the Northern (Amazonia) region would also be
focused mostly on the development of cropping systems and determination
of fertilization and agronomic practices for efficient production of maize
in the humid tropics. Early varieties of sorghum might be used in this
region in certain multiple production systems. Work would be done at
Belem, Altamira and Manaus. Basic breeding work would be concentrated in
the Agricultural Research Center in Belem. The UEPAEs at Altamira and
Manaus would serve mainly as testing and demonstration stations.
23.       In the Central-West, research in breeding and production nethodo-
logy in both maize and sorghum would be concentrated in Planaltina, in
collaboration with the Agricultural Research Center, and at Goiania
(UEPAE) in the region. A strong effort would be made to develop varieties
of both maize and sorghum adapted to the Campos Cerrados soil and rainfall
conditions and to determine how these might fit into efficient production
systems for this vast, potentially productive, underdeveloped region of
Brazil.
C. Organization and Management
24.       The national maize/sorghum research program team would be head-
quartered at Sete Lagoas. The station director would have overall respon-
sibility for administration of the headquarters station and would represent
EMBRAPA for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by
an advisory council 1/ and two associate directors, one for administrative
services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and
one for the implementation of the research program.
25.       The associate directors for research, as team leader/coordinators,
would have fuil responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members but
composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for example
of membership).



ANNEX 5
Page 8
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs)
and other cooperating institutions concerned with research on program
commodities and the application of new technology in maize/sorghum/
millet production would be achieved through contacts between the team
leaders and representatives for the appropriate research programs at
these stations. An annual workshop would be held at which current
activities would be evaluated and plans for the research program for the
comlng years woulu be iiLaUe.  Lnput from producers would bc anchnlved
through representation at such workshops, or field days, in the distinct
sub-areas of major production as may be necessary. As members of the
advisory council, the associate directors for research would provide
the necessary liaison between the council and producers (see also
Annex 17 for broad discussion of Organization and Management).

University Contract Research
27.        The more basic phases of supporting research needed would be
contracted out where possible to interested institutions capable of making
the desired studies.  For example, the national program would plan to

materials at the Instituto Agronomico at Campinas, the University of

of the national program.  Wherever desirable and feasible, the natlonal

D. Staffing
28.       The staff list at full program development of the National Maize
Program is given in Table 5. This includes 66 professionals and 31 admin-
istrative support staff to be assigned to the Sete Lagoas Center. In addi-
tion, 48 professional staff and 32 administrative support staff would be
available at the UEPAEs mentioned. The national maize/sorghum center would
have a director responsible primarily for the administrative aspects of
the center and two associate directors for research programs. They would
also act as technical program leader/coordinators, one for the maize team and
the other for the sorghum team. Leaders of each team would be responsible for
the planning, execution and harmonious operation of all phases of the research
program, whether directly executed by members of the national program, or
in coordination with individuals employed by other institutions. They would
be responsible for development and use of annual budgets as indicated in
paragraph 25 (see Table 5 for Basic Staffing Pattern).
E. Training and Fellowship Program
29.       Both leader/coordinators of the maize and sorghum teams have been
named, but additional staff will need to be trained as soon as possible. Plans
for academic upgrading of potential scientists are as follows: (a) a fellow-
ship program would provide for the training of five staff members to the Ph.D.
degree level and 34 to the M.Sc. level. In addition, funds would provide for
in-service training through short courses in appropriate subject areas for
18 participants.
30.       A training officer has been included in the staff list to plan,
direct, and execute the in-service training program. Many of the young men
in the in-service training program would be working as research assistants,
insofar as possible with the more experienced staff members. Candidates for
fellowships, especially for study toward the Masters degree, would be selected
from the in-service trainee group. Ph.D. candidates would be selected from
M.Sc. scientists who have demonstrated interest and special competence in the
work of the national program (see also Table 2).

F. Consultants
31.       A total of 9.5 man-years of consultancy services would be made
available for use at the National Research Center and assignments would be
made mainly in the areas of plant breeding, specialized services from the
International Research Centers and for program evaluation (see also Table 2).
C. Linkages with External Organizations
32.       The national program would solicit the collaboration of the Inter-
national Maize and Wheat Improvement Center (CIMMYT) in special projects,
where desirable and feasible. This might be in the development of basic
maize breeding materials for improved varieties for specific ecological
conditions. Collaboration of CIMMYT in the development of more nutritious
varieties for the Northeast might be especially valuable. Al-so, CIMMYT
could be helpful in the in-service training of personnel in breeding and
production methodologies. In studies of soil fertility, maintenance, and
agronomic practices in the Amazon area, the national team could benefit
greatly from the experiences of the International Institute of Tropical
Agriculture (IITA) in Ibadan, Nigeria.  Young men might be sent there for
training.  Linkages wl,h ,ha ter._at`coal Cropa Research I               for the
Semi-arid Tropics (ICRISAT), which has projects with sorghum and millet
in the arid zone, would be promoted in a similar fashion (see also Annex 17).

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR CASSAVA
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Cassava in Agriculture
Background
1.        Originating in Brazil, cassava is cultivated in all tropical
regions where edapho-climatic conditions are favorable. It is generally
found between the latitudes of 300 North and South, forming an ecological
zone called the "Cassava Belt." This region represents 46% of the world's
arable land and holds 47% of the world's population. The belt coincides
roughly with the geographic distribution of the major developing countries.
2.        In recent years, world cassava production has grown rapidly, ris-
ing from 90.5 million tons in 1968 to approximately 105.5 million tons in
1972, an increase of 16.6%. Brazil, Indonesia, Zaire, and Nigeria stand
out as the world's biggest producers, accounting for about 58% of the world's
cassava supply. Brazil alone accounts for 30% of that output. It is estimated
that cassava is the principal food in the tropics for approximately 200 million
people, constituting a basic source of carbohydrates. In view of the com-
bined effect of population and income growth, the demand for cassava in 1980
should be 33% above the 1970 figure. In 1970, cassava furnished 30% of the
calories consumed in Africa, 12% in Latin America, and 7% in the Far East. By
1980, these figures are expected to be, respectively, 37%, 11% and 6%, re-
spectively. While the relative importance of cassava for human consumption
may decrease as incomes increase, it is expected that consumption for animal
feed and industrial uses will more than offset this trend.
Production Performance
3.        Although Brazil is the principal cassava producer, international
trade is dominated by Thailand and Indonesia. Exports are to the highly
industrialized countries, which use these products as raw material in making
fiber, mixtures for balanced animal rations, and cereal substitutes for
barley and corn. Brazil places industrial by-products of cassava on the for-
eign market in the form of cassava flour, manioc meal, cassava starch. and
tapioca. The table below shows the declining tendency of Brazilian exports of
cassava and its by-products from 1969, when Brazilian exports reached a maximum.



AiNEX 6
Page 2
Manioc
Year   Cassava flour            Manioc 1Meal    Cassava Starch   Scrapings
------------------------- in metric tons  -----------------------
1968        754                    7,887             7,172
1969     46,660                    9,611            10,354           38,125
1970     25,549                    8,690                 8           24,272
1971     10,812                    2,168             7,557            9,069
1972        250                    2,055             4,298                1
Source:  CACEX, 1973.
4.        Most of the Brazilian cassava by-product exports come from the
states of Santa Catarina and Sao Paulo. The United States and Canada are
the principal buyers of manioc meal and starch, whereas Belgium, Luxembourg,
Uruguay, and the United States are big importers of edible flour. Cassava
scraping exports dropped from 38,000 tons in 1969 to 1 ton in 1972. West
Germany, the Netherlands, Belgium, and Luxembourg have been the biggest
importers of cassava by-products in recent years. The decline in Brazilian
exports would seem to be the resulL oL' greaLer  juiabLi    - p-O, pr-
marily as animal food, and also of the increasing use of manioc meal in
flour for bread making, which now constitutes 10% of such flour. Cassava
production is almost stationary and, hence, exportable surpluses continue
to decline. Cassava exports now account for only 0.5% of total exports.
Although all regions in Brazil produce some cassava, production is con-
centrated in the Northeast. In that region, cassava holds second place in
terms of output volume, exceeded only by sugarcane, and it holds sixth
place in terms of area cultivated.
Potentials and Constraints to Production
5.        In the period 1968 to 1972, cassava production increased at a rate
of only 1.06% per year, rising from 29,283,229 tons in 1968 to 29,829,919
tons in 1972. The main reasons for such a slight increase can be found in
the reduction of the cultivated area and the low level of technological inputs
devoted to its cultivation. Yields in Brazil are on the low side, approxi-
mately 13.5 tons per ha. The best land is used for more profitable crops
with better prospects for sale on foreign and domestic markets, and low in-
puts of technology and capital are used in cassava production.
6.        In 1973, the total area under cassava cultivation in Brazil was
2.1 million ha and production was 26.5 million tons. Based upon area cul-
tivated, the Northeast (50.15%) is the largest producer, followed by the
South (25.03%), the Southeast (13.85%), the North (5.87%), and the Central-
West (5.10%). Yields for all areas are fairly uniform, but those for the



ANNTEX 6
Page 3
Central-West, at 15.8 tons/ha, are slightly above the national average.
As mechanized crops, such as soybeans, become increasingly popular in the
South and Southeast, the opportunities for increased production of cassava
in the North must grow accordingly, particularly if the export position of
Brazil for cassava and its by-products is to be sustained. In addition,
there is already considerable evidence that increased yields (per unit area)
can be achieved in the Northeast. As an example, experimental results of
30 tons/ha have been recorded, and the current average yield in the Northeast
is well below half that (11.3 tons/ha). Also, the quantity of starch in
the fresh roots is low at 25 to 30%. Considering that cassava meal and
flour are staple foods for the farm population, it becomes important to de-
velop nationwide research in order to procure varieties with a higher
content of assimilable proteins and providing higher yields per unit area.
Demand Outlook for both Domestic and Export Markets
7.        Researching agricultural commodities and prices in Brazil, Brandt 1/
concluded that cassava demand elasticity is 0.23 for Brazil, whereas the food
demand elasticity in general is 0.80. Due to the low demand elasticity it
is to be expected that. as a result of the increase in income, the increase
in the consumption of cassava will gradually taper off as a natural corollary
to socio-economic advancement, since the population will switch to food
containing more proteins and less carbohydrates. Even so, it is estimated
that the annual production increase will have to be at least 4.1% per year
to meet domestic human consumption. Cassava holds first place in supplying
carbohydrates for the diet of the low income population group. In addition,
the farmer regards the cassava crop as a staple source for his own food
supply and for livestock raising. The average consumption (kg/yr per capita)
for Brazil in 1972 was calculated at 124.6 kg. Average per capita consumption
in the urban zone was 40 kg and in the rural zone 199.6 kg. According to
studies by the Getulio Vargas Foundation, the actual consumption of cassava
in 1970 came to 15,426,000 tons and, by 1980, it is expected that actual
consumption will be 23,151,000 tons (a 50% increase in 10 years). In the
Northeast, the per capita consumption has been estimated at 73.8 kg per year
in the rural areas and 32 kg per year in the urban areas.  Industrial uses
are discussed in paragraph 3.
B.   Current Status of Cassava Research
8.        Cassava, even in the Northeast and Central-West where 55% of pro-
duction is concentrated, has never received the attention of other crops
of comparable importance, on a cultivated area basis, such as corn, beans,
and cotton. The reason for this is, undoubtedly, the relatively low value
of the crop on a volume basis and the fact that, traditionally, it has major
1/   Source:  'Estudos de Pesquisa de Mercado e Precos Agricolas' by
Prof. Sergio Brandt (Universidade Federal de Viscosa), 1972.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
importance as a subsistence crop. The few research studies available on
cassava cultivation focus on cultivation procedures, spacing, planting
systems, fertility and comparisons among varieties. Results, however,
have not contributed greatly to increased output and productivity. The
level of technology employed by the growers is the most rudimentary possible,
and the varieties used are seldom subjected to selection or genetic improve-
ment. This lack of cooperation between grower and researcher has led to a
dissipation of the limited financial, material, and human resources involved
in cassava research.
9.        In the North (Amazonia), research programs have concentrated on
selection of new varieties, fertilizer application and cultivation practices.
In the Northeast, in addition to the above, there have been experiments in
plant protection, soil fertility and the technology of cassava processing.
In the Central-West, records indicate work on production systems only in the
state of Goias, but this obviously reflects the rather low importance the
crop has so far achieved in the Campos Cerrados area. In the Southeast,
breeding work on cassava has been done in the states of Rio de Janeiro and
Ilinas Gerais with yield comparisons on varieties, fertilizer trials and
production methods. From information available, there is no record of
significant research on cassava in the South, although, since 28% of national
production is concentrated there, it seems likely that some work has been
accomplished in the main producing states of Santa Catarina and Rio Grande
do Sul, each accounting for 3.2 million tons annually.
10.       It is not possible to identify research workers engaged exclusively
in cassava research, but, in the Northeast as an example, some 78 research
personnel at the graduate level are currently employed on stations where
cassava forms part of the research program.
II.  THE NTATIONAL CASSAVA RESEARCH PROGRALi
A. General Description
Agro-ecological Coverage
11.       Although cassava is an important crop now in three of the five
geographical regions of Brazil (Northeast, South and Southeast), it is likely
that, once specific soil problems are overcome, it may also gain major
importance in the Central-West, particularly in the states of iJato Grosso and
Goias, and in the North (Amazonia), where production is only 5.8% of the
national production at the present time. Based on 1973 production figures,
61% of the national production lies within the agro-ecological region in
which the national research program would concentrate. Given the importance
of the crop in the South, however, linkages to the experiment stations of
Pelotas and Passo Fundo would be maintained as part of the national research
network for the crop.



ANINEX 6
Page 5
Program Goals
12.       Research would be concentrated in two main areas:  (a) develop-
ment of methods to produce more cassava per unit area and identification of
areas for further expansion of the cultivated acreage, particularly in the
Central-West; and (b) development of new technology and improvement of
existing methods to increase the range of industrial uses, particularly in
the export market, as well as systems whereby storage life of raw cassava
(roots) can be lengthened to permit the use of mechanical harvesting tech-
niques. Annual increases of from 5 to 8% at the farm level are thought to
be necessary to maintain Brazil's current position in the export market.
Program Components
13.       In order to achieve the goals as outlined above, the project would
provide for the following:
(a) civil works at the National Commodity Center,Cruz das Almas
and at three UEPAEs--Manaus in the North (Amazonia) and
Bacabal and Quissama in the Northeast;
(b) research farm development at the above stations;
(c)  equi'v.enrt,    n 4 t1iire and trehicles for all stations mentioned
above;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental establishment costs of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
Improvements in Industrial Uses
14.       An attempt would be made to find new and better uses of cassava
as food, feed, starch and other industrialized products, thereby creating
a greater demand for the product and improving the income of the small



ATNEX 6
Page 6
farmers. Currently, there is considerable interest in the production
of methanol from cassava to be used, in the portion of 10%, as a gas-
oline additive.
Varietal Improvement
15.       The first step in the improvement of varieties and agronomic
practices would be to conduct a survey to determine the biological and
economic factors limiting cassava yields and its utilization. This work
would identify the research priorities for both breeding and cultural
practices. Close contact would be maintained with scientists at CIAT (the
International Center for Tropical Agriculture), where a germ plasm bank of
approximately 2,200 collections is maintained. These specimens are being
systematically screened for agronomic characteristics, particularly for
those influencing yield capacity. Brazilian scientists would collaborate
with CIAT scientists in the evaluation of exotic and local materials in
the different ecological situations in Brazil and identify materials for
breeding programs and/or immediate multiplication for conmercial production.
Where feasible and desirable, Brazilian breeders would also seek the co-
operation of CIAT scientists in the evaluation and use of breeding method-
ologies for combining desirable characteristics such as high harvest index
and leaf area index with disease and insect resistance, low cyanide content,
ease of harvesting, stability of performance, and other valuable attributes.
Also, Brazilian scientists would solicit the assistance of CIAT scientists
in introducing a method of rapidly propagating new clones released for
production.
Production Systems and Agronomic Practices
16.       Cropping systems and agronomic practices would vary according to
location, and much of the research required would have to be done at micro-
regional stations and in collaboration with farmers. Cropping systems for
each micro-region would be determined, and research in agronomic practices
would be designed to determine planting densities, stake size, kinds and
quantities of chemical fertilizer to be used, and effective control of weeds,
insects, and diseases.
i-arvest and Post-Harvest 11and_ing
17.       The need for new harvest techniques and simple post-harvest pro-
cessing becomes very apparent as farmers increase yields. Fresh cassava
spoils within a few days after harvest. Simple low-cost methods for on-
the-farm storage must be worked out if serious marketing problems are to
be avoided. Also, easier ways of harvesting the roots must be found. CIAT
scientists are already working on the problem and collaboration with them
would be sought for relevant studies.



ANNI-X 6
Paeg 7
Economic Researclh on Production Costs and Marketing
18.        Research in economics would be conducted Lo determnine the rost
of production under various sysLems and to identify and solve rnarketing
problems.  Studies would be developed Lo determine how the demand for
Lhe product in both domestic and foreign markets could be expanded.
C. Organization and Mariagement
19.       The nationaL cassava research program team would be headquartered
at Cruz das Almas, Bahia. The station director would have overall respons-
ibility for administraLion of Lhe headquarters station and would represent
EMBRAPA for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an
advisory council 1/ and two associate directors, one for administrative
services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and
one for Lhe implementation of the research program.
20.        The associaLe director for research as team leader/coordinat,r
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the progra!-
and would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluaLion. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAYs)
and other cooperating insLituLions concerned wiLh research on cassava anoi
the application of new technology in cassava production would be achieved
through contacts between the team leader and representaLives for the ap-
propriate research programs at these stations.  An annual workshop would
be held at which current activiLies would be evaluated and plans for the
research program for the coming years would be made.  Inputs from producers
would be obtained at such workshops or field days in the distinct sub--
areas of major production. As a member of the advisory council, the associaLe
director for research would provide the necessary liaison between the cocncil
and producers (see also Annex 17).
Research Facilities
21.       One hundred hectares of land are available at the research center
for the cassava program at Cruz das Almas.  In addition, adequate lae retcries
and field workshops for the multi-disciplinary team of researchers *;u'd
be available at this main station.  Facilities would include Laboratories
for plant physiology, plant pathology, entomology, soils and seed work, plus
Lhe necessary equipment and furnishings. Facilities at the satelliLt stations
or UEPAEs would include land allocations as follows:  Bacabal, 20 hna; "anaus,
10 ha; and Quissama, 5 ha. Field laboratories and crop storage fac.' LtLes,
plus additional field equipment for demonstration plot establishment, both
at the station and on farmers' fields, would also be provided.
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine r-embers, but
composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for example
of membership)



ANNEX 6
Page 8
Research Station Network and Collaborating Institutions
22.       The national team would collaborate with the UEPAEs and other
institutions as necessary to identify and solve the biological and economic
production constraints at the farm level.  More specifically, it would
collaborate with the UEPAEs in such activities as testing of new clones;
development of appropriate production systems and agronomic practices; solu-
tion of practical problems in harvesting, temporary storage and marketing;
and development and adoption of improved technological packages in collabor-
ation with farmers. In the initial stages, although certain activities might
be promoted nationwide, efforts would be concentrated mainly at the central
headquarters and at the stations located near Bacabal and Quissama in Lhe
Northeast, Manaus in the North, and Planaltina in the Central-West (Campos
Cerrados). In addition, four UEPAE stations in the Southeast and North would
also conduct research on cassava. The full research station network is shown
in Table 4.
Contract Research
23.       The national team would attempt to promote the necessary research
in food technology to find better ways to use cassava for food in collabor-
ation with the Food Technology Institute at Campinas or some highly qualified
university, if so indicated, on a contract basis. Research leading to new
industrialized products might also to worked out with certain food or feed
processing industries on a cooperative special contract basis. Necessary
feeding trials in the evaluation of cassava in various animal rations is an-
other area for university collaboration, although some research along this
line could be done on a cooperative basis with the national beef and dairy
cattle programs and national swine program (see also Table 4 for list of
collaborating institutions).
D. Staffing
24.       The staff at full development would comprise 40 professionals and
23 administrative support staff for the core research program (National
Commodity Center) and an additional 26 professional staff and 21 administrative
support staff at the three satellite stations. ITL may be necessary to add an
agricultural engineer, plus junior and supporting staff in this field, for work
in development of improved processing techniques. There are very few exper-
ienced cassava workers in Brazil now, perhaps no more than 10% of the number
required, and staff would have to be developed mostly on an in-service train-
ing basis (see Table 5).



ANNEX 6
Page 9
E. Training and Fellowship Program
25.        The project would provide for the academic upgrading of 26 young
professionals for the cassava reearch program.  Twenty-two of thlese would
be trained to the M.Sc. level, four to the Ph.D. level, and an additional
15 would participate in special short courses related to the program. De-
tails of this training component of the project are provided in Table 2.
A training officer has been included in the staff list to plan, direct and
execute the in-service training program. Insofar as possible, the young
staff members in the in-service training program would work as research
assistants with the more experienced personnel. Individuals to take the
short courses would be selected from this group. Candidates for the M.Sc.
degree would be selected from the in-service trainee group. Ph.D. candidates
would be selected from the M.Sc. scientists that have demonstrated interest
and special competence in the national program.
F. Consultants
26.        In view of the severe shortage of trained professionals for work on
the cassava program at present, a total of seven man-years of long-term con-
sultant service and 5.5 man-years of short-term consultant services would be
provided under the project. These specialists would provide expertise in
the areas of plant breeding, plant pathology, production agronomy, and mech-
anical engineering for food processing and technology (see Table 2).
C. Linkages to International Centers
27.       As has been previously mentioned, the International Center for
Tropical Agriculture (CIAT), Cali, Colombia has a very active program for
cassava research which has produced a great deal of new technology for the
crop during recent years and collaboration with it would be sought whenever
possible.  Mluch of this would be appropriate for simple technology trans-
fer, since conditions in the main growing areas of Brazil are similar to
those found in the Cauca Valley of Colombia. in addition, the storage and
food technology developed in Colombia can readily be adapted to the Brazilian
experience.

five-year period, would be approximately US$10.1 million. The estimated costs
are summarized in Table 1.

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR COTTON
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Cotton in Agriculture
World Production and Demand
1.        Cotton, a crop which is widely distributed throughout the world,
is produced in about 70 countries, most of which are developing countries;
in 1972-1973, it is estimated that approximately 37.4 million ha was
cultivated on all continents. The 1972-1973 world output was close to 13
million tons (59.3 million bales); the United States produced 2.9 million
tons (13.7 million bales), followed by the Soviet Union (2.4 million tons),
Mainland China (1.4 million tons), India (1.1 million tons), Pakistan
(702,000 tons), and Brazil (642,000 tons).
2.        A study of the cons-umption data for .he pas, 20 yas shows tho
the development of world cotton output during that time was characterized
by cycles of overproduction and underproduction. These cycles do not show
uniform duration and vary as a function of the climatic conditions which
may or may not be favorable to the crop. Even so, between the crop years
1963/64 and 1973/74, the world's cotton supply increased by approximately
18.0 million bales, corresponding to an average annual increase on the
order of 2.16%. Parallel to this, the demand increased by 18.4 million
bales in absolute terms, approximately 2.4%. Between 1972 and 1974 a
sustained demand growth of 2.0% per year was experienced. This means an
additional demand of 245,000 tons per year at present levels, or 75 million
bales (16.4 tons), by 1980.
Cotton Production in Brazil
3.        Two main types of cotton are cultivated in Brazil:  annual cotton,
grown mainly in the Southeast and South (49% of national production) and
the perennial cottons cultivated mainly in the project area of the Northeast
and Campos Cerrados (51% of national production). In the project area, the
distribution of the two types is as follows: annual 25%, and perennial, 75%.
The annual cottons of the Northeast are medium-staple, high yielding varieties.
Perennial cotton is further divided into the long-staple, low yielding and
the short-staple, high yielding types. The regional distribution of these
varieties is determined by soil and climatic conditions and, to some degree,
by tradition.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
4.        In areas with low rainfall or poor rainfall distribution, and
shallow soil, only the xerophytic or drought-resistant long-staple perennial
cotton can sustain itself and produce, even under severe drought conditions,
the only meager income that farmers can expect in these poor areas. The
area extends from the southern part of Ceara into Pernambuco and parts of
Bahia. For these reasons, the long-staple perennial cottons are of vital
importance to farmers in the Northeast of Brazil. In areas where rainfall
pattern and soil fertility permit, the other two types of cotton are grown,
often side by side with long-staple perennial cottons. This creates con-
siderable difficulties for any sort of seed multiplication, where isolation
of the three types is of extreme importance. Intercrossing of the three
types and mechanical mixing of seed of various cotton types and origins
has brought about either considerable reductions in yield or less fiber-
length and strength, the latter giving the long-staple perennial cottons the
advantage in the fiber marketplace.
5.        Production of annual cotton in the South and Southeast conLrasts
sharply with the above in that cropping systems are more highly mechanized,
and both use credit and technical inputs - such as improved seed and
fertilizer - on a large scale. As a result, yields are much higher, and
production costs relatively lower. In contrast, the cultivation methods
used in the Northeast are correspondingly more labor-intensive. These broad
differences in cultivation methods, with their social impact on the labor
force, are so distinct as to warrant an a ceptabla d-far_ --atn cf *h^
national research effort for the South, as opposed to the Northeast/Central-
West area in this case.
Potentials and Constraints to Productions
6.        During the 10-year period (1964-73) the relative importance of
Brazil's cotton exports has dropped by 50%, from 7.62% to 3.75% of total
exports, although actual value of exports increased by 134% during the
same period, and it is significant that, in rather unstable world markets,
the volume of Brazilian exports has been well maintained. Cotton, there-
fore, plays an important role in the export economy of the country, and
the main features of future strategy to sustain this position are as
follows: (a) further improvement of cultivation techniques on large scale
production of annual cottons in the South and Souitheast, as a separate
program, and (b) intensive research on the cotton varieties used in the
Northeast and Central-West to improve both quality and yield and to pro-
vide a more uniform, high quality product; (c) in the case of (b) above,
the aim would be to continue small scale intensive production by farmers
to preserve the labor-intensive requirements of the crop.   The main
constraints to production of high quality cotton in the Northeast and
Central-West have been related to cultivation procedures, particularly the
lack of certified seed and quality control standards for type and grade
of cotton being produced. Both these aspects influence strongly the
potential returns to crop production.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Demand Outlook for Cotton
7.        The Brazilian Institute for Economic and Social Planning (IPEA)
has estimated an increased demand for cotton in the Northeast of 6.5%
annually for the period 1973-1980. liost of this increase would be for
domestic use; 80% of total crop of 467,300 tons of baled cotton (ginned)
as opposed to 69% of the crop in 1971. Exports of cotton from the North-
east are not expected to rise above 20.0% (91,100 tons) of crop output from
the region in 1980. This not only reflects a worldwide tendency to trade
in finished or semi-finished cotton products, as opposed to raw cotton,
but also reflects Government policy to increae the potential for manufacture
of cotton yarn in the Central-West and Northeast. Currently, 30% of world
cotton production moves on the international market as raw cotton. Overall,
world demand for cotton, which has declined since 1973, is expected to re-
turn to 1973 levels by 1976, and show a slight increase (1.75% annually)
after that date. Domestic demand is expected to increase by 4.5% per year
in the region, and a target of production increases of 6% per year has been
set for the 1976-80 period, which appears justified, given the strong demand
for high quality long-staple cottons.
B. Current Status of Research
8.        Current research in the Northeast and Central-West is primarily
directed at perennial cotton cultivation. The principal institutions which
concentrate on cotton research are the following: Superintendency for
Development of the Northeast (SUDENE), Federal University of Ceara, San
I'iguel Cotton Co. Inc. (private enterprise), State Secretariats of Agri-
culture, Industry and Commerce Secretariat of Paraiba (SAIC), Pernambuco
Agronomic Research Institute (IPA), Institute for Agricultural Research in
the Northeast (IPEANE) and Institute for Agricultural Research in the East
(IPEAL). It must be emphasized that the technical personnel of these in-
stitutes do not work on cotton research exclusively, but are also concerned
with teaching activities (Federal University of Ceara) and with the agri-
cultural policies of the states (Agriculture Secretariats). Only the tech-
nicians of IPEANE, the IPEAL and the IPA are concerned solely with research.
9.        These institutions had a total of 68 experiments in progress during
1974. Technical personnel, totalling 292, were working at 21 different sites.
Experiments were concentrated in the areas of: genetic improvement, plant
physiology, plant protection, soil fertility and irrigation. The purpose of
these experiments was to increase yields through adaptation of varieties to
local conditions and provide resistance to insects and diseases. The per-
centage, quality and uniformity of fiber produced was the basis for selection
of new varieties in these experiments, which dealt with both annual and
perennial cottons for the Northeast.
10.       There have been two main problems which have severely hampered
researuci  ei -iurc5 su  ar.  Iiie rirst concerns une LacK o0  coordination and
orientation, as reflected in the absence of priorities, and in the difficulty



ANNEX 7
Page 4
of undertaking more large-scale projects. The other difficulty stems from
the fact that research, for many years, was aimed mainly at fiber quality,
and relative productivity was neglected.
11.       Genealogical selections have been in the region since 1930 in an
effort to purify the local perennial cotton populations. The research
emphasis was for a long time placed on getting satisfactory fiber length.
Three varieties sprang from the work done so far: MF-1 (San Miguel), APA
(IPA) and 9193 (IPEANE, long-staple cotton experiment). All of these were
less productive than the local populations during the first year of cultiv-
ation, although they made up for this first year inferiority during the
second and third years. The APA variety is the one that presents the great-
est fiber length and the greatest strength.
12.       Cotton research in the South and Southeast has been concentrated
on annual cotton, mainly in the states of Parana and Sao Paulo, respectively.
In Parana, research is carried out mainly by Instituto de Pesquisas Meridional
(IPEAME) and, in Sao Paulo, by the Agricultural Institute of Campinas (IAC).
Since the greater part of cotton cultivation in these southern areas is
already mechanized, research tended to stress uniformity of varieties, through
seed selection and production of selected seed to maintain a uniform product.
Research SLation NeLwurs
13.       Previous to the formation of EMBRAPA on April 26, 1972, all Federal
Research Stations were operated under DNPEA (National Agricultural Research
Department). In the cotton growing areas of the Northeast, South and South-
east, a total of 52 institutes, which controlled 107 field research stations,
had been established. Of these institutes, 31 were federal, 6 were state
owned, 5 were associated with universities, and 10 were privately owned.
Cooperation between these various groups was minimal, and only two IPEANE
(federal) and IPA (state) institutions in the Northeast provided some re-
sults from research on perennial cotton in the Northeast. Two more -- IAC
(state) in the Southeast (Sao Paulo) and IPEAME (Instituto de Pesquisas
Agropecuarias Meridional - federal) in the South (Parana) - contributed
greatly to the development of annual cotton in this region. Research has
stimulated the relatively vigorous nature of cotton cultivation in the South
and Southeast, as opposed to the poor and erratic commercial performance of
perennial cotton in the Northeat. Doubtless, this difference in economic
importance is related to the comparatively low level of technology and mechan-
ization employed in cotton cultivation in the Northeast and Central-West, as
well as to the climatic and soil conditions predominant in these areas.
Research Personnel
14.       It is not possible to determine precisely how nmany research personnel
are engaged in cotton research only, but an estimate for 1972 placed the
figure at 1,335 research personnel working on cotton in the states of Parana



ANNEX 7
Page 5
and Sao Paulo, and only 98 in the cotton growing states of the Northeast and
Central-West.
Research Achievements
15.       During the period 1960-70, the area in cotton (annual and perennial
combined) rose from 2,930,361 ha to 4,298,573 ha. Yields per hectare, however,
dropped from 549 kg/ha in 1960 to 455 kg/ha in 1970, or a drop of 17.2%.
Records 1/ of perennial cotton for 1973 show plantings of 2,347,105 ha for a
total production of 548,596 tons with an average yield of only 234 kg/ha. In
contrast, in the same year, 2,031,574 ha of annual cotton was grown for a total
yield of 1,707,863 tons with an average yield of 841 kg/ha. While the value of
perennial cotton (Cr$ 1,320,660,000) was approximately half that received for
the annual cotton crop (Cr$ 2,567,002,000), the cultivated area for the latter
was only 86.5% of the former. This would indicate that the application of
research results to the production process had been significant on annual cotton
production in the South and Southeast. Meanwhile, average kg/ha production
has continued to decline in the Northeast, particularly for perennial cotton.
16.       Along with genetic improvement on perennial cotton, agronomic
research is being developed on intercropping, planting density, pruning,
longevity, and use of insecticides. Studies on the use of fertilizer were
started recently, and the School of Agronomy of the Federal University of
Ceara already has somp prtl im1niarv hut fnv--l        S e  eslts.
17.       Preliminary data from SUDENE and the University of Ceara shows that
128 experiments were conducted in 1973; 43 of these experiments were made
with annual cotton and 85 with perennial cotton. Half of the total involved
fertilizer work; 25% pertained to genetic improvement and the other 25%
included experiments in crop cultivation practices and plant health.
18.       Starting in 1972, the National Association for Fertilizer Dis-
semination (AINDA) has been doing research work and has been setting up de-
monstration fields with the 12 main agricultural products of the region,
emphasizing the application of fertilizer. In 1973, 79 fertilization ex-
periments were conducted with cotton in various micro-regions of the nine
states in the Northeast. The financial analysis of these activities revealed
great variability, with value/cost indexes between 1.3 and 6.6 for the
experiments conducted.
1/   Source:  Annual Statistics for Brazil (IBGE), 1974.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
II. THE NATIONAL COTTON RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR THE NORTHEAST
A. General Description
19.        Cotton production is important mainly in the Northeast of the
project area where 50% of national cotton production occurs, based on value
of crop. Both annual and perennial cottons are grown in an area of approxi-
mately 3.2 million ha, representing 69% of the area under cotton in Brazil.
Yields for perennial cotton, the main type produced in the Northeast, are
only 38% of the national average.
20.       Agro-ecologically, the area may be divided into three main regions;
the narrow littoral belt, starting in the state of Paraiba and continuing
south to the extreme south of the state of Bahia. It represents 8.2% of the
land area of the Northeast and covers 126,000 km2. Little or no cotton is
cultivated in this area, known as the humid coastal area or "zona da mata."
The second region of major importance to agriculture is the "agreste," or
semi-arid region. This area, which lies inland and to the west of the littoral
is not, however, a continuous unit. The agreste covers 170,000 km2, and lies
in the states of Bahia, Piaui, Rio Grande do Norte, Pernambuco and Alagoas. It
is in this area that annual cotton, representing 25% of area cotton production,
is grown. The remaining 75z is produced in the semi-arid ("sertao") regiua,
which covers a broad belt running northwest to southeast in the states of
Ceara, Piauo, Rio Grande do Norte, Paraiba, Pernambuco and Bahia. Total area
of the "sertao" is 741,443 km2 and represents 47.9% of the land area of the
Northeast region. Perennial cotton, however, is produced principally in
the states of Ceara (47%), Rio Grande do Norte (18%), Paraiba (18%), and
Pernambuco (9%).
Research Program Goals
21.       The main objective of research on the two types of cotton (annual
and perennial) would basically be to increase production per unit area, and
also to introduce cotton as a possible crop in the farming systems of pro-
duction units over a wider range in the agro-ecological area, where suitable
conditions for its cultivation are to be found. Target figures (1980) for
increased unit area yields would be 320 kg/ha for perennial cotton, repre-
senting a 13% increase from the current 283 kg/ha level, and 1,200 kg/ha
for annual cotton, or an increase of 20% from current levels of 1,000 kg/ha.
Based on a national production figure of 3.4 million tons of cotton, in
1980, the portion for the project area (Northeast and Central-West) would be
1.7 million tons. Of this total, 85% of the area (3,200,000 ha) would be in
perennial cotton producing 1,024,000 tons, and 15% (570,000 ha) would be in
annual cotton producing 684,000 tons. Included in research program goals



ANNEX 7
Page 7
would be the selection and adaptation of suitable soil types for annual
cotton, particularly in the Central-West region, which becomes a possible
growing area for the crop as acreage moves out of annual cotton and into
soybeans in the South and Southeast.
Program Components
22.       In order to accomplish the above objectives, the project would pro-
vide for the following:
(a) civil works at the research stations of Campina Grande, Bacabal,
Irece, Cruzeta, Alagoinha, and Barreiras to provide the necessary
laboratories and research facilities;
(b) research farm development at the above stations;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at all stations
mentioned above;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental etablishment costs of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
The Research Program
23.       The research program would be aimed at the improvement of varieties,
soil fertility and agronomic practices, and cropping systems as follows: core
research would be done at the National Research Center for Cotton at Campina
Grande, which would be the headquarters for both annual and perennial cotton
research. Substations under the core program would be located at Cruzeta for
perennial cotton and at Irece for the annual varieties.
24.       The Agricultural Research Center (ARC) for the Northeast at Petrolina-
Juazeiro would provide a base for production of stock seed under irrigation
to permit the necessary isolation for production of pure seed. Cotton would



ANNEX 7
Page 8
also be one of the crops chosen for testing in farming systems and in demon-
stration plots at the UEPAEs of Bacabal, Alagoinha and Barreiras. The core
research program at the National Research Center for Cotton would have the
following components.
Varietal Improvement
25.       Work on varietal improvement would comprise the collection and
screening of commercial varieties, currently available both in Brazil and
abroad, when similar conditions for cultivation are known to exist. Proper
cataloging and preservation of well identified genetic strains would require
a germ plasm bank to be established at the National Cotton Research Center
at Campina Grande. This collection would form the basis for improvement of
varieties to meet the requirements of cotton to be grown in the project area.
Plant Health Protection
26.       Studies would be undertaken on the integrated control of pests,
diseases, and weeds harmful to perennial and annual cotton in different pro-
duction systems, and a survey would be conducted on the frequency of principal
pests, and of diseases affecting both perennial and annual cotton. Research
would also investigate the resistance of perennial and annual cotton to
diseases and pesLs.
Economic Studies
27.       Potential domestic and foreign sales of medium and long-fiber cotton
would be reviewed. Costs of producing perennial and annual cotton in the semi-
arid region under the varying soil and climatic conditions to be found in the
project area plus influences of size of production unit and availability of
inputs such as capital (credit) machinery and technology available to the farmer
at all levels would be studied.
Agronomic Practices
28.       These would include land preparation, planting dates, spacing ex-
perim.ents, use of fertilizers and herbicides, plant protection techniques,
varying levels of mechanization to provide for interests of farmers at all
levels of inputs, i.e., land, capital, mechanization and labor.
Cropping Systems
29.       On the basis of data on economic and agroclimatic conditions in the
project area - much of which would be provided by the substations and UEPAEs
through contact with growers, cooperatives and marketing associations - it
should be possible to devise associations of three to four crops, to provide



ANNEX 7
Page 9
maximum yields under the varying conditions found in the project
area. Depending somewhat on relative values for each crop, combin-
ations of cotton, sorghum, beans, cassava with sheep and goats are
likely to find a place in cropping systems research for the area.
C. Organization and Management
30.       The station director would have overall responsibility for
administration of the headquarters station, and would represent EMBRAPA
for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an ad-
visory council 1/ and two associate directors, one for administrative
services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer,
and one for the implementation of the research program. The Associate-
Director of the National Commodity Center for Cotton, located at Campina
Grande, would be program leader/coordinator for the research program in
the Northeast. The program for annual cotton in the South and Southeast
would still be coordinated from the state of Sao Paulo. Even so, the
program leader from Campina Grande would maintain close liaison with the
Sao Paulo station in matters relating to the cultivation of annual cotton
in the project area under the program. The program leader/coordinator
wouia rorm Lne researcn program Leam fLoin Lthe leaders in cotton research
at the other five research stations (UEPAEs) participating in the cotton
program for the region, and this group would be responsible for elaboration
of the research program. The research network for annual and perennial
cotton would comprise 11 stations within the project area (see Table 4 for
details).
Research Facilities
31.       These would be available at the following six stations:  Campina
Grande (headquarters), 300 ha; Bacabal, 10 ha; Cruzeta, 5 ha; Alagoinha, 3 ha;
Itapetinga, 5 ha; and Barreiras, 5 ha. The project would provide adequate
laboratory facilities for plant breeding, disease control, agronomic studies
and fiber analysis at the National Commodity Center. In addition, the
satellite stations would be equipped with adequate field laboratories to
carry out the adaptive research necessary and provide demonstration plots
on farmers' fields.
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members,
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
an example of membership).



ANNEX 7
Page 10
Collaborating Institutions
32.       The Institute of Cotton and Oil Seeds (INFAOL), using financial
incentives from federal, state and private sctors, encourages cotton
cultivation, particularly in the Northeast, putting most of its efforts
on price incentives and marketing. The National Research Program would
collaborate with this institute on studies of economic returns to pro-
duction and on marketing. Four other research and educational institutes
in the project area also contribute to cotton research (see Table 4).
University Contract Research
33.       It is proposed that when research problems of a specific nature
occur, even those that may necessitate some long-range basic research
program, it would be advisable to contract the work of solving them to select
universities in the region which have demonstrated an interest in agriculture
in general and cotton cultivation in particular. These problems generally
involve soil science, plant physiology, microbiology, biochemistry or plant
anatomy, but may also include specific studies in the field of agricultural
economics as an adjunct to farming systems research (see also Annex 17).
D. Staffing
34.       The program, at full development, would require a total of 65
professional staff and 31 support staff at the National Commodity Center.
An additional 32 professional staff and 27 support staff would be engaged
in cotton research at the five state research centers (UEPAEs) listed in
Table 4. Currently, only 10% of the personnel required are available so that
an ambitious program of staff training would be required (see Table 5).
E. Training and Fellowship Program
35.       A Lraining officer has been included in the staff list at the com-
modity program center to plan and execute in-service training and to assist
in the selection of appropriate candidates for the fellowship program. This
program calls for the training of five staff members to the Ph.D. level and
a further 34 for the M.Sc. degree. Selection of the candidates will depend
upon the areas of specialization required to achieve research program goals.
In addition, in-service short course training would be provided for 15 staff
participants (see Table 2).



ANNEX 7
Page 11
F. Consultants
36.       It is evident that experienced staff in the areas of plant breed-
ing, production agronomy, entomology and mechanical engineering would not be
available during the first four years of project execution. For this reason,
five man-years of long-term and 5.5 man-years of short-term consultant ser-
vices would be provided by the project, particularly in the areas of plant
breeding, entomology and mechanization (see Table 2).
G. Linkages to International Centers
37.       Cotton is not among the commodities currently being given specific
attention at any of the eight established International Research Centers.
However, the International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-arid Tropics
(ICRISAT) is broadly engaged in developing farming system techniques in agro-
ecological conditions, such as are found in Northeast Brazil and particularly
in those areas devoted to perennial cotton cultivation. For this reason,
links to ICRISAT - to provide existing technology related to farming systems
in which cotton was a crop - would warrant consideration as soon as the re-
search program is sufficiently advanced in this area. Some commercial and
EraCe organizaLions hdvinfg capabiliLy ai                   -S a- .---; -        t
in the USA and Europe and they might be able to provide short-term assistance
on specific problems within the program of research.

five-year period would be approximately US$15.8 million.   The estimated
