BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAII FOR RUBBER
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Rubber in Agriculture
Production Performance
1.        Rubber is quite unique from the other eight commodity programs
being supported under the project because only a very small portion of local
consumption, approximately 3% per year, is provided from Brazilian cultivated
natural (plantation) rubber. The remaining 97% of natural rubber consumed
in Brazil is either obtained from extractive tapping of wild forest trees
in Amazonia (46%) or from imports (51%). In addition to natural rubber,
synthetic rubber, both of Brazilian manufacture and imported, is used. The
balance of the demand for manufacturing is met by using regenerated rubber.
The relati4e importance of plantation grown natura lruber  an  that ;r2vided
by the extractive source industry, manufacture of synthetic and imports is
shown below:
Rubber Consumption by Source and Type 1973
Quantity in Tons   % by    % of
Item            Source                      Dry Rubber       Type   Total
Natural    Cultivated plantation              1,658           3.2    0.73
"1       Extracted from forest             23,402          45.8   10.25
it       Imported                          26,096          51.0   11.43
Synthetic  Brazilian manufacture            125,620          84.0   55.00
if       Imported                          23,922          16.0   10.47
Regenerated  Brazilian                         27,685         100.0   12.12
228,383           --   100.00
Source: Anuario Estatistico, Mercado Nacional
Superintendencia da Borracha, Ano 7,
Brasil, December 1973.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
2.        From the above, it will be seen that cultivated rubber currently
plays only a very minor role in supplying consumer requirements. This posi-
tion will further deteriorate, as imports are expected to reach 40,000 tons
by 1975 and 80,000 tons by 1980. The lack of a viable plantation industry
(only four commercial sized units exist at present) further hampers the
transfer of technology which may be developed under the research program.
This is further discussed under special program objectives (para 15).
Potentials and Constraints to Production
3.        Since the Amazon basin is the home of the natural rubber tree
(Hevea spp) and there is a wide range of native species of Hevea which
produce natural rubber (latex) of commercial quality, the potential for
commercial rubber production would appear, at first glance, to be enormous.
However, the South American leaf blight (Microcyclus ulei) has effectively
nullified repeated attempts to establish commercial plantations such as have
been developed in the Far East where selections of high yielding Hevea bra-
siliensis clones from the Amazon are used but where the debilitating South
American leaf blight does not occur. To this day the presence of the leaf
blight disease remains the major constraint to plantation-type rubber produc-
tion in Amazonia. The only reason that an extractive, as opposed to a
plantation-type, industry has been able to survive is the relative resis-
tance of manv low yielding spoc;es wkich. q- sonttered   reh           t t - forest,
thus avoiding any potential buildup of the disease, as would occur in
formal plantings. In addition to low yields per tree and per unit area,
however, the tapping methods used produce rubber of low quality when com-
pared to that produced under plantation conditions. The major constraint
to production, therefore, remains closely related to the future potertial
for development of a successful plantation-type industry, as opposed to
haphazard extractive tapping of native trees. The future of the plantation
industry, as we have seen, does, in turn, depend greatly upon the effective
control of the leaf blight disease.
Demand Outlook
4.        Brazil currently (1973) consumes 51,156 tons of natural rubber
yearly but only 49% (25,060 tons) of this was produced in Brazil. Data
supplied by the Superintendencia da Borracha (SUDHEVEA) indicates that, by
1980, the portion supplied by native rubber extraction would meet only 32%
of total requirements (80,000 tons). Even to retain the current position,
therefore, Brazil would need to increase natural rubber production to
39,200 tons or by 14,140 tons, a 56% increase over the seven-year period.
Demand estimates for rubber consumption are unusually difficult to define
in Brazil, as they are closely related to industrial or economic development



ANNEX 8
Page 3
in general. However, the fact that Brazil only uses 2.45 kg of rubber per
capita per year indicates that there is considerable opportunity for upward
adjustment of the figures quoted. 1/
B. Present Status of Rubber Research
5.        The three Government sponsored agencies which have been responsible
for rubber research in the past are the Executive Commission for the Economic
Recuperation of the Cacao Industry (CEPLAC), the Instituto de Pesquisas
Agropecuarias do Leste (IPEAL), and the Instituto de Pesquisas Agropecuarias
do Norte (IPEAN). Their work has been sponsored, and partially funded,
through SUDHEVEA. In addition, the three commercial rubber firms still active
in rubber production in Brazil --- Firestone Plantations at Itubera, Goodyear
Marathon Plantation, and Pirelli, Guama Agro-industrial with the Taiboca
Plantation at Marituba --- have all contributed to research on a commercial
basis and have provided considerable cooperation with Government research
programs.
6.        The CEPLAC and IPEAL research programs have concentrated on (a) soil
and plant nutrition studies; (b) plant physiology of the producing rubber
tree; (c) plant pathology, with emphasis on South American leaf blight control,
and screening or disease-resistant clones; (d) entomology with empnasis on
chemical and biological control of pests, mainly the moth (Erinnyis ello);
(e) selection and improvement of high yielding disease-resistant clones; and
(f) economic studies on production from native and cultivated trees. Currently,
there are 187 clones registered in the clonal garden collections at CEPLAC/
IPEAL.
7.        The major constraints to achieving a significant advance in the
industry through research remain, as they have been for the past 50 years,
since the Ford Motor Company established plantations at Fordlandia and Belterra,
the need to overcome losses due to the ravages of the South American leaf
blight disease. All attempts since that time to obtain adequate control of
the disease, economically, and to allow for a reasonable return on investment
in plantation rubber enterprises have failed. Only limited success has been
achieved in i-dentifying highly resistant clones with acceptable yields and
the more recent attempts to obtain adequate control of the disease through
use of fungicides have also proved economically unfeasible. Attention is now
being focused on the application of systemic fungicides to reduce costs, which
are always high in the case of tree crops such as rubber, where the foliage
canopy must be protected.
1/   Comparative figures are 1.5 for Argentina, 5.5 for Canada, 6.5 for
Europe, and 9 kg for the USA.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
II. THE NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR RUBBER
A. General Description
8.        The main thrust of the program would initially be to review
the current status of research land, and, based on the findings, formulate
the national program designed to achieve national goals. In the short term,
consideration must be given to the possibility of maintaining and eventually
improving the output and quality of the rubber being harvested from native
trees (extractive industry). This could readily be achieved through exist-
ing technology, namely (a) use of stimulants to promote latex flow, (b) control
of tapping methods to prolong productive life of selected forest trees, and
(c) improvement of methods and equipment used for handling and processing
of the latex produced by establishing acceptable grades within the extrac-
tive industry.
9.        In the long term, the basic goal would be to develop relatively
disease-free, high yielding clones adaptable to plantation type culture
to ensure the strong foundation necessary for a viable rubber industry.
Considerable advances have already been made in this area but the sense of
urgency necessary to achieve a significant breakthrough is stijl  iaclking.
The basic economics of a viable plantation rubber industry for the vast
potential of Amazonia must be determined at an early stage. The major con-
straints, so far, have been those related to the rather long development
period necessary for the establishment of plantations, i.e., six to seven
years before initial returns accrue to the investment, and it is believed
that a viable plantation industry cannot be achieved until these financial
difficulties covering the development period are resolved by means of long-
term credit or other fiscal incentives provided by the Government, possibly
through the SUDHEVEA agency.
10.       Since demonstration and field testing of research results are
essential steps in any research program of this nature, it is also true that
investment in research on rubber cannot be warranted unless these necessary
incentives to promote a viable plantation industry become an effective com.-
plement to the research effort.
Agro-ecological Coverage
11.       Ideal climatic conditions for rubber cultivation are to be found
in the wet tropics of Amazonia, particularly in the states of Amazonia and
Para, except in the extreme north, and in the Federal Territories of Amapa
and Rondonia, and extending as far south as Cuiaba in Northern Mato Grosso.
Although rainfall is in excess of 2,200 mm in this general area, the best
conditions for rubber cultivation are to be found where a distinct dry season



ANNEX 8
Page 5
of three to four months occurs. For this reason, the area around Belem,
which has no distinct dry season, is less favorable to rubber cultivation
because of heavy incidence of the South American leaf blight. The area
presents a very wide range of soil types, predominantly with only low
to medium fertility; soils favoring rubber culture are normally yellow
latosols and red-yellow podzolic soils. More important is the physical
condition, permeability and depth of soil, since rubber is deep rooting and
also requires proper drainage. For this reason, the largest concentration
of natural rubber is to be found in the drainage basins of the headwaters
of the rivers in the Amazon basin where drainage is adequate.
12.       The other, much more limited, area for rubber cultivation is in
southern Bahia state in the Ileus, Itabuna region. Here an area of approxi-
2
mately 20,000 km , consisting of red to yellow oxisols of sedimentary origin
and lying on well-drained table lands at approximately 600 m elevation,
appears to be adequate for rubber cultivation on a commercial scale. Geo-
graphically at 14.50S, it lies outside the recommended latitudes of 100 north
or south of the equator and this may explain the slightly slower growth noted
for rubber in this region. Rainfall tends to be low, at 1,500 to 2,000 mm
annually, with a distinct four-month dry season. The latter, however, may
well be the reason for low incidence of disease in the area. Further details
on these agro-ecological areas are given in Annexes 12 and 14.
Program Goals
13.       Production of 1,500 kg/ha of dry rubber would be considered viable
as a commercial enterprise. However, the criteria of rubber production
(latex) per tree, as opposed to unit area, must be used as an early guide in
the selection of suitable high yielding clones for the research program, and
the first priority must be to find a sufficiently high degree of resistance
to, or economical control of, the South American leaf blight disease. Until
this is accomplished, and with a fairly high degree of success, there seems
little possibility of establishing a viable rubber industry based on planta-
tion culture. Both actual and projected demands for domestic use, even
given a wide margin for error, would seem to assure a ready market for all
rubber which could be produced within the next several decades.
Program Components
14.       In order to execute the desired program on disease resistance and
clonal selection, the following would be provided under the project:
(a) civil works for adequate laboratory and research facilities at
the research station of Manaus (Commodity Research Program
Headquarters) and for satellite (sub-station) activities at
Belem. Facilities at CEPLAC, Bahia are considered sufficiently
well-equipped at present;



ANNEX 8
Page 6
(b) research farm development at IManaus and Belem;
(c) equipment, furnishings and vehicles for use at M4anaus and Belem;
(d) library, documentation, and information services at the two
stations mentioned above;
(e) technical services including consultants and fellowship programs;
(f) land purchase at Manaus; and
(g) incremental establishment costs of the project during a five-year
period. Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1,
2 and 3.
Special Program Objectives
15.       As noted earlier (para 1), the commodity research program for rubber
is unique among the other eight commodities selected for support under the
project because (a) the crop requires at least seven years to reach maturity,
i.e., tapping size; (b) the commercial (plantation) rubber industry is currently
not well established in Brazil so that research results would, initially,
benefit only a few plantation owners; and (c) resultinq from the above. there
is a severe lack of established rubber plantings sufficiently representative
both in size and geographic distribution, to serve as field test plots for
the research program. In view of the lengthy period necessary to establish
such a network of plots of sufficient size and scope, it would be essential
for the success of the project to ensure that the establishment of such demon-
stration plots moves parallel to the research program itself.
B. Detailed Features
Selection of High Yielding, Disease-Resistant Clones
16.       Using two main sources of genetic materials, i.e., the clonal collec-
tions of CEPLAC, Bahia, and the wide selection of species and varieties to be
found in the Amazon forests, the plant breeders, plant physiologists and entomo-
logists and pathologists would assemble elite materials for crossing and/or
vegetative propagation as clones.
Soils and Nutrition
17.       Soil scientists, working with production agronomists and the agro-
climatologist, would identify soil conditions that influence yield and would
identify the conditions which are optimum for latex production under the various
sets of soil/plant/water relationships to be found in the prescribed agro-
ecological area.



ANNEX 8
Page 7
Cultivation Practices
18.       The agronomists, working closely with the plant physiologists and
economists, would define the farming systems packages which would offer
optimum returns under various systems of clean cultivation, use of cover
crops, inter-planting and spacing distances influenced by total land use
methods, and leaf disease incidence. In the wider spacings and after trees
are sufficiently mature, ribbon planting with rubber/livestock combinations
may also be attempted.
Economic Studies
19.       In addition to performing production and marketing studies, the
economists would investigate the problems of income sources during the
initial seven-year period until trees are ready to yield.
C. Organization and Management
20.       The national rubber research program team would be headquartered
at Manaus. The station director would have overall responsibility for admin-
istration of the headquartcr        L s  a_L '      e saSt  EMB-APA '
nity relations at the center. He would be assisted by an advisory council 1/
and two associate directors--one for administrative services, usually function-
ing as the financial and procurement officer, and one for the implementation
of the research program.
21.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs), and
other cooperating institutions concerned with research on rubber, and the
application of new technology in rubber production would be achieved through
contacts between the team leader and representatives of the appropriate
research programs at these stations. An annual workshop would be held
at which current activities would be evaluated and plans for the research
program for the coming years would be made. Inputs from producers would
be obtained at such workshops, or field days in the distinct sub-areas of
major production. As a member of the advisory council, the associate director
for research would provide the necessary liaison between the council and
producers (see also Annex 17).
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members but
composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for example
of membership).



ANNEX 8
Page 8
Research Facilities
22.       The National Rubber Research Center to be established at Manaus
would require a minimum of 1,000 ha, plus laboratory facilities for soil
science, plant physiology, plant pathology, entomology, plant breeding,
economic research and latex processing. Adequate laboratory facilities
at Belem are already available, but additional field laboratories for
handling latex would be added under the project.
Collaborating Institutions
23.       There would be close cooperation with CEPLAC at Itabuna, Bahia
on all matters relating to the program of research for the production area
in that state, and the very complete rubber research facilities there would
be made available to the National Program of Research. The Firestone and
Goodyear companies have also expressed an interest in collaborating with
the National Program.
Research Network
24.       Currently, there is no network of research stations devoted
exclusively to rubber research. Therefore, one of the major tasks of
the program leader would be to establish one. The present skeleton
status of cooperating units is shown in Tabie 4.
University Contract Research
25.       The Federal University of Para (UFP), Belem, Para and the Agronomic
Institute at Campinas have been engaged in specific problems concerning
rubber cultivation in the past. Their collaboration would be on a contract
basis and they would be asked to undertake specific problems of a basic and
long-range nature under the approved program of research.
D. Staffing
26.       Almost all new staff would have to be assigned under the program.
There is only a small nucleus currently engaged in rubber research at Belem,
or less than 10% of manpower requirements for the new program. The National
Commodity Center at Manaus would have a staff of 84 professionals at full
development, plus 31 supporting staff. In addition, there would be 23 pro-
fessionals and 16 supporting staff at Belem. A detailed staff list is given
in Table 5.



ANNEX 8
Page 9
E. Training and Fellowship Program
27.       A training officer would be allocated to the Manaus Center and it
would be his duty to assist staff members with their professional development
within program needs. The training component of the project would provide
for the training of 34 to the M.Sc. level and six to the Ph.D. level. In
addition, 20 short courses would be available for the upgrading of staff
within the program in specific areas of study related to rubber research and
development. Details of this program are given in Table 2.
F. Consultants
28.       To further supplement the availability of trained scientists to
provide the necessary program leadership during the early stages of project
development, the project would supply the following consultancy services:
(a) a total of six man-years of long-term consultancy
services; and
(b) an additional 5.5 years of short-term consultancy service.
Specialists provided would be a production agronomist, a plant
pathologist, a plant physiologist, and a rubber processing
specialist. Details of consultant services to be provided
are given in Table 2.
G. Linkages to International Centers
29.       None of the eight International Research Centers deals with rubber
as a specific commodity program. However, the International Institute of
Tropical Agriculture (IITA) is particularly interested in matters dealing
with environmental changes where the humid tropics are being brought under
cultivation. Rubber, as a tree crop, offers an interesting opportunity for
study under such a program since it relates closely to the utilization of
forest resources in the Amazon basin. In addition, there are several private
organizations dealing with rubber, as a plantation crop, which have offered
their services commercially for specific studies on aspects of rubber culti-
vation in the humid tropics. The majority of these have had wide experience
in Southeast Asia and in Africa.

five-year period would be approximately US$11.4 million. The estimated

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR BEEF CATTLE
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Beef Cattle in Agriculture
1.        Brazil has the world's fourth largest cattle population, and,
while yields are now quite low, the country has considerable potential for
further beef cattle development. Current population of cattle in Brazil
is estimated at 80,584,000 head. Of these, 33.5% are in the Southeast,
22.1% in the South, 20.9% in the Northeast, 20.6% in the Central-West and
only 2.3% in the North (Amazonia). Pure beef herds account for only 49.6%
of this total, 35.5% are mixed (beef/dairy) herds and 14.9% are pure dairy-
cattle herds. Total beef produced in 1971 was 1.8 million tons. Some con-
straints to Brazilian meat exports are the existence in some areas of hoof
and mouth disease and fluctuations in the quantities offered. Export volumes
have varied considerablv since 1960. After a slight decline in 1971 due to
intervention by the Federal Government, to give priority to the domestic
supply, exports reached in 1972 a record/level of 181,700 tons worth
US$219,700,000, or about 5.5% of the country's total export earnings during
that year.
2.        In order for Brazil to maintain or increase its export market in
beef and hold a competitive position, it must first reduce fluctuations in
supply and bring about considerably increased yields per ha. In order to do
this, however, existing improved technology must be more widely applied.
Potentials and Constraints to Production
3.        Of Brazil's total grazing land area of about 146 million ha, only
27.4% (40.0 million ha) consists of "improved" pastureland and the remainder,
72.6% (106.0 million ha), is made up of natural pastureland, which has low
cattle carrying capacity, especially during the dry season. The main reasons
for low herd productivity, however, relate to animal health. The herds suffer
from nutritional deficiencies and various diseases (hoof and mouth, brucello-
sis, rabies, anthrax) which, in addition to causing an excessive mortality
rate and reducing the birth rate, also results in low production of meat and
milk due to a decline in the animal growth rate.
4.        The average birth rate of the Brazilian beef cattle population is
said to be less than 53%, which is very low when compared to the results
obtained on some ranches, which show rates of 80 to 85%. Another indication
of the rather weak development of the nation's cattle herds is the mortality



ANNEX 9
Page 2
rate, particularly in calves less than one year old, when it is about 10%.
The average beef cattle death rate is 3.8%. Also, the beef cattle popula-
tion density, that is to say, the average carrying capacity, on the basis
of pasture area, remains low at an average of 1.8 head per ha. In the case
of natural pastureland, this average drops down to only 0.5 head per ha.
The carrying capacity depends, above all, upon the dry season or the rainy
season, as follows:
Average Capacity of Natural and Artificial Pastureland by Agro-ecological
Region, Brazil (1971)
Average Carrying Capacity (head/hectare)
Natural Pasture              Improved Pasture
Rainy Season   Dry Season    Rainy Season Dry Season
North                1.3               0.9             4.6          2.3
Northeast            0.8               0.4             3.4          2.1
Southeast            1.0               0.6             2.2          1.6
South                0.7               0.5             3.0          2.4
Central-West         0.5               0.3             1.6          1.0
Brazil               0.8               0.5             2.9          1.8
Source: SUPLAN.
Demand Outlook for Domestic and Export Markets
5.        Brazilian domestic consumption is currently (1973) 16 kg/capita
(1.4 million tons) and population increase is 2.9% per year. Based on these
calculations, a 1970 population of 92.3 million and an assumed demand elasti-
city of 0.5, internal demand could reach 1.8 million tons by 1980. Export
demand will, of course, depend on many factors but, provided the present
pricing policies of greater parity between domestic and world price for beef
are continued, then a net increase of up to 3.5% per year, on a 1972 export
total of 155,600 tons, might well be maintained assuming that there is no
further deterioration in the economies of the major importing countries.
This would indicate a 1980 export demand of 250,000 tons and an annual total
production goal of at least 2.0 million tons by that year.
6.        The strategy to be used in supplying this additional output is
two-fold. First, there would be further improvement in areas of the South
where much can still be done by extending the benefits of existing knowledge
over a wide range of producers. In the Northeast, much work still needs
to be done to reduce losses, particularly during the prolonged dry season,
and appropriate farming systems, involving not only beef cattle but sheep
and goats, must be evolved and demonstrated. In the Central-West, also,
much has still to be learned concerning the adaptation of the cerrado soils
to produce adequate grazing, although selection of improved grasses adaptable
to some cerrado conditions has already been demonstrated. In the North,



ANNEX 9
Page 3
problems of animal health and management practices are of greater importance
and establishment of improved pastures has proved less difficult. Here, the
use of water buffalo shows considerable promise, both for meat and milk.
In all areas, however, the factors affecting the meat supply growth rate are
closely related to overall management, health and nutrition factors.
B. Present Status of Beef Cattle Research
7.        Nationally, there is very little beef cattle research being done
on the major problems facing the industry. Some research projects are
listed for all states of the Northeast, but the lack of the research imple-
mentation structure, as well as the lack of trained research teams and finan-
cial resources, has produced a situation in which there is practically no
real contribution from research to the improvement of productivity at the
farm level, and the level of technology adopted by the cattlemen is generally
low. There are several factors responsible for this situation, the most
important among them being the lack of contact between the research teams
and the producers. Research studies are, for the most part, concerned with
comparison between fodder species, fertilization and general pasture improve-
ment but they have usually not been accompanied by adequate cost-factor
analysis, with land inputs appropriately valued. The lack of joint program-
ming among the agencies involved in research has also led to the proliferation
of isolated studies, with results of limited importance to ranchers.
8.        Among research activities recently programmed for the Northeast, a
pastureland improvement and management program is now being set up on the
basis of an agreement between the Bank of Northeast Brazil (BNB) and EMlBRAPA
and financed by the Central Bank (BACEN). The BNB will act as financing agent
and will supervise program implementation through its Northeast Economic
Studies Department (ETENE), which administers the Fund for Scientific and
Technological Development (FUNDECI). The program will also have the support
of the UNDP-funded Northeast Livestock Development Project (BRA/71/552), which
is being executed on the basis of an agreement between the Brazilian Govern-
ment and FAO. This program, to be developed with 22 demonstration plots in
six states, has the following main objectives:
(a) analysis of promising beef cattle farming systems;
(b) demonstration of pastureland improvement and management with
sites for the introduction and trial of fodder species; and
(c) phosphate fertilization of pastureland and the palatability of
introduced pasture species.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
9.        As the principal agency promoting agricultural and livestock
research, EMBRAPA is now responsible for the technical aspects of this pro-
gram, including coordination of experimental activities and analysis and
evaluation of results.
10.       Since 1973, the Conselho Nacional de Desenvolvimento da Agropecuaria
(CONDEPE) has also been responsible for a technical assistance program to
farmers and an applied husbandry research program in the South and Central-
West areas of Brazil (Bank Loan 868-BR - Interim Livestock Project).  Research
activity under this project has concentrated mainly on demonstrating pasture
improvement techniques and herd management in the states of Santa Catarina,
Rio Grande do Sul, Sao Paulo, and Mato Grosso. Plans are now under way to
transfer the research component of this project to EMBRAPA.
II. THE NATIONAL BEEF CATTLE RESEARCH PROGRAMI
A. General Description of Project
Agro-ecological Coverage
11.       Although the national orogram woolAl be res-on--4 fr-h^ -r_-_
tion and coordination of beef-cattle research throughout the country, activi-
ties leading to an improvement in beef production would primarily be focused
on the following areas: Central-West (Campos Cerrados), the "agreste" and
'mlata" areas of the Northeast and the humid tropics (Amazonia).  In the humid
tropics, work would initially be limited to research with buffalo in the state
of Para and on dual purpose (dairy/beef) cattle in the north of the state of
Mato Grosso.
Program Goals
12.       Basically, the goals would be to maintain Brazil's position of self-
sufficiency for domestic demand and, at the same time, strengthen the export
position, consistent with opportunities in that area, which would appear to
represent no more than 14% of total production, i.e., 1.8 million tons domestic
and up to 0.25 million tons of export demand in 1980. The first step in attain-
ing the above goals and objectives would be the promotion of research, as may
be needed, to find out how to:
(a) provide more adequate year-round nutrition to grazing animals
through pasture improvement and supplemental nutrition;
(b) control major diseases more effectively;



ANNEX 9
Page 5
(c)  improve management practices to increase reproduction and
growth rates; and
(d)  more effectively provide the necessary incentives, credit
facilities and efficient marketing systems.
13.       The second step is to define precisely the production packages for
different situations and get them applied. Once the information is at hand,
the rate at which production would accelerate would depend on how rapidly
the technological packages worked out for each region are applied. This, in
turn, would depend a great deal on incentives in which input cost/production
price relationships would play a major role.
14.       The yield and production goals to meet projected (1980) requirements
are indicated in the table below.

Dam Population (1,000 head)                     28,285            37,776
Source:  EMBRAPA and mission estimates, July 15, 1975.



ANNEX 9
Page 6
Program Components
15.       The National Research Program for Beef Cattle is designed to
strengthen research capability and to respond more readily to the require-
ments of the beef cattle industry. The project components to be provided
under the project are:
(a) civil works at the research stations of Campo Grande,
Cuiaba' and Itapetinga to complete necessary laboratories
and research facilities;
(b) research farm development;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at the stations
mentioned;
(d) library documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs; and
(f) establishment costs during a five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Table 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
16.       Priorities in research would vary from area to area and could
be determined only by an adequate survey of factors limiting production.
In general, however, emphasis would be on activities as described below.
Animal Nutrition
17.       Improvement of production and nutritional value of native and
introduced pasture species adapted to the various ecological conditions of
the different regions would be a major effort, involving (a) evaluation of
native and exotic grasses and legumes for their nutritive value, yield
and production stability under various types of environmental stress; (b)
development of efficient methods for seed production, and introduction and
establishment of selected grasses and legumes; (c) improvement of pasture
management practices, including fertilization, weed control and carrying
capacity under prevailing environmental conditions; (d) determination and
establishment of mineral supplements in accordance with requirements of
various animal categories under different feeding conditions and forage
availability in the region; and (e) increasing production and conservation
of native spontaneous or cultivated fodder crops for cattle feeding during
periods of pasture shortage.



ANNEX 9
Page 7
Animal Health
18.       Studies would aim at determining the principal agents or causes
affecting the state of animal health in order to prevent and control in-
fectious and contageous diseases; prevent and control ecto- and endoparasites;
and maintain hygienic conditions and take preventive measures among cattle
herds. Studies of the physiology of reproduction would also be undertaken.
Herd Management Practices
19.       Research would aim at determining the most efficient herd manage-
ment practices during the breeding, reproduction and finishing phases for
the acceleration of production for various producer categories. Special
emphasis would be given to studies of management practices that increase
calving and animal growth rates and decrease diseases.
Farming Systems
20.       Research would be done to determine the best life cycle for beef
production systems for both large and small operaLors. Special aLtenLion
would be given to the development of family size operations. Studies would
also deal with beef production systems where beef production might be in-
cluded in farming systems with food or feed crops such as maize and cassava.
Breeding
2i.       Researchl in breediTig would priritarily be coLceiLL1ed wiLh Lhe develop-
ment of genetically more productive herds and production of more efficient
animals for each ecological condition through cross-breeding.
Economics
22.       Studies would be made on the physical and economic factors of beef
cattle production and sales systems aimed at providing cattlemen with in-
formation that would help them increase their output through the use of
new practices derived from research information obtained.
C. Organization and Management
23.       The national beef cattle research program team would be head-
quartered at Campo Grande. The station director would have overall respons-
ibility for administration of the headquarters station and would represent
EMBRAPA for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an
advisory council 1/ and two associate directors -- one for administraLive
services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and
one for the implementation of the research program.
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
example of membership).



ANNEX 9
Page 8
24.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs) and
other cooperating institutions concerned with research on beef cattle and
the application of new technology in beef cattle production would be achieved
through contacts between the team leader and representatives of the appropriate
research programs at these stations. An annual workshop would be held at
which current activities would be evaluated and plans for the research program
for the coming years would be made. Inputs from producers would be obtained
at such workshops, or field days, in the distinct sub-areas of major produc-
tion. As a member of the advisory council, the associate director for re-
search would provide the necessary liaison between the council and producers
(see also Annex 17).
Research Facilities
25.       Facilities to be provided at the National Beef Research Center at
Campo Grande, where 4,500 ha of land are available, would include adequate
pasture, holding pens, animal chute stabling for experimental animals, and
laboratories for veterinary, animal nutrition, soils, agronomy and seed
improvement. Animal slaughter is to be carried out at a nearby commercial
facility, but station facilities would include a meats laboratory for ex-
perimental use. At the satellite stations (UEPAEs), which are also desig-
nated as part of the research network, the following land areas are avail-
able: Itapetinga, 200 ha and Cuiabd', 500 ha. These stations would
be equipped with a livestock/crop facility including the necessary field
laboratory for animal handling and demonstration plots for work at the
farm level. In the case of the two Agricultural Research Centers at
Planaltina and Belem, where cooperative work would be carried out under
differing agro-ecological conditions, the facilities would also include
laboratories for the analysis of soils and animal nutrition related to
pasture management aspects of beef cattle research.
Research Station Network
26.       The total research network for beef cattle would comprise work
at five research stations under the project. In addition, there would be
five UEPAEs, not funded by the project, but engaged in adaptive research
and technology transfer both within and outside the project area. There
are also 10 Agricultural Research Institutes, Schools of Agronomy or
Faculties of Agriculture in the Northeast, South and Southeast regions
which would be available for university contract research programs
related to the project (see also Table 4 for details).
University Contract Research
27.       As with other national commodity programs, university contract
research would be requested through EMBRAPA headquarters, Brasilia (see
Annex 17). Rather than accept programs presented by the universities,



ANNEX 9
Page 9
the plan should be to request university participation as and when needed and
confine the requests to carefully selected areas of research where answers
are needed for clearly defined problems of a basic nature where specific
laboratory facilities and discipline-oriented inputs are more readily
available and can, at the same time, provide graduate study for university
educational problems. Care would always be provided, however, to see that
duplication of effort is avoided and that specific problems related to the
current research program are given national rather than regional importance.
Technical Program Structure and Management
28.       An annual workshop would be held for the evaluation of current ac-
tivities and for the planning, coordination and execution of work for the
coming year. Key individuals concerned with the acceleration and improve-
ment of beef production, regardless of how they are employed, would parti-
cipate in these workshops. The purpose would be to promote a harmonious,
well planned and coordinated effort in the improvement of beef production
on a rational basis, while devoting adequate attention to problems of a
regional nature, as well as to those of the beef cattle industry as a whole.
D. Staffing
29.       Staff requirements at full scale operation call for 68 professional
staff and 31 administrative support staff at the Campo Grande center. In
addition, 64 professional staff and 32 support staff would be directly in-
volved with the program at the satellite stations mentioned. As in many of
the other commodity programs, only 10% of the number of staff required is
available. Further, most of the staff would need to be developed and em-
phasis would be on in-service training. A training officer has been included
for the planning and supervision of this program (see also Table 5).
E. Training and Fellowship Program
30.       In conjunction with the in-service training, certain individuals
will be encouraged to study for the advanced degrees or to participate in
special short courses. The project provides fellowships for six individuals
to study toward Ph.D. degrees and 42 toward M.Sc. degrees while 18 staff
members would be encouraged to participate in special short courses related
to their area of research (see also Table 2).



ANNEX 9
Page 10
F. Consultants
31.       A total of 5.5 man-years are made available under the project for
use of the national beef cattle research program and assignments would be
made according to specific needs (see also Table 2). Emphasis would be
placed on satisfying the need for livestock management specialist.
G. Linkages with External Organizations
32.       Where both desirable and feasible the research team, through the
team leader/coordinator, would solicit the assistance and collaboration of
the outreach programs of the International Center for Tropical Agriculture
(CIAT) for pertinent research and in-service training activities. Certain
universities in the USA and Europe might also be able to help in specific
studies and an attempt would be made to bring specific talent to bear in
the solution of specific problems, as may be required (see also Annex 17).
III. COST ESTIMATES
33.       The cost for the National Research Program for Beef Cattle for
the five-year period would be approximately US$15.4 million. The estimated
costs are summarized in Table 1.

AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR DAIRY CATTLE
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Dairy Cattle in Agriculture
Role in Development Strategy
1.        Planning the development and, hence, research strategy for dairy-
ing during the next five-year period depends on three essential factors:
(a) the perishable nature of the fresh product, (b) population location
trends, and (c) the rather specialized agro-ecological demands of pasture
production and animal health which bear very strongly on the economics of
milk production.
2.        Combining these influences, one can conclude that (a) production
must be improved in the Northeast to meet the already existing requirements
of the area, ana (bj as popuiacfion incr-ease  .  .n r  he Cntr_-' -T.7-^st  -A V-
regions, greater efforts will have to be made to meet the rapid increase
in the demand for fresh milk products in those areas. It is expected that,
in the case of the large population centers now forming in the Central-West,
particularly the growth taking place in the Federal District, demand will in
the first instance be met by intensive production in Minas Gerais and Goias.
In the case of increased liquid milk demand in the North (Amazonia), however,
and in the extreme West (Mato Grosso), it is expected that demands will be
met, in the first instance, from either dual purpose or low intensity dairy
herds, where beef production plays either a major or equally important role
in farm income. These factors must, of necessity, dictate the pattern of
research relative to selection of breeds, pasture improvement, nutrition
levels and management methods to meet the specific needs of the area. These
will be reflected in the regional programs for milk production research.
Production Performance
3.        Data for 1974 indicate a total of 9.7 million cows being milked,
including dual purpose (beef/milk) animals. Total production was 7.2
million tons kg. 1/. Production is concentrated close to the areas of
high density population, which also have climatic conditions most favorable
to milk production. The Southeast region, with milk production concentrated
in the Minas Gerais triangle, accounts for 54% of all dairy cattle and 57%
of all milk produced. The South is next in importance, with 19% of cattle
and 25% of milk produced. Production in the Central-West comes from 11% of
the cattle and amounts to 6% of the milk volume. The Northeast has 14% of the
1/   Source:  FAO Production Yearbook, 1974.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
cattle, producing 11% of the milk. The North has 2% of the cattle and
produces 1% of the milk. The figures given, although estimates, do point
out the superior position of the South and Southeast, which account for
73% of the cattle and 82% of the milk produced. Average production in the
South is 860 kg/cow/year, as compared to only 430 kg/cow/year in the North.
Potentials and Constraints
4.        Brazil's total milk demand calculated both Lor fresh and procenscd
milk (industrialized use) and, based on a 1973 population of 101.5 m, shows
the consumption for that year was 91.62 kg per capita, or 9.299 m tons. If
a continued 2.9% per annum population increase is assumed for the seven-year
period 1973-1980, demand would rise to 13,038 million tons in 1980, based
on a population of 124.83 m and consumption of 104,45 kg/capita, which also
reflects demand elasticity of 0.7 over the seven-year period. The potential
expansion for the dairy industry over the seven-year period is seen to be
40%, or 5.5% increase per annum, based on the projected increase in domestic
consumption.
5.        The major constraints to efficient milk production up to the present
time have been those normally associated with intensive milk production in the
humid tropics, particularly at low elevations and within latitudes 300 north
and south of the equator.  Mlajor problems are chose coiLceriuilig anImal he_l.i
and nutrition, the latter being particularly related to establishment of
improved pastures under tropical conditions. Much research has already been
done, however, and the major constraints to improved production are largely
those related to the need for improving methods for the transfer of technology,
particularly to the small farmer.
B. Present Status of Dairy Cattle Research
6.        Some thought has been given to developing the water buffalo (bubalino)
as a dual purpose beef/milk animal. Experiments have been carried out on pro-
duction costs of milk produced by buffalo under restricted pasture and stable
conditions similar to those currently in use for intensive dairy cattle herds,
7.        PLA1ANd (Plano de Melhoramento e Alimentacao do Gado Leitero) is a
special agency of the Ministry of Agriculture to promote dairy cattle develop-
ment in the Northeast region. Research has been concentrated on nutrition and
pasture improvement, plus supplementary use of feed concentrate rations. This
work has been done at Nossa Senhora do Socorro by DNPEA and by IPA at Sao
Bento do Una, Arcoverde and Caruaru. However, the research program for dairy
cattle in the Northeast has always been much smaller than that devoted to
beef cattle.



AlNNEX 10
Page 3
8.        In the Central-West region, particularly in the states of Mato
Grosso and Goias, experiments have been carried out on optimum breeding
dates for dual purpose animals and on the relationship of calf weaning to
the length of the lactation period.
9.        In the Southeast region, research on dairy cattle has been
well advanced, particularly in the state of Minas Gerais, with some spe-
cialized work also being done in Rio de Janeiro state on reproductive
physiology. In Minas Gerais, numerous experiments have been done on animal
nutrition, optimum weaning dates and the use of agricultural by-products
as possible feed supplements for dairy cattle. In both states (Minas Gerais
and Rio de Janeiro), considerable work on improved pastures for intensive
dairy production has been carried out. This has included (a) selection
of grass and legume species for improved pastures, (b) establishment of
pasture cultivation techniques, (c) supplements to pasture nutrition, (d)
relationship of pasture species to soil types of the region, and (e) economic
studies based on differing pasture/supplemental nutrition regimes.
10.       In the South, the states of Rio Grande do Sul and Parana have been
the areas of greatest research activity on dairy cattle. Some basic work on
cattle breeding has been done on weaning dates in relation to both dairy herd
selection (of heifers and of young males for transfer to beef cattle herds)
-andA on t1he use o'0 r_Lcc str-aw f^r  f-el-^t-r    _-1nog n- -rec-ently? harvested
rice fields. Economic studies have included relative production indices for
purebred dairy cattle (Holstein-Friesian) in comparison with dual purpose
breeds under similar management practices. The same two states (Rio Grande
do Sul and Parana) have reported numerous experiments on pasture improvement,
levels of fertilization, nutritive value of native pasture species and methods
for pasture establishment. In addition, agricultural by-products have been
studied for their nutritional value of feed substitutes and/or additives to
the normal rations for dairy cattle.
Present Research Station Network
11.       The present system dates from the formation of the National Depart-
ment for Agricultural Research (DNPEA), which was formed in 1971 (see also
Annex 3). Under that system, dairv cattle research was the responsibility
of two technical divisions--the Division for Animal Husbandry Research (DPZ)
and the Division for Animal Pathology (DPZP). The research program, which
was not well coordinated, was carried out at the regional agriculture research
institutes in the main areas of milk production. These were, for dairy service,
mainly IPEACS (Institute for Agricultural Research for the Central-South), with
headquarters at Itaguai (RJ), serving the states of Rio de Janeiro, Guanabara
and Espirito Santo; and in the south, the Institute for Agricultural Research
of the South, with headquarters at Pelotas - Rio Grande do Sul, serving the
states of Rio Grande do Sul and Santa Catarina; and IPEACO (Institute for
Agricultural Research for the Central-West Region), serving the states of
Minas Gerais, Goias, and Mato Grosso and the Federal District of Brasilia,



ANNEX 10
Page 4
with headquarters at Sete Lagoas. Additional work under UNDP/FAO sponsor-
ship was originally started under agreement with the Pernambuco State
Secretariat for Agriculture and the Institute for Agricultural Research
at Pernambuco (IPA). Later, DNPEA assumed the counterpart role for this
project for livestock development, mainly through pasture improvement and
herd selection designed for the Northeast.
Research Personnel
12.       There is no data available to show numbers of research personnel
devoted exclusively to dairy cattle research, Per se, as opposed to the
number working on cattle (dairy, beef, dual purpose and buffalo).
Achievements
13.       During the decade 1961-1970, national milk production per cow -
based on general herd size - rose only 4.2%. This reflected mainly pasture
improvement and slightly better management practices. This was particularly
true in the Northeast, where the corresponding productivity increase was 26.6%
over the same period. Increased production was particularly marked in the
states of Maranhao (143%), Piaui (61%), Ceara (21%) and Alagoas (36%), and was
the result of a movement towards more intensive milk production, as opposed to
the use of dual purpose animals, since the number of head increased only 19%
during the sa-e pcrlod.  Achia-va.mens d rectly related                       _ n -oarAw results
have been the increased adoption of improved pasture grasses and the use of
animal feed concentrates. Research related to breed selection and herd
improvement have been much less emphasized. Constraints to further improve-
ment in the industry have related mainly to poor coordination of limited
research efforts and lack of on-farm demonstration and extension effort at the
farm level. Government price controls on milk have, in recent years, failed
to keep pace with production costs and thus have had a depressing effect on
production goals. Recently (July 1975), however, price adjustments were
granted as a stimulus to production.
II. THE NATIONAL DAIRY CATTLE
RESEARCH PROGRAM
A. General Description
Agro-ecological Coverage
14.       Research would continue in the highly populated areas of the South
and Southeast, particularly in the states of Parana, Rio Grande do Sul, Rio de
Janeiro, and Minas Gerais. In the Central-West, new work would be designed



ANNEX 10
Page 5
to overcome problems peculiar to the Campos Cerrados soils in the states
of Mato Grosso and Goias and in the Federal District of Brasilia. In the
North, it is expected that, initially, the preliminary work on adapting the
water buffalo as a dual purpose animal would be concentrated in the state of
Para, with Belem as a base for these operations. In the Northeast, the states
of Piaui, Maranhao, Ceara, Pernambuco and Bahia are important to the milk
production of that area. The agro-ecological details concerning the specific
areas mentioned above are to be found in Annexes 12, 13 and 14e
Program Components
15.       In order to achieve the broad goals outlined above, the program
would have the following components:
(a) civil works at the state research centers (UEPAEs) of Itapetinga
and Quissama;
(b) research farm development at the stations mentioned above;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at stations mentioned
above plus the headquarters station at Agua Limpa;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental establishment costs of the project during a five-year
period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
16.       The objectives of the research would be to increase current averaae
yields, under the varying sets of conditions to be found in the producing areas.
On a general basis, these results must be capable of increasing average
commercial dairy production by some 5.5% on a sustained basis, merely to main-
tain self-sufficiency in the dairy industry.
Yield and Production Goals
17.       These would be consistent with economic levels of production, and
would take into account factors such as milk subsidies and special market
influences related to distance from markets, etc. The capability to produce



ANNEX 10
Page 6
milk of quality for use as fresh (crude) milk, as opposed to processed milk,
also influences the production goals of each farmer. In this area, the
economist must consider market prices, as well as production costs, to deter-
mine the level of inputs desired.
Herd Improvement
18.       Improvement by selection, within existing herds, would be given
priority, as would the improvement of herds by well proven cross-breeding
programs. Of second priority, however, would be improvement of genetic
potential at the purebred level.
Animal Nutrution
19.       Several experiments in this area would be given first priority
such as (a) evaluation of nutrition content of pasture species; (b) esta-
blishment, management and conservation of improved pastures; (c) fertiliza-
tion of native and improved pastures; and (d) supplementary feeding and
mineral supplements.
Animal Husbandry
20.       Hera managemenc, animaL huSDbnufy mf;Lo  cid z
returns, based on farming systems developed for each of the main producing
regions would be tested taking into consideration the best cropping system
and combination of crops,to assure maximum production at the several levels
of production activity,determined by farm size.
Animal Health
21.       Priority would be given to the development of systems to maintain
herd health by sanitation, of preventive means to control both ecto- and
endo-parasites, and to the training of stockmen in day-to-day animal health
care.
Marketing and By-product Development
22.       Although of secondary priority, the work in this area would first
involve marketing studies to ensure that the producer would obtain maximum
returns from the fresh product (milk). These experiments would later be
widened to include the agribusiness aspects of processed milk and its by-
products to determine ways of increasing income at the farm level.
Special Program Objectives
23.       These are likely to relate mainly to the degree of production inten-
sity, both in relation to production per unit area and to production per animal.
The latter is often an important factor in deciding to use purebred milk cows



ANINEX 10
Page 7
for intensive production with a high level of technology and management or
dual purpose animals where beef yield is also important under more exten-
sive, lower cost management practices.
C. Organization and Management
24.       The national dairy cattle research program team would be head-
quartered at Agua Limpa. The station director would have overall respon-
sibility for administration of the headquarters station and would represent
EMBRAPA for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an
advisory council 1/ and two associate directors--one for administrative
services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and
one for the implementation of the research program.
25.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs)
and other cooperating institutions, concerned with research on dairy cattle
and the application of new technology in milk production, would be achieved
chrough contacts OeLween chr  Leam leader anid representatives of the appro-
priate research programs at these stations. An annual workshop would be
held at which current activities would be evaluated and plans for the
research program for the coming year would be made. Inputs from producers
would be obtained at such workshops or field days in the distinct sub-areas
of major production. As a member of the advisory council, the associate
director for research would provide the necessary liaison between the council
and producers (see also Annex 17).
Research Facilities
26.       The National Commodity Research Center for Dairy Cattle would be
at Agua Limpa, near Juiz de Fora, 2/ Minas Gerais. Satellite substation
activity would be located at Itapetinga and Quissama in the Northeast. The
work related to dual purpose animals (beef/milk) would continue at the
National Commodity Center for Beef Cattle, Campo Grande and at the Agricultural
Research Center for the North (Amazonia) at Belem where work on dual purpose
buffalo would continue.
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members but
composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for example
of membership).
2/   This is one of the two national commodity centers being financed outside
the project area, the other being that for maize/sorghum at Sete Lagoas.



AtINEX 10
Page 8
27.       The Agua Limpa station has 1,900 ha of land available and also
has adequate buildings and laboratory facilities to carry out the core
research program. Under the project, 2,000 ha of land are to be provided
by the Government, at Itapetinga, and building requirements are to be
provided for under the project. At Quissama, 100 ha of land is available,
but additional buildings are necessary to equip this station. These would
be provided as part of project costs. For milk production research, using
dual purpose animals, adequate facilities are currently available at Belem
for research on buffalo. At the National Center for Beef Cattle Research
(Campo Grande), land facilities for work on dual purpose animals are cur-
rently adequate, but the addition of a laboratory complex would be a part
of the project (see also Annex 15).
Research Station Network
28.       In addition to the headquarters station for the program at Agua
Limpa, there are four other stations: two UEPAEs, one Agricultural Research
Center, and one other National Commodity Center (Campo Grande) which would
contribute towards the program within the project area and which would be
funded by the project. In addition, five UEPAEs, mainly in the South and
Southeast, would also be involved in dairy cattle adaptive research and
technology transfer. Finally, two universities and one livestock research
institute would be available to collaborate on university contract research
as may be necessary for specific problem areas. The stations contributing
to this program are listed in Table 4.
University Contract Research
29.       The use of university contract research would depend upon the
specific needs of the research program as a whole and would be on the basis
of a request from ENI3RAPA to the university most capable of carrying out
the specific research program. Projects designated for university con-
tract research would generally be specific in nature, of rather short
duration and concern basic research to provide necessary linkage to ongoing,
long-term research within the national commodity program.
D. Staffing
30.       Staff at the national commodity center (Agua Limpa) would consist,
at full development, of 63 professionals plus 31 administrative support staff.
Also involved in the national dairy cattle research program would be 64 pro-
fessionals and 32 administrative support staff at the two UEPAE stations
mentioned. Only 9% of the staff required is available to the project so that
incremental staffing costs of 91% are needed at year 5 of full project devel-
opment (see Table 5).



ANNEX 10
Page 9
E. Training and Fellowships Program
31.       A training officer would be provided as part of the technical
services team at the national commodity center. His duties would be to
advise the technical staff at the center concerning their training needs
in order to fulfill their roles in the research program and to recommend
further opportunities for in-service or advanced degree academic training
as may be necessary to meet these needs. His recommendations would then
be transmitted to the Department for Human Resources (DRH) at EMBRAPA in
Brasilia, through the program director (see also Annex 17 for details). The
proposed graduate training component assigned to this commodity research
program includes five candidates for Ph.D. training and 34 at the M.Sc.
level, plus 10 positions for participation in short courses relevant to
the research program (see Table 2).
F. Consultants
32.       The project would provide for a total of 3.0 man-years of long-
term consultancy service and 2.5 man-years of short-term consultancy services.
Consultancy services for the do&rv  'qttla re>aonrh poIram would be dictated
by individual program needs and the availability of Brazilian technicians
to adequately fill these needs. Long-term consultancy services would be
utilized mainly to help provide a leadership role and assist in program
development. Short-term services would supply interchange with other insti-
tutes and for project evaluation (see Table 2).
G. Linkages to International Centers
33.       A collaboration program with the International Center for Tropical
Research (CIAT) at Cali, Colombia would be beneficial in the area of general
livestock research, and particularly so in relation to animal nutrition,
pasture improvement and breeding where the accent is on dual purpose (beef/
milk) herds, as opposed to intensive dairy farming (see also Annex 17).
