BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR SHEEP AND GOATS IN SEMI-ARID TROPICAL REGION 1/
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Importance of Sheep and Goats
Production Performance
1.        Goats.  The world population of goats amounted to 391 million head
in 1973. Brazil reportedly has approximately 16 million head, which makes it
sixth in the world, ranking behind India, China, Nigeria, Turkey and Pakistan.2/
Goats tend to be concentrated, at least relative to other species, in drier
areas with sparse water and grazing resources, often corresponding with areas
of greatest human poverty..
2.        In Brazil, goats are scattered throughout the country, but most are
found in the Northeast (78% of the national total). The major concentrations
are in the states of Bahia, Piaui, Ceara, Pernambuco and Paraiba, in that order.
1lSo .~ait d  pLvpilL'y .^<; e-<pf>.{tx=<  uy  ,.e  .a;.Lht-, 6cLd, dLiu -wz.iimes only
goats, do well in areas of sparce precipitation where woody plant species
provide a major part of the feed supply. Whatever the reason, it is important
to realize that the food proteins they supply are of great significance to
Lhe people of these areas. To a large degree, they may be irreplaceable un-
less water and plant resources can be developed for more intensive producLion
of crops or other livestock. Goats also make an important contribution to
the Brazilian economy, largely through the production of hides or skins. In
1972, the value of goat skins exported was on the order of six million dollars
with the majority coming from the Northeast. In recent years, Government
policies have encouraged local processing in order to increase the value of
the export product.
3.        Sheep.  Brazil has a population of approximately 25.5 million sheep,
the largest concentration of whclh is found in the state of Rio Grande do Sul,
with approximately 53.4% of the total. The sheep of Rio Grande do Sul are
primarily wool type animals, but they also produce meat. In the past, most
research with sheep in Brazil has been directed toward this population. Also,
1/   Currently EMBRAPA does not have a sheep/goat program of research in the
northeast. It is intended that the proposed project will concentrate on
goat production for meat, with some attention being given to hair-type
sheep, also for meat production. The national center for wool-bearing
sheep will, as heretofore, a headquartered at Bage, Rio Grande do Sul.
2/   Source:  FAO Production Yearbook, 1973.



ANNEX 11
Page 2
research from other countries can be more readily adapted to serve the type
of production in this more temperate region. Research on behalf of the sheep
industry of Rio Grande do Sul should continue, but the proposed project is
intended to serve the other center of production which is located in the
Northeast, mainly in the states of Bahia, Piaui, Pernambuco, Ceara and
Paraiba. The sheep of this area are the hair type or non-wool producing
sheep, and in many respects, they resemble and serve a similar purpose as
the goats of the area. However, there are some important differences.
First, they have different grazing habits from goats and their forage con-
sumption can, in some cases, complement rather than compete with that of
the goat. Second, sheep are potentially more efficient meat producers be-
cause they expend less energy in movement than goats, and, also, sheep can
be crossed with exotic or introduced breeds to markedly increase growth
rates and carcass yields.
Potentials and Constraints to Goat and Sheep Production
4.        Goats are truly multi-purpose animals--they produce meat, milk,
sometimes fiber hair, and leather. The importance of these contributions
is not fully documented in available statistics since these products are
often used for home consumption. Goat consumption of brush or browse species
may be a detriment under severe conditions of overgrazing, but the animals'
eating habits can generally be looked upon as an advantage, since they assist
in controlling often invading species as well as producing valuable products
from them.  Even in  zrea3                   IDhe-e ca. 'ia _a. -c U.,  t -ay zC:irIz tc{
also include goats to more completely utilize the vegetation available and
assist in the control of invading browse species. Use of a pasture by mixed
species often produces more offtake with less damage to the vegetation than
when a single species is involved. The potential of using goats for milk
production to provide badly needed protein and calcium in the human diet has
not been effectively exploited, particularly in the Northeast. Species com-
parisons as to grazing patterns and productive efficiency would be areas of
research, working with all three of the animals mentioned--cattle, sheep
and goats--at those centers where feed conditions and land area permitted.
5.        There is a worldwide shortage of animal protein and there would be
potential for Brazilian penetration of export markets, particularly in the
Western Hemisphere. However, forecasts for 1980 produced by the Banco do
Nordeste do Brasil (BNB) anticipate a meat deficit in the Northeast of some
10,900 tons. The potential exists for some increase in production of these
products from the Northeast, and research is needed to appraise this po-
tential, as well as to facilitate its accomplishment.
6.        Good statistical data are seldom available on net slaughter as a
function of the animal population maintained. BNB has estimated annual
slaughter rates of 15 to 17% for goats and 13 to 17% for sheep. This is
probably expressed as a function of the total population, and it is not clear
if this includes home or local slaughter. Hide sales should provide an
estimate of slaughter, but must, in all cases, represent something less than
total slaughter. The major factors affecting offtake are reproductive rate,



ANNEX 11
Page 3
total slaughter. The major factors affecting offtake are reproductive rate,
age at slaughter, death, and length of productive life. Assuming a net re-
productive rate of 100% for adult breeding females, that all animals are
carried to at least one year of age before slaughter, and that all female
offspring are kept for replacement or herd increase, the annual offtake rate
expressed as a percent of the total population maintained would be 25%, plus
any sales of cull females. If one assumes a 50% survival to time of culling,
the result would be a projected slaughter rate of 37.5%, assuming no provision
for flock increases. This indicates, based on the assumptions made, a pro-
jected offtake rate approximately double that reporteu at present.  The cal-
culations give some indication of the potential for increased production,
but, realistically, the economic feasibility of achieving even more modest
goals must be thoroughly tested through research, as little or no comparative
data are available.
Demand Outlook for Industry
7.        Realistically, in the Northeast region (except where the potential
exists for irrigation), the sheep and goat industries are not ones in which
the most dramatic changes may be expected. However, the potential for some
increase in production is good. The 1980 estimates 1/ reflect a demand for
goat meat and mutton of 60.9 thousand tons, and a deficit of possibly 4.9
thousand tons. These figures are based on 1970 consumption of 56,000 tons
(goat meat and mutton comhinRd) on a nonulation of 93.3 million. showing a
per capita consumption of only 0.6 kg per capita per year. The demand for
sheep and goat skins is also expected to remain strong and, at current meat
prices, the skins frequently represent 30% of the value of the animal.
Based on the above projections, a production increase of 9% during the
period 1970-80 could readily be absorbed.
B. Current Status of Research
8.        Very little production or management research work has been done
with sheep and goats in the Northeast region. That which has been done in
the south of Brazil or other countries has little application to the Northeast.
Several states in the Northeast, especially Bahia, Pernambuco and Piaui, have
initiated development and improvement programs with goats. The enthusiasm for
work with these species is high in these areas at the present time and the
programs which have been undertaken are well designed and consistent with
presently available technology. However, the limitation in these programs
is generally a lack of technology adequate to permit a major advance, and
some of the programs now being emphasized have not been thoroughly tested.
Thus, research to provide this new technology is a weak link at the present
time.
9.        Observations on goats have been or are presently being made at a
number of locations in the Norteast, and a few locations also have sheep.
1/   Source:  Studies by Banco do Nordeste do Brasil (BNB), 1973.



ANNEX 11
Page 4
However, research data are not being recorded and direct comparisons are not
being made. Some of the locations where such flocks are presently found
include Sertania (goats only), Quixada (sheep only), Campo Major (sheep and
goats), and Valencia (goats only). In the case of goats, the primary interest
in breeding programs is with the Bhuj and Anglo Nubian. However, these geno-
types have apparently not received thorough testing before release to the
public. In the case of sheep, the stations presently possessing breeding
flocks are working with the native or "Deslanado" genotype. The animals being
maintained appear to be distinctly improved over those in public hands, but
these are apparently not being released at present. A unit at Serra Talhada
is, however, making observations on management systems, parasite control,
mineral supplementation and their interaction with genotypes through the in-
troduction of Anglo Nubian breeding.
10.       Disease control seems to have received more attention than animal
breeding and management. At least a number of disease and parasite problems
have been identifed, and vaccination programs, where applicable, are underway.
Universities and institutes such as the Bahia Biological Institute, the
Center for Zoopathological Research (CPZ), and the Federal University of
Ceara (UFCE) appear to have made major contributions. Major disease problems
include foot and mouth disease, rabies, botulism, anthrax, blackleg, carbunculo,
gaseous lymphadenitis, mastitis, parasitism and contagious ecthyma.
11.       Recently, there has been renewed interest in research into practical
aspects of sheep and goat raising, and some experiments are being developed
in this field. These experiments are concerned mainly with determining the
economic impact of the introduction of improved practices on sheep and goat
raising, either separately or in combination. It is felt that more detailed
research should be undertaken only after this basic question has been studied.
Experiments are being initiated in Serra Talhada (Pernambuco) by IPA/EMBRAPA/FAO;
in Quixada (Ceara) jointly by the Agronomic Research Institute Foundation
(FIPA), the Ministry of Agriculture (DEMA), the Superintendency for the
Development of Ceara (SUDEC), and the National Department of Drought Control
Works (DNOCS); and in Juazeiro (Bahia) jointly by the Center for Agricultural
Trials and Improvements SA (CEMSA) and the Bank of Northeast Brazil (BNB).
All these trials have the same objectives and even use similar treatments,
which further indicates the lack of coordination and integration of experi-
mental programs in the Northeast, and the resulting duplication of effort.
12.       Currently, the major constraints to achieving an effective research
program would appear to be (a) the lack of a well coordinated research pro-
gram with clearly defined goals, and (b) the lack of research personnel with
specialized training in this field who would be available for full-time partic-
ipation in research.



ANNEX 11
Page 5
II. THE NATIONAL RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR SHEEP AND GOATS
A. General Description
13.       In formulating the national research program, it is essential to
point out that research on wool sheep (in the South and the Southeast) would
be based on research requirements for wool sheep in that area. The research
program of the Northeast would concentrate on the distinctive requirements
of hair-type sheep and goats adapted to the semi-arid "sertao" and "agreste"
regions of the agro-ecological area of the Northeast.
Agro-ecological Coverage
14.       The major concentration of the sheep and goats research effort in
the Northeast would be in the semi-arid interior or sertao. This region is
characterized by low annual rainfall with long dry periods, poor soils, and
a generally dense ground cover of woody or browse species called "Caatinga".
There is considerable variability in topography, soil type and vegetative
coverage and various micro-climates exist within the larger region involved.
Limited numbers of sheep and goats are also found throughout the "agreste" area
but the potential for greater sheep and goat production would be explored.
Essentially, no sheep and goats are found in the coastal mata and no research
anol-vement with shnc-  -Ar  gcztn      e;  nrinodc,o The rnnly likely
contribution of these species in the coastal mata would be the possibility
that a small number of goats might be kept for the home milk supply (see also
Annex 13).
15.       There would be three principal locations for sheep/goat research
in the Northeast. The headquarters of the national research program for
sheep and goats would be at Sobral, Ceara where there are opportunities for
research in arid as well as in semi-arid areas. Satellite activities would
be carried out at Teresina, Piaui to permit research in the tropics having
a distinct dry season and also at Petrolina/Juaziero, site of the Agricul-
tural Research Center for the Northeast. The location of these stations is
designed to provide the wide range of conditions necessary for the research
program.
Program Goals
16.       The broad aims of the program would be to provide the technology
necessary to increase the production of goat meat and mutton by approximately
5% per year. The areas where most effective advances can be made in the short
term are in (a) identification and correction of nutritional deficiencies, (b)
selection and breeding of superior genotypes already existing in the country,
and (c) improvement of animal health through disease and parasite control,
using preventive measures. Long-term research programs would include (a)
introduction of superior genotypes into the population, (b) identification
and utilization of existing pasture and forage species, and (c) the develop-
ment of farming systems to utilize combinations of pasture for cattle, sheep
and goat herds for maximum utilization of forage available.



ANNEX 11
Page 6
Program Components
17.       In order to carry out the research program at the localities men-
tioned, the following components would be provided under the project:
(a) civil works for the necessary laboratory and office buildings
at Sobral, Teresina and Petrolina/Juaziero;
(b) research farm development at the above stations;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at all stations
mentioned above;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowship
programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) incremental establishment cost of the project during a
five-year period.
Details of the investment to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
18.       The program would include experiments designed to increase growth
rate and reproductive efficiency of the herd through selection of superior
animals in the herd and introduction of exotic strains where this proves
feasible.
Range Management Methods
19.       Studies would be undertaken on improved range management methods,
including rotation grazing, brush and weed control, protection of desirable
native species, and introduction of improved or new forage species; on water
development and control of herds either through fencing or sheep herding;
and on appropriate stocking for maximizing utilization of available resources.
Animal Nutrition
20.       Controlled experiments, under laboratory conditions, would include
analysis to determine feed value of forages, both native and introduced
species; animal growth rates under various levels of nutrition; and the effect
of supplemental feeding during critical periods in the animal reproduction
cycle. Work on animal nutrition would include all types of livestock in the
grazing cycle, including beef cattle, and would identify the causes of nutri-
tional deficiencies in the particular animals in the farming system to be
adopted in the specific agro-ecological area.



ANNEX 11
Page 7
Farming Systems and Herd Management Economics
21.       The economic margin on sheep and goat production is currently
thought to be very low but studies would be required, in the first instance,
to establish more clearly the economics of the industry for the wide range
of land holdings and tenure systems involved. Once these have been estab-
lished, the economist would work closely with the farming systems and range
management specialists to determine the farming and herd management system
necessary to maximize returns at various levels of inputs and under varying
market conditions.
Research Facilities
22.       Two thousand acres of land would be made available at Sobral for
the program. In addition, 30 ha of experimental fields would be available
at Teresina and a selected area of 25 ha at the Petrolina/Juazeiro (ARC)
station. In addition, it is proposed to acquire land at Uaua, to be used
for a substation to be operated from the ARC location. In order to carry
out animal breeding, grazing management and farming systems research it
would be necessary to provide at least 1,200 head of sheep, a similar number
of goats, and at least 100 beef cattle for integrated systems of livestock
production where the forage species available are suited to this type of
grazing management.
23.       Adequate laboratories, particularly for soil and forage analysis,
would be available at the Petrolina/Juazeiro station. Facilities for selec-
tion and breeding would be situated at Sobral. Laboratories for disease and
pest control would also be placed at that station. All three stations,
Sobral, Petrolina/Juaziero, and Teresina, would develop range management
and farming system techniques for the conditions of their particular area.
Suitable field laboratory facilities would be provided.
C. Organization and Management
24.       The national sheep and goat research program team would be head-
quarterCd at Sobral, Ccara. The station director would have overall re-
sponsibility for administration of the headquarters station and would repre-
sent EMBRAPA for community relations at the center. He would be assisted
by an advisory council 1/ and two associate directors, one for administra-
tive services, usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer,
and one for the implementation of the research program.
25.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
example of membership).



ANNEX 11
Page 8
would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation. Coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs)
and other cooperating institutions concerned with research on sheep and
goats and the application of new technology in sheep and goat husbandry
would be achieved through contacts between the team leader and representa-
tives of the appropriate research programs at these stations. An annual
workshop would be held at which current activities would be evaluated and
plans for the research program for the coming years would be made. Inputs
from producers would be obtained at such workshops or field days in the
distinct sub-areas of major production. As a member of the advisory council,
the associate director for research would provide the necessary liaison
between the council and producers (see also Annex 17).
Research Station Network
26.       This program is somewhat similar to that for rubber in that it is
being developed from few formal beginnings and it is expected to take a few
years before a viable research station network is developed. However, it is
expected that three stations in the Northeast would initiate the program
and that they would eventually build up significant collaborative programs
with several institutes in the South, particularly in relation to sheep
breeding. The present limited status of the research network may be noted
in Table 4.
University Contract Research
27.       When research problems of a specific nature occur, especially those
which may require long-range basic research, it may be desirable to contract
such problem-solving research to select universities or institutes in the
region which have a demonstrated interest and competence in the area involved.
Another instance in which contract research might be indicated is when special
facilities or equipment are required that are not provided for at one of the
centers working with these species. Such a project might, for instance, in-
clude experimental vaccine production, meats technology or work with animals
which must be kept under quarantine.
D. Staffing
28.       Currently, less than 10% of the required staff is available for the
research program. At full development, the National Commodity Research Center
would have 68 professional staff and 31 supporting staff. In addition, at the
UEPAE mentioned (Teresina) and at the Agricultural Research Center for the
Northeast, there would be a total of 32 professional staff and 16 support staff
designated for sheep/goat research. An active staff selection and training
program would be necessary to build up staff to full development during the
five-year period, and a training officer has been provided for this program
(see also Table 5 for details).



ANiNEX 11
Page 9
E. Training and Fellowships Program
29.       The project would provide fellowships in order to upgrade junior
staff and to provide specialists in the disciplines and areas of study re-
quired to carry out the research program. Fellowships would include 34
candidates for the M.Sc. degree, and six for the Ph.D. degree. In addition,
funds would be made available for the participation of 10 staff members in
short courses during the five-year period of project execution. The training
officer would be responsible to the program leader/coordinator for making
recommendations for these staf development courses (see Table 2).
F. Consultants
30.       In order to help provide the necessary leadership for research proj--
ect execution and planning during the early stages of development, the project
would provide for 5.0 man-years of long-term consultancy and 4.5 man-years of
short-term consultants. These would be selected, as required, in the specific
areas of livestock management, animal breeding and veterinary science (see
Table 2).
G. Linkages to International Centers
31.       In the past, most of the arid zone research involving sheep and
goats has been done in Australia, the United States and the Union of South
Africa. A number of U.S. land grant colleges are involved in rangeland
management, but only the state of Texas has any significant goat population
and has integrated these species into range management programs. Texas A&M
University has done considerable work in this field. Work in Australia is
more recent, and that on rangeland for sheep has been well advanced by the
Commonwealth.
32.       The Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organization
(GSIRO) in Australia and the International Center Research Institute for the
Semi-arid Tropics (ICRISAT) at Hyderabad, India have not worked specifically
with goats, but the techniques developed there for the management of range
pasture in areas similar to the Northeast and for plant/water relationships
in general would seem to indicate that linkages to a few of the above-
mentioned universities and institutions would provide a valuable interchange
of research methods and concepts.



ANNEX 11
Page 10

five-year period would be approximately US$13.6 million. The estimated costs

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
RESEARCH PROGRAMS FOR FARMING SYSTEMS IN THE SEMI-ARID TROPICS
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Agricultural Importance of the Region
1.        The project area, Northeast Brazil, covers 1.5 million km  and rep-
resents 18.2% of the national territory; it is comprised of nine states, and
one federal territory, and in 1970 had a population of 28.7 million inhabi-
tants, or 30.3% of the total population of Brazil. Agriculturally, the area
is very diverse, but can conveniently be divided into six major subregions
which influence agricultural activity in the area. In 1970, agriculture
accounted for 33.4% of the regional NDP and amounted to US$1,341 million at
factor costs. These amounts compare with a national NDP of US$35,981 million,
of which 14.6% (US$5,443 million) was derived from agriculture. 1/
Physical Aspects
2.        The six subregions are defined as follows:
(a)  The Middle-North comprising the2state of Maranhao and northern
Piaui. This area of 382,000 km represents 25% of the Northeast.
Well traversed with several large rivers, it is considered the
transition zone between the Amazon basin and the Northeast.
Average temperature of the region is 26 C and annual rainfall
is in excess of 1,800 mm in the west, lessening gradually to
1,400-1,600 mm in the east. The rainfall distribution gives
rise to three types of vegetation: tropical rainforest,
"tagreste" (transitional), and "caatinga" (brush). Soils of
this subregion vary between latosols, podzols, mono-calcic
brunizems, grumosols and alluvials. Soil classification by
the Natural Resource Survey (RADAM), a project of the Ministry
of Mines and Energy, produced the following estimated distribu-
tion of land for agricultural use: suitable, 16%; restricted
use, 31%; and unsuitable, 53%.
1/   Source:  "Sistemas de Contas Nacionais:  Novas Estimativas",
Getulio Vargas Foundation, September 1974.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
(b) The Littoral or "Zona da Mata" represented by the narrow coastal
strip extending from the state of Rio Grande do Norte tX the
south of the state of Bahia. The area is of 126,968 km and rep-
resents 8.2% of the total area.  Temperatures average 25 to 26 C,
and rainfall is in the range of 1,500 to 2,000 mm per annum, with
a pronounced four-month dry season. The area was once densely
forested, hence its name, but it has been extensively cultivated
and only forest fragments remain. Soils of the region are vari-
able, but may be classified into four main types: thin alluvials
of the coastal plain; deep humid and fertile soils, high in
organic matter, which lie inward from the coast; flatland soils,
which are tertiary formations from the crystalline bedrock of
the center Northeast (these soils are usually heavily layered
mixtures of sand and clays and are very deep); and, finally, the
older formations in areas characterized by hilly relief with steep
slopes and soils that are predominantly latosols and podzols.
(c)  Small fertile valleys that occur further inland in the hills.
Although widely scattered, the area covers 34,600 km , or 2.2%
of the total land area. The zone is somewhat transitional be-
tween agreste and semi-arid lands, but climate is suitable for
both annual and perennial crops. The rainfall is well distri-
buted, and the soils are those of group (b) but higher in
organic material and with adequate moisture.
(d)  The plains, encompassing western Bahia state and part of
southern Piaui, an area of 94,508 km , which represents 6.1% of
the Northeast region. These plains, at 400 to 600 meters eleva-
tion, are sparsely populated, and rainfall is frequently insuffi-
cient to produce a crop.  Annual average temperature is 25 0C and
there is a marked four-month dry season. The soils, predominantly
acid latosol and podzols, are of low fertility and are highly
permeable so that the rainfall drains quickly in the undulating
topography of the region.
(e)  The agreste or major zone of transition between the "mata" and semi-
arid "sertao", one of the major subregions o agricultural produc-
tion in the Northeast. It covers 169,698 km representing 11%
of the area. Seven of the states in the region have some portion
of agreste, but Bahia, Pernambuco and Piaui contain 96% of this
agro-ecological area. The rainfall pattern is similar to that
of the lush coastal mata but with precipitation of only 600 to
1,000 mm.  Average temperatures of 21 C are lower than the coast.



ANNEX 12
Page 3
Topography is rolling, with elevations seldom above 600 m until
the foothills in Rio Grande do Norte and Pernambuco are reached.
Here, again, soils are predominantly latosols and podzols, with
some monocalcium brunizems in the drier areas. The area is
the most important subregion as a producer of basic food crops
for the large urban centers of the Northeast.
(f) The semi-arid region, by far the largest of the six subregions,
covering 741,000 kmL, or 48% of the land area. Although agri-
culturally poor, it supports an almost equal portion of the
regional population (46%) and is important in the agricultural
economy. It is here that periodic droughts have created, over
the years, severe socio-economic problems. Rainfall is erratic
but can range between 500 to 750 mm per annum. Of more importance
from the agricultural viewpoint is rainfall distribution. Nor-
mally, rainfall occurs in a single three to five months period,
followed by seven to nine months of grolonged drought.  Temperatures
are high, averaging between 23 to 27 C, relative humidity is low
(50%), and annual evaporation exceeds 2,000 mm. The region thus
fits the criteria for semi-arid agriculture. In general, the soils
are thin and of low fertility. By no means homogenous, a few soil
types, such as the alluvials, vertisols and latosols have agri-
cultural potential.  Soil permeability, rather than soil fprtility,
is of much more importance in the crystalline soils of the drought
polygon. Agriculture in this region is confined to the fertile
alluvial plains ("varzeas"), along stream courses. In addition, an
estimated 50,000 ha are now irrigated along the Sao Francisco river
basin.
Population Density
3.        The northeastern states having the larger populations are Bahia,
Pernambuco, Ceara and Maranhao, but tVe greatest population densyties occur
in the states of Alagoas (57.9 cap/km )2 Pernambuco (53.4 cap/km ), Piaui
(43.4 cap/km), and Sergipe (41.1 cap/km ).
The following table provides population data on the nine states
of the Northeast Region in 1972:

Agricultural Production
4.        The principal agricultural field crops of the Northeast in 1973 were,
in order of economic importance, cotton, sugarcane, cassava, cacao, beans,
bananas, maize, rice, coconuts and castor beans. It will be noted that,
of these 10 crops, eight are food crops, one a fibre crop, and the other indus-
trial. Of the eight food crops, six are considered basic to the diet of the
Northeast. Of the crops mentioned, research on five would be supported under
the commodity research projects proposed for the Northeast. In addition to
mixed crop livestock farms, enterprises in the area include beef cattle,
dairy cattle, sheep, goats and swine. All but the latter would be supported
under the commodity research programs of the project.
B. Current Status of Research
5.        Under the Department for National Agriculture Research (DNPEA), the
research network was operated by the Instituto de Pesquisas Agropecuarias do
Nordeste (IPEANE). There were 10 experiment stations allocated on a state
basis, but research on individual commodities was not coordinated within an
experiment station network. Research programs were designed on a disciplinary,
rather than a commodity, basis and it was difficult for the farmer to utilize
research results at the farm level.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
Achievements and Constraints
6.        Achievements, as measured by significant increases in agriculture
that can be directly related to research inputs, are almost exclusively lim-
ited to the favorable results obtained by two of the major monocultures in
southern Bahia state, namely sugarcane and cacao. The main reasons for this
are: (a) the particularly favorable conditions to be found in the Zona de
Mata area for these crops, the strong supportive services, such as credit,
markets and technical services available to a rather homogenous group of
large-scale farmers; and (c) the existence of experiment stations devoted ex-
clusively to these crops, operated by the Commission for Recuperation of
Cacao Culture in Rural Areas (CEPLAC) for cacao and the Institute for Alcohol
and Sugarcane Research (IIAA) for sugarcane. The latter, incidentally, pro-
vides further proof that the commodity-oriented approach to agricultural re-
search is effective in the Brazilian context.
7.        The constraints experienced in agricultural production in the area
are many, but a few of the more important ones may be listed here: (a) limita-
tions due to climate, soil fertility and topography; (b) population density
in areas of limited agricultural potential where both food and employment
opportunities cannot be supplied for the rural population; (c) the limited
expansion opportunities due to uncertainties in world demand and supply for
one of the major agricultural crops of the region, i.e., cotton; (d) the low
level of inputs, other than labor, i.e., mechanization, fertilizers, credit
and technology; and (e) generally insufficient agricultural extension and
credit services.
Potentials for Future Development of the Area
8.        The Northeast has figured prominently in special development pro-
grams, but fragmentation of efforts, weak institutional support and poorly
defined objectives have resulted in few successes (see "Rural Development
Designs and Options in Northeast Brazil", Report No. 665A-BR of June 23,
1975). Several promising programs, however, to which the Government hopes
to devote substantial resources to effectively integrate development efforts
in this area are: (a) the Development Program for Integrated Areas of the
Northeast (POLONORDESTE); (b) Development of Irrigation Programs (DNOCs);
(c) Livestock Development Program (CONDEPE); and (d) Agricultural Development
Programs.



ANNEX 12
Page 6
II. The Agricultural Research Program for the
Semi-arid Tropics (Northeast)
A. General Description
9.        The broad objectives of the program would be to improve the economy
of the area by strengthening the agricultural research potential and, by means
of the new technology developed, increase the area's agricultural output in
keeping with the needs of the population. The program would have three main
thrusts: (a) the development of farming systems technology to provide maximum
production from the limited natural resources available; (b) the modification
of certain resources, particularly water, by means of storage, flow control
and controlled irrigation in areas adaptable to such procedures; and (c) the
selection of either native or exotic plant and animal species of agricultural
value which can best utilize the limited resources of the region.
Program Components
10.       In order to carry out the research program as planned, the following
components would be provided under the project:
(a) civil works at the research station of Petrolina/Juazeiro,
to provide the necessary laboratory and research facilities;
(b) research farm development;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at the station
mentioned;
(d) library documentation and information services;
(e) technical services, including consultants and fellowships
programs;
(f) land purchase; and
(g) establishment costs during the five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables 1, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
11.       Priorities in research will vary from area to area and can only be
determined through an adequate survey of factors limiting production. In
general, however, emphasis will be on activities described below.



ANNEX 12
Page 7
Farming Systems Research for Semi-arid Areas
12.       The short-term goal of farming systems research for the semi-arid
lands which can support limited but rainfed agriculture would be to study
the potentials of current farming methods in relation to: land use, crops
and cropping systems, crop rotations, length of growing season, response to
fertilizers, crop harvesting, and storage systems. On the basis of existing
knowledge, the farming systems team would then investigate the constraints to
increased production and identify them as to cause, i.e., crop varieties,
cultivation methods, depth of rooting, fertilizer response and optimum growing
period. During this stage, the research teams would require close collabora-
tion with the National Commodity Centers in the area to determine optimum
adaptation of crop varieties and the potential of both native and exotic
range species as forage for livestock.
13.       The long-range program would commence with an in-depth study of the
natural resources available to determine how these may be best adapted to
provide a wider range of agricultural pursuits and crop rotation through a
rational use of these available resources. Such studies comprise:
(a) control of available moisture by the use of ridges, furrows,
and bunds;
(b) depth of seeding;
(c) control of water runoff during the short rainy season, plus
storage and recycling of runoff;
(d) control of wind and water erosion;
(e) determination of water storage capacity of the soil profile,
and methods by which this may be modified;
(f) evaporation rate under varying systems of cropping and clean
cultivation;
(g) moisture conservation effects of crop rotation and fallowing;
(h) evaluation of native herbage during the dry period;
(i) water balance relationships in connection with crop or live-
stock utilization during different periods.
The above studies would be closely linked to general hydro-ecological studies
of each watershed and the micro-region influenced by it.



ANNEX 12
Page 8
Studies of Irrigated Agriculture for Arid Zones
14.       The program would study the efficient utilization of the hydrologic
resources of the area and particularly those which can be successfully deployed
for agricultural production. Hydrologic studies of the watershed and storage
area would be basic to planning the rational and economical use of this basic
resource. Special features to be studied are (a) water quality, (b) costs of
distribution, (c) fertility of land to be irrigated, and (d) value of crops
to be grown.
15.       In an area such as the Northeast, where cropping is extremely
varied, high value crops, such as vegetables, rice, cotton, alfalfa, or
crops grown for seed under controlled (isolated) conditions, would play
an important part in each crop selection and rotation, The farming system
research team must, in each case, work closely with economists and irrigation
engineers to ensure that irrigation costs are warranted, based on crop returns
under these manmade conditions for crop production in the specific micro-
region under study. Studies of freshwater fish culture are frequently in-
cluded in such studies.
C. Organization and Management
16.       The farming systems research program team would be headquartered at
Petrolina/Juazeiro. The station director would have overall responsibility
for administration of the headquarters station and would represent EMBRAPA
for community relations at the center. He would be assisted by an advisory
council 1/ and two associate directors, one for administrative services,
usually functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and one for
the implementation of the research program.
17.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator
would have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program
and would provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting,
execution and evaluation, coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs) and
other cooperating institutions concerned with research on farming systems.
The application of new technology to production methods in the Northeast
would be achieved through contacts between the team leader and representa-
tives for the appropriate research programs at these stations. An annual
workshop would be held at which current activities would be evaluated and
plans for the research program for the coming years would be made. Input
from producers would be achieved through representation at such workshops,
or field days, in the distinct sub-areas of major production as may be
necessary. As a member of the advisory council, the associate director
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members but
composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for example
of membership).



ANNEX 12
Page 9
for research would provide the necessary liaison betwen the council and
producers (see also Annex 17).
Research Facilities and Research Station Network
18.       One hundred ha of land would be made available at the Petrolina/
Juazeiro center. In addition, the UEPAE at Alagoinha would set aside 50 ha
for farming systems research on rainfed crops being grown in the agreste and
humid littoral regions since the Petrolina/Juazeiro site is more suited for
irrigated agriculture. The UEPAEs that would receive farming systems techno-
logy for farm demonstration and adaptation purposes would be Bacabal, Teresina,
Caico, Itapetinga, Quissama, and Irece, Caruaru, Penedo, and Barreiras. There
would be no distinct network of research stations since the program is expect-
ed to be areawide in its approach and would provide outreach and technology
transfer programs for the Northeast, which it is designed to serve (see
Table 4 for details).
Collaborating Institutions
19.       A list of the institutions engaged in development programs for the
Northeast is provided in Table 4. These are designed to promote development
of the area through various investment programs. Several of these projects
concern agricultural production for specific agricultural commodities, and
would invo'lve agronomic research carried out in collaboration -with the EMBRAPA
program.
20.       The following institutions, therefore, become important to the
research program:   Superintendencia do Desenvolvimento do Nordeste (SUDENE),
the Banco do Nordeste do Brasil (BNB), the Company for Development of the
Sao Francisco Valley (CODEVASF), and the National Development for the Improve-
ment of Dry Land Agriculture (DNOCS).
University Contract Research
21.       The State University at Ceara (Fortaleza) has long been interested
in agricultural research projects in the area, and has previously made contri-
butions to livestock improvement projects. Once again, it should be stressed
that, in planning for such cooperative programs, the initiative should come
from EMBRAPA after the projects have been selected and proposed by the program
leader/coordinator and his planning committee at the Petrolina/Juazeiro station.
This will ensure that contracted research is an integral part of the research
program and does not focus on academic problems.
D. Staffing
22.       Staffing of the research program to be executed at the Agricultural
Research Center for the Semi-arid Tropics would require 113 professional staff



ANNEX 12
Page 10
and 50 administrative support staff. Part (54 professional and 18 support
staff) of this group would be available to guide the somewhat enlarged pro-
gram of farming systems for the agreste humid littoral area from the Alagoinha
UEPAE Station, as indicated by the staffing list (Table 5). In addition,
staff at the UEPAEs mentioned in paragraph 18 would participate in the
on-farm demonstration of farming systems practices.
E. Training and Fellowship Program
23.       The project would provide for the training of 35 professionals in
M.Sc. programs and seven in Ph.D. degree programs. In addition, funding
would be provided for 15 staff participants for in-service, short-term
training courses, mainly in the areas of farming systems technology for
arid and semi-arid agriculture, but including range management and irriga-
tion techniques, as may be required (Table 2).
F. Consultants
24.       It is recognized that, if properly administered and staffed, the
agricultural research program for the semi-arid tropics could well have a
most significant impact on the economy of the Northeast, particularly in
the short term since much of the technology to be adapted has been devel-
oped in similar regions, for example, in India and Mexico. The program
cannot be successful in this crucial early stage, however, unless there is
an immediate infusion of trained professional staff with experience in the
specific areas of research required. These areas are dry land farming
systems, minimum tillage systems, soil conservation, ecology, mechanization
and irrigation. To meet this need during the initial two or three years of
program execution, the project would provide for nine man-years of long-term,
and 6.5 man-years of short-term consultancy services. Of these, one consult-
ant would be attached to the Agricultural Research Center at Petrolina/Juazeiro
where he would assist the associate director in research program planning
(Table 2).
G. Linkages to International Centers
25.       The International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-arid Tropics
(ICRISAT) is ideally suited to provide a wide range of existing technology,
not only on farming systems, but on several major grain crops of the region,
i.e., sorghum and millets, and some of the pulses such as pigeon pea and chick-
pea, which have been developed for cultivation in low rainfall areas. The



ANNEX 12
Page 11
International Center for Maize and Wheat Improvement (CIMMYT) has also devel-
oped maize and sorghum, with adaptation to similar semi-arid zones in Mexico,
and the CIMMYT outreach program is already active in Brazil. The Institute
for Tropical Agriculture (CIAT) is also in a position to assist with cassava
and bean programs within the farming systems concepts for this area, and
farming systems research for the small farmer has been developed at CIAT,
Colombia during the past several years. Cooperative programs with either
of these three International Institutes would, therefore, offer excellent
opportunities for technology transfer at the research center and UEPAE
levels where it could then be adapted and adopted to the needs of the
Northeast region (see also Annex 17).

arid Zone (Northeast) for the five-year period would be approximately
US$15.3 million. The estimated costs are summarized in Table 1.

BRAZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR FARMING SYSTEMS IN THE
CAMPOS CERRADOS (CENTRAL-WEST)
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Agricultural Importance of the Region
1.        The Central-West represents 22.1% of Brazil's total land area and has
a population of 5.1 million, or 5.5%, of the national total. The area comprises
two states (Mato Grosso and Goias) and the Federal District. Agriculture ac-
counts for approximately 45% of income, but total income is low, forming only
3.3% of national income (1968).
Physical Aspects
2.        Much of the region has never been effectively settled and, as a
result, many of its subregions are not clearly defined. The northern areas
phase into the southernmost Amazon forest area, but the remainder of the
Central-West consists of upland plains or savannas and the swampy lowlands
to the south of Mato Grosso State.
Climate
3.        The climate of the entire region is basically tropical, with annual
average temperatures ranging between 20 C to 26 C. Maximum temperatures range
between 300C and 320C and minimum temperatures between 24 C to 26 C in the
North, but as low as 60C in the extreme South. Rainfall, which occurs dur-
ing summer and autumn, varies between 1,100 to 2,000 mm per annum, and is fol-
lowed by a winter dry season of four to six months. The dry season is more
pronounced in the state of Goias to the Northeast and less so to the South.
Soils
4.        The rather unique properties of the Campos Cerrados soils are the
major limiting factors to successful agricultural exploitation of the area.
There are four major regions, based upon soil classification. The first of
these is the plains area with low scrub and sparse grass land. The topog-
raphy is regular, with only slight undulations. Soils are heavily deficient
in calcium and phosphorous and fertility is low. Occasionally the vegetation



ANNEX 13
Page 2
increases in isolated dense patches, indicating higher fertility, and the
color of the latosols shows a higher organic matter content. These areas
generally occur in southern Goias and in central Mato Grosso.
5.        The second homogenous soil type is that of the Mato Grosso lowlands.
These are areas of poor drainage forming part of the drainage basin of the
Parana and Paraguay rivers. The soils are generally somewhat more fertile
than those in Group 1 and respond well to pasture and rice cultivation.
6.        The third soil type is found in northern Mato Grosso.  It occurs
on the high plateaus which are divided by several mountain chains, themselves
forming high plateaus and uplands. Little is known about the agriculture
potential of these soils, but it is thought to be generally poor.
7.        The fourth soil type is in the central depression of Brazil.  The
concretionary soils of its higher tableland occasionally provide arable strata
which can be classified as yellow-red podzols. Little is known of the agri-
cultural potential of the area.
8.        In general, agriculture has been found to be successful in small
micro-regions, frequently along river banks, principally in southern Goias and
in southern and western Mato Grosso, but much still remains to be learned about
the agricultural potential of the remaining 70% to 80% of the lowlands and
upland plains.
Principal Agricultural Regions
9.        Agriculturally, and based on crop potential, the Central-West is
divided into three principal subregions as follows:
(a) Southern Goias. 2-iain crops produced here are cotton, beans,
maize and cassava, rice,sugarcane, fruits and vegetable oil
crops. The predominance of basic food crops reflects the
need to supply six major urban centers in this area.
(b)  Southern Nlato Grosso.  This is the most developed part of
the state. Several important agricultural areas are to be
found, based on several areas of red, well structured latosols.
Principal crops grown on these soils are rice, maize, beans
and pasture for beef cattle and, more recently, soybeans
and wheat. The largest urban market for the region is
Campo Grande.
(c)  South and West Lowlands.  This is an area of 100,000 km2,
poorly defined in vast subareas and frequently referred to as
the "pantanal" or swamp. Native "pantanal" pasture grasses
of several species are plentiful in much of this area through-
out the year, and beef cattle production is the most important
agricultural activity.



ANNEX 13
Page 3
Population Density
10.       As indicated in the following table, the population density is rela-
tively low, about 2.7 inhabitants per km2.  The Federal District of Brasilia
cannot, of course, be considered typical of the area, but the food demands of
this large urban center play a most important role in the agriculture of the
region.
Area                        Population Density
State or Federative Unit            (kn2)    Population              (km2)
Mato Grosso                   1,231,549        1,623,618                1.32
Goias                           642,092       2,997,570                4.67
Brasilia, D.F.                    5,814          546,015              93.91
Total for Central-West        1,879,455       5,167,203                2.75
Source:   Anuario Estatistico do Brasil, IBGE, 1973.
Agricultural Production
11.       Livestock (beef cattle) production is one of the most important agri-
cultural activities of the area. However, pig and poultry raising is also
significantly close to the urban centers. The region as a whole has 22% of the
national beef herd.
12.       The major crops cultivated are rice, cassava, maize, beans and cotton.
The state of Goias produced 68.4% of these crops on a regional basis and Mato
Grosso, 31.4%. Only maize, cassava and beans are produced in any significant
quantity in the Federal District. Compared to the agriculture activity of the
North (Amazonia), extractive agriculture plays only a limited role in the
Central-West. Among plant extracts harvested from the southern tip of
Amazonia, however, are rubber, ipecac, "mate" and some hardwoods. Extractive
activities reach commercial proportions in both the states of Goias and
Mato Grosso.
B. Current Status of Research
13.       Under the Department for National Agricultural Research (DNPEA)
system, research for the Central-West was organized under the Institute of
Agricultural Research and Experimentation of the Central-West (IPEACO), with



ANNEX 13
Page 4
headquarters at Sete Lagoas and having jurisdiction over the states of Minas
Gerais, Goias and Mato Grosso and the Federal District. Research activity
under this system tended to be concentrated on crop research at Sete Lagoas.
However, under the former Institute of Animal Husbandry, at the Uberaba
Experimental Farm, a limited amount of livestock research was also carried out.
14.       From purely climatic considerations, large areas of the Central-West
present suitable growth conditions for a fairly wide range of crops. However,
the soils of the region are largely of low to medium fertility and, in general,
present a formidable challenge to research to alter their chemical composition
sufficiently to make them adaptable for economic crop production. Major con-
straints are acidity, lack of one or more of the major elements required for
plant nutrition, basically nitrogen and phosphorus, and high levels of undesir-
able elements, particularly aluminum, which create a serious imbalance and
unfavorable chemical reactions within the soil, thus impeding normal plant
nutrition. Several of these problems have proved difficult to identify
accurately, and even more costly to rectify, on a commercial basis.
15.       In addition to problems of soil fertility are the problems concerned
with more rational use of the huge grass-covered savannas of southern Mato
Grosso. Incentives, such as credit, subsidized use of fertilizer and other
soil additives, such as lime, may be necessary before any significant advances
can be made in bringing these vast regions into agricultural production.
Land tenure must also be considered as having a decisive role in crop produc-
tion since 9% of holdings are in excess of 1,000 ha and cover 74% of the land
area currently under some type of intensive or extensive exploitation.
II. THE RESEARCH PROGRAM FOR THE DEVELOPMENT OF
FARMING SYSTEMS IN THE REGION
A. General Description
16.       The basic philosophy of the research program would be to determine
the methodology whereby large areas of this region can be adapted to livestock
and crop production. In order to accomplish this, the program would have
three main thrusts, as follows:
(a) a study of existing natural resources of the area;
(b) research to overcome limiting factors currently identified
as constraints in the soil/climate/plant relationships on
which agriculture must be based; and
(c) following the results of (a) and (b) above, research to deter-
mine the most efficient systems of production for the area
and adapt such technology to the micro-regions being developed.



ANNEX 13
Page 5
17.       Since much work has already been done on the cataloging of the natu-
ral resources of the area, it is now necessary to give greater emphasis to the
utilization of this knowledge and to the development of farming system techni-
ques designed to influence production.
18.       The headquarters for this research would be at Planaltina, D.F.
Satellite or substation activities would be carried out at Goiania, Campo
Grande, Cuiaba and Sete Lagoas, as necessary. The existing EMBRAPA network of
stations within the region, i.e., national commodity centers and UEPAEs, would
all support the work on Campos Cerrados type problems and would be available
for the testing of farming systems developed at the headquarters center at
Planaltina.
Research Program Components
19.       Under the project, the following components would be provided:.
(a) civil works, additions to laboratory facilities at Planaltina;
(b) research for development at Planaltina;
(c) equipment, furniture and vehicles for use at the above station;
(d) library, documentation and information services;
(e) techhical services, including consultants and fellowships
programs; and
(f)  incremental establishment costs of the project d-,ring a
five-year period.
Details of the investments to be made are given in Tables I, 2 and 3.
B. Detailed Features
20.       To achieve objectives, the program would comprise the following:
(a) completion of soil maps;
(b)  identification and mapping of vegetation types and their
relationships to soil types;
(c) based on the above, identification of areas for specific agri-
cultural enterprises, i.e., livestock, annual crops, perennial
crops or forestry;



ANNEX 13
Page 6
(d) where soil characteristics are found to be a major constraint
to production, the determination of factors involved and methods
by which such limiting factors may be overcome; and
(e) development of farming systems based on the knowledge acquired,
crop selection and economic factors for all levels of agricul-
tural production.
The disciplines required to carry out the research program of the center would
be reflected in the staff list and include the following areas of study:
production agronomy, plant protection, plant physiology, soil science, agro-
stology, animal husbandry, agricultural economy, agroclimatology, and agricul-
tural engineering.
C. Organization and Management
21.       The station director would have overall responsibility for admin-
istration of the headquarters station and would represent EMBRAPA for commu-
nity relations at the center. He would be assisted by an advisory council 1/
and two associate directors -- one for administrative services, usually
functioning as the financial and procurement officer, and one for the imple-
mentation of the research program.
22.       The associate director for research as team leader/coordinator would
have full responsibility for all technical aspects of the program and would
provide the necessary leadership for program planning, budgeting, execution
and evaluation, coordination with satellite stations (UEPAEs) and other co-
operating institutions concerned with research on farming systems and the
application of new technology in regional agricultural production. This
would be achieved through contacts between the team leader and representa-
tives of the appropriate research programs at these stations. An annual
workshop would be held at which current activities would be evaluated and
plans for the research program for the coming years would be made. Inputs
from producers would be obtained at such workshops or field days in the
distinct subareas of major production. As a member of the advisory council,
the associate director for research would provide the necessary liaison
between the council producers (see also Annex 17).
Research Facilities
23.       An area of 1,600 ha of land is available at the Planaltina station.
Fifty percent of the required laboratory facilities are also available and
one additional laboratory building would be provided under the project.
1/   The advisory council would normally comprise seven to nine members
but composition might vary according to location (see Annex 17 for
example of membership).



ANNEX 13
Page 7
Facilities at the national commodity centers for livestock, beans and rice
in the area would also be provided for under the related commodity research
programs, as would be the equipping of the two state UEPAEs at Cuiaba and
Goiania (see Table 4).
Collaborating Institutions
24.       Because of the unique qualities of some of the soils of the Campos
Cerrados and the present limits of knowledge concerning these soils, there
is still considerable interest, both in Brazil and abroad, in pursuing this
line of research. In recent years, scientists from North Carolina State
University have been engaged in this research under a USAID contract
(AID/csd/2806) and, provided that the research problems selected follow closely
the specific aims of the center's approved research program, continued colla-
boration would be mutually beneficial in the future (see Table 4).
University Contract Research
25.       The Federal University of Brasilia, through its Zoological Garden,
has indicated an interest in collaborating with the program. Provided such
programs originate from the center at Planaltina and are carefully coordinated
to support, rather than duplicate the regional programs of research, it is
believed such contract research could be beneficial (see alsc Annex 17).
D. Staffing
26.       Staffing for the research program to be executed by the agricultural
research center at Planaltina would require 75 professional staff and 31
administrative support staff. It is proposed that, as necessary, research
teams from Planaltina would also carry out work at the commodity research
centers and UEPAEs within the project area of influence (Campos Cerrados) as
may be necessary to achieve research program goals. It is estimated that no
more than 25% of this staff is currently available, thus requiring the addition
of 75% before full development at the end of the five-year implementation
period. A training officer has been included in the staff list to assist in
staff development and training programs (see also Table 5 for staff list at
full development).
E. Training and Fellowship Program
27.       The project would provide for the training of 29 staff members in
M.Sc. degree programs, and a further eight candidates would receive training
to the Ph.D. level. In-service training courses, mainly in the area of
farming systems technology, would be provided for 15 staff members during
the period of project execution (Table 2).



ANNEX 13
Page 8
F. Consultants
28.       A research center consultant would be provided for the first three-
year period to develop effective programs from the rather complex range of
problems which typify the Campos Cerrados area, particularly the unique soil
problems of that region. In addition there would be 18 months of short-term
specialist consultant services, mainly for international cooperative projects
and program review (Table 2).
G. Relationships with International Research Centers
29.       The similarities of the soil problems to be found in the savannas
("llanos") of eastern Colombia and those of the Brazilian Campos Cerrados
have been noted on many occasions. Not all soil scientists agree as to the
extent of similarities involved, but there is certainly sufficient common
ground to warrant a close exchange of knowledge and technology in this area
by cooperative agreement with the International Center for Tropical
Agriculture (CIAT), Cali, Colombia. In addition to soil-related studies,
the range of crops to be studied under farming systems is almost identical,
i.e., rice, maize, beans, cassava and pasture production for beef cattle.
III. COST ESTIMATES
30.       The cost of the Research Program for Farming Systems in the Campos
Cerrados (Central-West) for the five-year period would be approximately
US$6.5 million. The estimated costs are summarized in Table 1.
