ANNEX 18
I'age 1
BURZIL
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH I PROJECT
Assumptions on Benefits and Justification
Introduction
1.        In assessing the returns on investments in agricultural research in
Brazil, ex-post data on the results of similar investments in other countries
provide the best basis for comparison. Studies of specific commodity re-
search programs and of national overall agricultural research programs have
been conducted by economists in several countries and they indicate high in-
ternal rates of return, ranging between 20X and 90%.  Summary information of
results on both kinds of research programs is given in Table 1 (Direct
Inference Commodity Studies) and Table 2 (Implicit Technology Generation
Function).
2.        An economic rate of return ex-ante for a project of this kind is
of doubtful value as an investment decision tool since there are so many un-
certainties involved in any kind of research process. Furthermore, the re-
sult of any internal rate of return calculation would, of course, depend
largely on the amnunt nf time that elapses between actual expendltures for
research and the time useful results are first generated within the research
system, and then from that time to the point when such results are applied
by farmers on their fields. The speed with which research findings are
applied by farmers, however, depends also on the efficiency of extension
measures and supporting agricultural policies which night either stimulate
or delay such applications. To predict accurately all these variables is
not feasible. However, research will certainly bring about changes in the
overall agricultural production function that, in turn, will provide a major
stimulus for modernization of agricultural production methods in the project
area. This modernization can then be expected to lead to subsequent changes
in other area related to the agricultural production process (e.g., credit,
marketing) and to speed up the diffusion and adoption process of newly gene-
rated technology.
3.        Also to be considered is the question of whether it is more econo-
mical for a country to create its own technology or to adopt that of another.
It is clear from past evidence that the extent to which a country can benefit
from "borrowed" research findings depends largely on the degree oc develop-
ment and sophistication of its own research establishment. Therefore, there
is no substitute for indigenous agricultural research in Brazil.



ANNEX 18
Page 2
Agricultural Research in Brazil: Past Performance and Potential
4.        Thus far, Brazil has paid little attention to agricultural research
and, as a result, it compares unfavorably with other Latin American countries
as well as with countries in other parts of the world in ratio of research
expenditures to value of agricultural product and research expenditures per
farm. Comparative data compiled in Table 3 show that Brazil spent only
US$1.34 on research per farm in 1965 whereas El Salvador, for example, spent
twice as much and developed countries with strong agricultural bases such as
Australia, Canada, New Zealand, and the U.S.A. spent more than 70 times as
much as Brazil on research per farm. Besides this, the resources that were
invested in research in Brazil have been concentrated heavily in the South
and Southeast, leaving other regions virtually without a research infra-
structure. Additionally, research efforts have not been coordinated or
integrated with the extension system (Annex 2).
5.        There is much room for improvement in yields in the project area,
as can be proved by comparing yields of various regions within Brazil, as
well as with average yields in other countries (Annex 1, Table 4 and 5).
Of the selected commodities, productivity per unit in the Northeast is
between 20% (cassava) and 60% (maize) lower than it is in the South, and
between 19% (milk) and 57% (maize) lower than it is in all of South America
(Table 4). Furthermore, the large quantities of land and labor involved in
Brazilian agriculture, specitically in the project area, indicate the
enormous economic gains that could be realized from even small factor pro-
ductivity increases (Annex 1, Table 4). Except in the South and South-
east of Brazil and in connection with the specific cash crops grown there
(coffee, soybeans, annual cotton) largely for export, increases in agri-
cultural production are almost always a result of increases in cultivated
area, with yields remaining stagnant. The growth rates in yields of food
crops, from 1967 to 1972, show either no increase (rice, beans and wheat)
or a minimal increase (manioc and corn). The table below gives figures for
the period with production in 1,000 metric tons, area harvested in 1,000 ha
and yield in kilograms per hectare, unless otherwise stated:

Selected Cost Indicators for the Project
6.        The staffing patterns for the various stations and programs are
designed to assure that the critical minimum number of researchers would work
on the inter-disciplinary teams resolving specific problems. All other items
of the project are derived from this basic assessment of the number of scien-
tists needed. Selected project cost indicators are as follows:
(a) average incremental project research expenditures per
year and per farm in the project area: US$10.61 1/; and
(b) incremental project research expenditures per year and
per ha for the different crops and per animal for livestock
products.

ANNEX 18
Page 5
The figure of US$10.61 as the incremental research expenditure per farm and
year during 1976-80 indicates that, once the project is implemented, Brazil
will spend about four times as much a year on research as it did in 1965;
but this will still be 10 times less than countries like Canada, New Zealand,
USA or Australia spent in 1965. The data on research expenditures per crop/
livestock unit, taken together with the present yield levels, also lead to
the conclusion that the prospects for a high rate of return for this project
are good, especially taking into account the fact that, once research results
have been generated in the project area, they may be transferred to other parts
of the country at relatively little cost.
The Selected Commodities and Income Situation of Farmers in the Project Area
7.        Research on the commodities included in the project is necessary
and justified for several reasons. Besides the poor state of applied tech-
nology in all cases, which is reflected in the low yields per hectare in the
case of the crops and in other measures of efficiency in the case of live-
stock, production at present is barely, if at all, keeping up with increases
in internal demand (Annexes 3 to 11). This situation will not change with-
out significant research efforts. The constraints on productivity increase
are known. Private research on project commodities is non-existent and un-
likely to occur in the normal course of events since the crops and livestock
products in question are mainly for the local market and grown by large
numbers of farmers.  This means that individual advantages from private
investments in research would be difficult to realize. Public sector
support is therefore clearly called for.
8.        At present, all of the commodities except rubber and, to some
degree, beef cattle and dairy cattle, are significant sources of income
for small farmers. Beans, cassava, rice, maize, goats and sheep provide
most of the resources of many small farmers, as well as the staple diet
of the poorest segment of the population. By helping to increase the
productivity of these commodities, the project would have a direct effect
on the well being of the small farmers and poorest classes in the country.
9.        The socio-economic situation of most of the farmers in the project
area is precarious. Land distribution is highly skewed and this reflects
the further uneven distribution of capital, wealth and income among land-
holders (SUDENE-IBRD/DRC Survey 1973, Northeast) 1/. Data on land distribu-
tion in the project area and income levels for families in the semi-arid
Sertao (the major region of the Northeast in terms of land area and popu-
lation) based on farm size are shown below. These income data are valid
for most of the farm population covered by the project.
1/   Combined report by the Superintendency for the Development of the
Northeast (SUDENE), Bank (IBRD) and Development Research Center (DRC).

10.        It can be concluded that virtually all of the 2 million families on
farms of less than 50 ha - especially those 1.6 million on less than 10 ha -
live in either absolute or relative poverty, as defined by the Bank's sector
policy paper on Rural Development. Their per capita income is below US$225,
which was considered to be the relative poverty level for Brazil in 1974. The
project can be expected to lay the basis for substantial improvements in the
living standards of this segment of the population.
December 15, 1975
